The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Sources of Error in the Syllogism: Material and Formal Defects
Author defines syllogism and classifies errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three sources of error in a syllogism and distinguish between material defects (premises) and formal defects (arrangement).

Core Idea
Premises and form are two pillars; a flaw in either breaks the syllogism.

The author defines the syllogism as a composite structure of specific statements arranged in a specific order to necessarily produce a conclusion. This composition is vulnerable to error from three sources. Understanding these sources helps you evaluate any argument in Islamic disciplines, from kalam proofs to fiqh analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the syllogism
Find the three requirements: specific statements (muqaddimat), specific composition (ta'lif), and specific condition (shart) that necessitates the conclusion.
This definition is broader than categorical syllogism alone.
2
Distinguish material vs formal error
Error arises from the premises (material) if they lack conditions like certainty; from the arrangement (form) if the order is wrong even with sound premises; or from both.
3
Apply the building analogy
A house can be defective in form (crooked walls) despite good materials, or in materials despite good form. Translate: a syllogism can be formally valid but materially unsound, or materially sound but formally invalid.
The analogy is from the sensible world, as the author says.
4
Check for both types in a sample argument
Take an Islamic example: 'Every intoxicant is haram. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is haram.' Verify premises are certain (material) and the arrangement (first figure) is correct (formal).
This is a valid syllogism if both conditions hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في شروط القياس
اعلم : أنَّ القياس : عبارةٌ عن أقاويل مخصوصةٍ أُلِفَتْ تأليفاً مخصوصاً، ونُظمت نظماً مخصوصاً بشرط مخصوص يلزم منه رأي هو مطلوب الناظر
والخلل يدخل عليه :
تارةً مِنَ الأقاويل التي هي مقدّماتُ القياس ؛ إذ تكون خالية عن شروطها .
وأخرى مِنْ كيفيَّةِ الترتيب والنظم وإنْ كانَتِ المقدّمات صحيحة يقينية
ومرَّةً منهما جميعاً
ومثالُهُ مِنَ المحسوس : البيت المبني ؛ فإنَّه أمر مركَّب :
تارةً يختل بسبب في هيئة التأليف ؛ بأن تكون الحيطان معوجة ، أو السقف منخفضاً إلى موضع قريبٍ مِنَ الأرضِ ، فيكونُ فاسداً مِنْ حيث الصورة وإنْ كانَتِ الأحجار والجذوع وسائر الآلاتِ صحيحة.
وتارةً يكون البيتُ صحيحَ الصورة في تربيعه ، ووضع حيطانه
Statement on the conditions of the syllogism. Know that the syllogism is an expression of specific statements composed in a specific composition, and arranged in a specific arrangement with a specific condition from which necessarily follows a judgment sought by the investigator. Error enters into it: sometimes from the statements that are premises of the syllogism, when they are devoid of their conditions. And sometimes from the manner of arrangement and order even if the premises are true and certain. And sometimes from both together. Its example from the sensible: a built house; for it is a composite thing: sometimes it becomes faulty due to a fault in the form of composition, such that the walls are crooked, or the roof is low near the ground, so it is defective in terms of form even if the stones and beams and other tools are sound. And sometimes the house is sound in form in its squaring and placement of its walls.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism (qiyas) and explains the possible sources of error in it. The author begins by stating that the syllogism consists of specific statements (aqawil makhsusah) arranged in a specific composition and order, with a specific condition, such that a desired conclusion necessarily follows. This highlights that the syllogism is a composite structure with both material (premises) and formal (arrangement) aspects.

Error can occur in three ways: (1) in the premises themselves if they lack their required conditions (e.g., not being certain, or not being in the proper logical form); (2) in the arrangement and order of the premises even if the premises themselves are true and certain; or (3) in both premises and arrangement simultaneously.

To illustrate, the author uses a building analogy. A house is a composite thing. It may be defective in its form (hay'ah al-ta'lif) if the walls are crooked or the roof too low, even if the building materials (stones, beams) are sound. Conversely, the house may be correct in form (square walls, proper roof) but the materials themselves may be faulty. This analogy helps the reader understand that a syllogism's validity depends on both the "material" (truth and certainty of premises) and the "form" (logical figure and mood). For example, in Islamic jurisprudence, the premises 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)' and 'Nabidh (date wine) is an intoxicant' are sound, but if one arranges them in a way that does not yield the middle term, the conclusion may not follow.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): A compound of specific statements arranged in a specific order with a specific condition that necessitates a conclusion. [Note: In later works, qiyas sometimes refers more narrowly to categorical syllogism; here it is the general definition.]
المقدّمات (al-muqaddimat): The premises of the syllogism, which are statements that must fulfill certain conditions (e.g., certainty, truth).
الصورة (al-surah): The form of the syllogism, i.e., the arrangement and composition of its components.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, error in a syllogism can originate from which two aspects?
A) The premises and the conclusion
B) The premises and the arrangement
C) The middle term and the major term
D) The mood and the figure

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is sound only when both its premises are true/certain and its form (arrangement) is valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following qiyas: 'All temporally originated things have a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore, the world has a cause.' Identify the material and formal aspects and explain whether any error is present based on this lesson.
?
Given a defective qiyas where the major premise is not certain (e.g., 'Most scholars hold that usury is forbidden'), explain which source of error this represents and why.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Definition and Structure of Qiyas
Definition and analogy for qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components of a valid syllogism (qiyas) as defined by the author: specific premises, specific arrangement, and the condition of necessity.

Core Idea
A syllogism is a composite that demands both sound materials and correct arrangement.

The author begins by defining the syllogism (qiyas) in precise terms: it is a composition of specific statements arranged in a specific way under a specific condition, from which a necessary conclusion follows. This definition lays the foundation for all logical reasoning in the Islamic tradition. Let's unpack it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic text. The author defines 'al-qiyas' (القياس). What is the first word of the definition?
It is 'عبارة عن أقاويل مخصوصة'.
2
Extract the five components
The definition has five components: (1) statements (أقاويل), (2) specific (مخصوصة), (3) composed in a specific composition (أُلِفَت تأليفاً مخصوصاً), (4) arranged in a specific arrangement (نُظمت نظماً مخصوصاً), (5) under a specific condition (بشرط مخصوص). List them.
The condition ensures necessity.
3
Understand the condition
The phrase 'بشرط مخصوص' refers to the conditions of validity that make the conclusion follow necessarily. What does the author imply if the syllogism lacks this condition?
Without the condition, the conclusion may not follow.
4
Connect to the house analogy
The author later compares a syllogism to a house: both are composites. In a house, materials and form must both be sound. How does this analogy illustrate the components of a syllogism?
The materials are the premises, the form is the arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول في شروط القياس
اعلم : أنَّ القياس : عبارةٌ عن أقاويل مخصوصةٍ أُلِفَتْ تأليفاً مخصوصاً، ونُظمت نظماً مخصوصاً بشرط مخصوص يلزم منه رأي هو مطلوب الناظر
والخلل يدخل عليه :
منشأ الغلط في القياس
تارةً مِنَ الأقاويل التي هي مقدّماتُ القياس ؛ إذ تكون خالية عن شروطها .
وأخرى مِنْ كيفيَّةِ الترتيب والنظم وإنْ كانَتِ المقدّمات صحيحة يقينية
ومرَّةً منهما جميعاً
ومثالُهُ مِنَ المحسوس : البيت المبني ؛ فإنَّه أمر مركَّب :
تارةً يختل بسبب في هيئة التأليف ؛ بأن تكون الحيطان معوجة ، أو السقف منخفضاً إلى موضع قريبٍ مِنَ الأرضِ ، فيكونُ فاسداً مِنْ حيث الصورة وإنْ كانَتِ الأحجار والجذوع وسائر الآلاتِ صحيحة.
وتارةً يكونُ البيتُ صحيحَ الصورة في تربيعه ، ووضع حيطانه وسقفِهِ ، ولكن يكون الاختلالُ مِنْ رَخاوة في الجذوع ، وتشعُثِ في اللبنات
فهذا حكمُ القياس والحدّ وكلّ أمرٍ مركَّب ؛ فإِنَّ الخلل فيهِ إِمَّا أن يكونَ في هيئة تركيبه وترتيبه ، وإما أن يكون في الأصل الذي يرد عليه التركيب ؛ كالثوبِ في القميص ، والخشب في الكرسي ، واللَّبِنِ في الحائط ، والجذوع في السقف
وكما أَنَّ مَنْ يريدُ بناءَ بيتٍ بعيد عن الخلل يفتقر إلى أن يعِدَّ الآلات المفردة أولاً ؛ كالجذوع واللَّبِنِ والطين ، ثمَّ إذا أرادَ اللَّبِنَ افتقر إلى إعدادِ مفرداتِهِ ؛ وهي الماءُ والتبن والتراب ، والقالب الذي فيه يضربُ ، فيبتدئ أولاً بالأجزاء المفردة فيركبها، ثمَّ يركّب المركَّبَ ، وهكذا إلى آخرِ العمل .. فكذلك طالب القياس ينبغي أن ينظر في نظم القياس وفي صورتِهِ ، وفي الأمر الذي يضعُ الترتيب والنظم فيهِ ، وهو المقدمات .
وأقلُّ ما ينتظمُ منهُ قياس مقدمتان ـ أعني : علمين - يتطرَّقُ إليهما التصديق والتكذيب
وأقل ما تحصلُ منه مقدمةٌ ،معرفتانِ ، توضعُ إحداهما مُخبراً عنه ، والأخرى خبراً أو وصفاً
The discourse on the conditions of the syllogism. Know that the syllogism is an expression of specific sayings composed in a specific composition, and arranged in a specific arrangement under a specific condition, from which necessarily follows an opinion that is the aim of the investigator. Error enters into it: The origin of error in the syllogism is sometimes from the sayings that are the premises of the syllogism, when they are devoid of their conditions; and sometimes from the manner of arrangement and order even if the premises are sound and certain; and occasionally from both together. Its example from the sensible is the built house, for it is a composite thing: sometimes it is defective due to a defect in the form of composition, such as the walls being crooked or the ceiling being low, close to the ground, so it is defective in terms of form even if the stones, beams, and all other tools are sound. And sometimes the house is sound in form in its squaring, the placement of its walls and ceiling, but the defect is from weakness in the beams and splitting in the bricks. So this is the judgment of the syllogism, the definition, and every composite matter; for the defect in it is either in the form of its composition and arrangement, or in the material upon which the composition is applied, like the cloth in the shirt, the wood in the chair, the brick in the wall, and the beams in the ceiling. And just as one who wants to build a house free from defect needs to prepare the individual tools first, such as beams, bricks, and clay; then when he wants the bricks, he needs to prepare their constituents: water, straw, and earth, and the mold in which it is struck. He starts with the individual parts, combines them, then combines the composite, and so on until the end of the work. Similarly, the student of the syllogism should consider the arrangement of the syllogism and its form, and the matter in which arrangement and order are placed, namely the premises. The minimum from which a syllogism is composed is two premises—that is, two items of knowledge—to which affirmation and denial pertain. And the minimum from which a premise is obtained is two cognitions, one placed as that about which something is reported, and the other as the report or description.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion on the conditions of the syllogism (qiyas) and the sources of error that can affect it. The author begins by defining qiyas: it is a composite of specific statements (aqawil makhsusa) arranged in a specific manner under a specific condition, such that a certain opinion necessarily follows—this is the sought conclusion. Then he identifies three sources of error: (1) the premises themselves lack their required conditions (e.g., not certain, not true); (2) the arrangement or order (tartib/nazm) is faulty even if the premises are sound; (3) both defects occur together. To illustrate, he uses the analogy of building a house: a house can be structurally unsound due to crooked walls or a low ceiling (form defect) even if materials are good, or it can be well-shaped but the materials are weak (matter defect). This analogy applies to all composite things: the defect is either in the form (sura) of composition or in the underlying material (asl). The author then expands: just as a builder must first prepare individual materials (beams, bricks, clay) and then combine them step by step, the logician must consider the arrangement (nazm) and form (sura) of the syllogism, and the matter (maddat) into which arrangement is placed, i.e., the premises (muqaddimat). Finally, he states the minimal components: a syllogism minimally consists of two premises (muqaddimatan), which are two 'ilm (tasdiqat, affirmations), and each premise minimally consists of two cognitions (ma'rifatan, tasawwurat), one being the subject (mukhbar 'anhu) and the other the predicate (khabar or wasf). This passage thus lays the foundation for understanding the structure and validity conditions of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
القياس
المقدّمات
الصورة
المفردات
التصديق
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three components of a valid qiyas? (Choose the best answer)
A) Premises, conclusion, and middle term
B) Specific statements, specific composition, and specific condition
C) Subject, predicate, and copula
D) Two premises and a conclusion

Key Takeaway
The soundness of a syllogism depends on both the truth of its premises and the correctness of its logical form; neither alone suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyse the following syllogism from usul al-fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (haram); every nabidh (date drink) is intoxicating; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Identify whether it meets the author's definition of qiyas by checking the premises, arrangement, and condition.
?
Using the house analogy, determine what would be the 'materials' and 'form' if this syllogism is compared to a house.

The Syllogism
intermediate
6 min
Sources of Error in Qiyas: Form vs Matter
Sources of error in syllogism with house analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between errors in the form (sura) and errors in the matter (madda) of a syllogism, and identify which type of error is present in a given argument.

Core Idea
A syllogism can fail either because its premises are unsound or because its arrangement is invalid.

After defining qiyas, the author identifies two primary sources of error: the premises themselves may be defective (lacking their conditions), or the arrangement (nazm) may be faulty. He uses the analogy of a house to clarify this distinction. Understanding these sources is crucial for constructing valid arguments and spotting fallacies in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sources
From the Arabic, list the three ways error enters a syllogism as stated by the author: (1) from the premises, (2) from the arrangement, (3) from both.
Find the phrases 'تارةً', 'وأخرى', 'ومرَّةً'.
2
Map the house analogy
The author gives an example of a house. Explain how a defect in composition (form) differs from a defect in materials (matter).
Crooked walls vs weak beams.
3
Apply to a logical argument
Consider a syllogism: 'All A are B; all B are C; therefore all A are C.' If the premises are true but the conclusion does not follow, what type of error is it? If the premises are false, what type?
Form error if invalid structure; matter error if premises false.
4
Recognize both together
The author mentions a third possibility: both form and matter are defective. Give an example of a syllogism that is both invalidly arranged and has false premises.
E.g., invalid figure with false statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اعلم : أنَّ القياس : عبارةٌ عن أقاويل مخصوصةٍ أُلِفَتْ تأليفاً مخصوصاً، ونُظمت نظماً مخصوصاً بشرط مخصوص يلزم منه رأي هو مطلوب الناظر

وأقلُّ ما ينتظمُ منهُ قياس مقدمتان ـ أعني : علمين - يتطرَّقُ إليهما التصديق والتكذيب

The discourse on the conditions of the syllogism. Know that the syllogism is an expression of specific sayings composed in a specific composition, and arranged in a specific arrangement under a specific condition, from which necessarily follows an opinion that is the aim of the investigator. Error enters into it: The origin of error in the syllogism is sometimes from the sayings that are the premises of the syllogism, when they are devoid of their conditions; and sometimes from the manner of arrangement and order even if the premises are sound and certain; and occasionally from both together. Its example from the sensible is the built house, for it is a composite thing: sometimes it is defective due to a defect in the form of composition, such as the walls being crooked or the ceiling being low, close to the ground, so it is defective in terms of form even if the stones, beams, and all other tools are sound. And sometimes the house is sound in form in its squaring, the placement of its walls and ceiling, but the defect is from weakness in the beams and splitting in the bricks. So this is the judgment of the syllogism, the definition, and every composite matter; for the defect in it is either in the form of its composition and arrangement, or in the material upon which the composition is applied, like the cloth in the shirt, the wood in the chair, the brick in the wall, and the beams in the ceiling. And just as one who wants to build a house free from defect needs to prepare the individual tools first, such as beams, bricks, and clay; then when he wants the bricks, he needs to prepare their constituents: water, straw, and earth, and the mold in which it is struck. He starts with the individual parts, combines them, then combines the composite, and so on until the end of the work. Similarly, the student of the syllogism should consider the arrangement of the syllogism and its form, and the matter in which arrangement and order are placed, namely the premises. The minimum from which a syllogism is composed is two premises—that is, two items of knowledge—to which affirmation and denial pertain. And the minimum from which a premise is obtained is two cognitions, one placed as that about which something is reported, and the other as the report or description.

Commentary

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following is NOT a source of error in a syllogism?
A) The premises are lacking their conditions
B) The arrangement of premises is faulty
C) The conclusion is not stated explicitly
D) Both premises and arrangement are defective

Key Takeaway
Always check both the truth of premises and the validity of the form when evaluating a syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The following syllogism is used in kalam: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' Identify whether the error (if any) is in form or matter, and explain why.
?
Consider a defective syllogism: 'Every scholar is knowledgeable; Zayd is a scholar; therefore Zayd is knowledgeable.' If the conclusion is false, which type of error is it? (Hint: check whether the form is valid and premises true.)

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Minimal Components: Tasdiq and Tasawwur
Minimal components of qiyas: two premises, each with two concepts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasdiq (judgment) and tasawwur (concept) as they function in a syllogism, and identify the minimal components of a premise.

Core Idea
Every premise is a composite of two concepts (subject and predicate), and every syllogism minimally requires two such judgments.

In the final part of the passage, the author breaks down the syllogism into its smallest constituents. He states that a syllogism minimally consists of two premises (muqaddimat), which are affirmations (tasdiqat), and each premise consists of two cognitions (ma'rifatan, i.e., tasawwurat): one is the subject and the other the predicate. This hierarchy shows that concepts (tasawwurat) are the atomic units of logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minimum of a syllogism
Find the sentence that says 'أقل ما ينتظم منه قياس مقدمتان'. How many premises are minimally required?
Two premises.
2
Identify the minimum of a premise
Find the sentence 'وأقل ما تحصل منه مقدمة معرفتان'. What two things make up a premise?
Two cognitions/ma'rifatan.
3
Understand 'tasdiq' and 'tasawwur'
The author says premises are 'علمين بهما التصديق والتكذيب' (two items of knowledge admitting truth/falsehood). This is tasdiq. The two cognitions are tasawwur. How do tasawwurat combine to form a tasdiq?
A tasdiq is the affirmation of a relation between two tasawwurat.
4
Apply to an example
Take the premise 'The world is temporally originated'. What are the two cognitions (subject and predicate)?
Subject: 'the world'; predicate: 'temporally originated'.

القول في شروط القياس
اعلم : أنَّ القياس : عبارةٌ عن أقاويل مخصوصةٍ أُلِفَتْ تأليفاً مخصوصاً، ونُظمت نظماً مخصوصاً بشرط مخصوص يلزم منه رأي هو مطلوب الناظر
والخلل يدخل عليه :
منشأ الغلط في القياس
تارةً مِنَ الأقاويل التي هي مقدّماتُ القياس ؛ إذ تكون خالية عن شروطها .
وأخرى مِنْ كيفيَّةِ الترتيب والنظم وإنْ كانَتِ المقدّمات صحيحة يقينية
ومرَّةً منهما جميعاً
ومثالُهُ مِنَ المحسوس : البيت المبني ؛ فإنَّه أمر مركَّب :
تارةً يختل بسبب في هيئة التأليف ؛ بأن تكون الحيطان معوجة ، أو السقف منخفضاً إلى موضع قريبٍ مِنَ الأرضِ ، فيكونُ فاسداً مِنْ حيث الصورة وإنْ كانَتِ الأحجار والجذوع وسائر الآلاتِ صحيحة.
وتارةً يكونُ البيتُ صحيحَ الصورة في تربيعه ، ووضع حيطانه وسقفِهِ ، ولكن يكون الاختلالُ مِنْ رَخاوة في الجذوع ، وتشعُثِ في اللبنات
فهذا حكمُ القياس والحدّ وكلّ أمرٍ مركَّب ؛ فإِنَّ الخلل فيهِ إِمَّا أن يكونَ في هيئة تركيبه وترتيبه ، وإما أن يكون في الأصل الذي يرد عليه التركيب ؛ كالثوبِ في القميص ، والخشب في الكرسي ، واللَّبِنِ في الحائط ، والجذوع في السقف
وكما أَنَّ مَنْ يريدُ بناءَ بيتٍ بعيد عن الخلل يفتقر إلى أن يعِدَّ الآلات المفردة أولاً ؛ كالجذوع واللَّبِنِ والطين ، ثمَّ إذا أرادَ اللَّبِنَ افتقر إلى إعدادِ مفرداتِهِ ؛ وهي الماءُ والتبن والتراب ، والقالب الذي فيه يضربُ ، فيبتدئ أولاً بالأجزاء المفردة فيركبها، ثمَّ يركّب المركَّبَ ، وهكذا إلى آخرِ العمل .. فكذلك طالب القياس ينبغي أن ينظر في نظم القياس وفي صورتِهِ ، وفي الأمر الذي يضعُ الترتيب والنظم فيهِ ، وهو المقدمات .
وأقلُّ ما ينتظمُ منهُ قياس مقدمتان ـ أعني : علمين - يتطرَّقُ إليهما التصديق والتكذيب
وأقل ما تحصلُ منه مقدمةٌ ،معرفتانِ ، توضعُ إحداهما مُخبراً عنه ، والأخرى خبراً أو وصفاً
The discourse on the conditions of the syllogism. Know that the syllogism is an expression of specific sayings composed in a specific composition, and arranged in a specific arrangement under a specific condition, from which necessarily follows an opinion that is the aim of the investigator. Error enters into it: The origin of error in the syllogism is sometimes from the sayings that are the premises of the syllogism, when they are devoid of their conditions; and sometimes from the manner of arrangement and order even if the premises are sound and certain; and occasionally from both together. Its example from the sensible is the built house, for it is a composite thing: sometimes it is defective due to a defect in the form of composition, such as the walls being crooked or the ceiling being low, close to the ground, so it is defective in terms of form even if the stones, beams, and all other tools are sound. And sometimes the house is sound in form in its squaring, the placement of its walls and ceiling, but the defect is from weakness in the beams and splitting in the bricks. So this is the judgment of the syllogism, the definition, and every composite matter; for the defect in it is either in the form of its composition and arrangement, or in the material upon which the composition is applied, like the cloth in the shirt, the wood in the chair, the brick in the wall, and the beams in the ceiling. And just as one who wants to build a house free from defect needs to prepare the individual tools first, such as beams, bricks, and clay; then when he wants the bricks, he needs to prepare their constituents: water, straw, and earth, and the mold in which it is struck. He starts with the individual parts, combines them, then combines the composite, and so on until the end of the work. Similarly, the student of the syllogism should consider the arrangement of the syllogism and its form, and the matter in which arrangement and order are placed, namely the premises. The minimum from which a syllogism is composed is two premises—that is, two items of knowledge—to which affirmation and denial pertain. And the minimum from which a premise is obtained is two cognitions, one placed as that about which something is reported, and the other as the report or description.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory expansion on the conditions of the syllogism (qiyas) and the sources of error that can affect it. The author begins by defining qiyas: it is a composite of specific statements (aqawil makhsusa) arranged in a specific manner under a specific condition, such that a certain opinion necessarily follows—this is the sought conclusion. Then he identifies three sources of error: (1) the premises themselves lack their required conditions (e.g., not certain, not true); (2) the arrangement or order (tartib/nazm) is faulty even if the premises are sound; (3) both defects occur together. To illustrate, he uses the analogy of building a house: a house can be structurally unsound due to crooked walls or a low ceiling (form defect) even if materials are good, or it can be well-shaped but the materials are weak (matter defect). This analogy applies to all composite things: the defect is either in the form (sura) of composition or in the underlying material (asl). The author then expands: just as a builder must first prepare individual materials (beams, bricks, clay) and then combine them step by step, the logician must consider the arrangement (nazm) and form (sura) of the syllogism, and the matter (maddat) into which arrangement is placed, i.e., the premises (muqaddimat). Finally, he states the minimal components: a syllogism minimally consists of two premises (muqaddimatan), which are two 'ilm (tasdiqat, affirmations), and each premise minimally consists of two cognitions (ma'rifatan, tasawwurat), one being the subject (mukhbar 'anhu) and the other the predicate (khabar or wasf). This passage thus lays the foundation for understanding the structure and validity conditions of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

التصديق
التصور
مخبر عنه
خبر
وصف

Key Takeaway
Every syllogism is built from statements, and every statement is built from concepts; understanding this hierarchy is essential for logical analysis.

The Syllogism
beginner

First Figure of Categorical Syllogism: The Pattern of Equivalence
Author explains the first figure of categorical syllogism with examples and analysis of premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first figure of a categorical syllogism and verify that the middle term is predicate in one premise and subject in the other, using Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
The first figure's secret: the middle term links premises by being predicate in one and subject in the other.

In this passage, the author introduces the first figure of the categorical syllogism, called the 'pattern of equivalence.' He stresses that for a syllogism to be valid, its premises must be arranged so that the middle term ('illa) has a specific position: it must be a predicate in one premise and a subject in the other. This structure guarantees that the conclusion follows necessarily. Understanding this figure is crucial for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam, where analogical reasoning must be reduced to explicit syllogistic form to avoid fallacious conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natija)
Look at the fiqh example: 'Every nabidh is forbidden.' This is the claim the syllogism aims to prove. Write it down as the conclusion.
2
Find the two premises
The author gives two premises: 'Every nabidh is intoxicant' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' These are the building blocks. Label them as minor and major premises.
The major premise contains the predicate of the conclusion (forbidden); the minor premise contains the subject (nabidh).
3
Locate the middle term
The middle term (al-'illa) is the term repeated in both premises. Here it is 'intoxicant' (muskir). Verify that it appears in both premises.
The author calls this the 'cause' because it connects the two premises.
4
Check the position of the middle term
In the major premise ('Every intoxicant is forbidden'), the middle term is the subject. In the minor premise ('Every nabidh is intoxicant'), it is the predicate. This is exactly the first figure arrangement.
If the middle term were in a different position, it would be a different figure (second, third, or fourth).
5
Count the distinct terms
Each premise has two parts (subject and predicate), totalling four parts. But since the middle term appears twice, only three distinct terms remain: nabidh (minor), intoxicant (middle), and forbidden (major). This is characteristic of a valid categorical syllogism.
The author emphasizes that without this reduction to three terms, the argument is not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطرف الأول
في نظم القياس
النمط الأول : نمط التعادل نظمة من ثلاثة أوجه
النظمُ الأوَّلُ : أن تكونَ العلَّةُ حكماً في إحدى المقدمتين ، محكوماً عليه في الأخرى (١) : مثل قولنا : ( كلُّ جسم مؤلّفٌ ) ، و ( كلُّ مؤلف حادث ) فيلزمُ منه : ( أنَّ كلَّ جسم حادث ) .
وقولُنا في الفقه : ( كلُّ نبيذ مسكر ) ، و( كلُّ مسكر حرام ) فيلزم منه : ( أنَّ كلَّ نبيذ حرامٌ ) .
وعادة الفقهاء في الصيغةِ أنْ يقولوا : ( النبيد مسكر ، فينبغي أن يكونَ حراماً ؛ قياساً على الخمر ) ولا ينكشف الغطاء ولا تنقطعُ المطالبة إلا بالنظم الذي ذكرناه (1)
ومثل هذه الأقيسة إذا لم يمكن ردُّها إلى هذا النظم .. لم تكن النتيجة لازمة ، ولم تنقطع المطالبة
فإذا فهمتَ صورة هذا النظمِ .. فاعلم أن في هذا القياس مقدمتين :
إحداهما : قولنا : ( كلُّ نبيذ مسكر ) .
والأخرى : قولنا : ( كل مسكر حرام ) .
وكلُّ مقدمةٍ تنقسم إلى جزأين بالضرورة ؛ مبتدأ وخبر ، وحكم التحصل النتيجة ومحكوم عليه ، فيكون مجموع أجزائها أربعة أمور (٢) ، إلا أنَّ أمراً واحداً يتكرر في المقدمتين ، فتعود إلى ثلاثة بالضرورة ؛ لأنَّها
(١) وعكسه - وهو الشكل الرابع - قد استبعده الإمام الغزالي ، ولذا لن يعرض إلا للأشكال الثلاثة فقط ، والمقصود بـ ( العلة ) : هو المعبّر عنه بالحد الأوسط .
(1) ولذلك لم يجعل المصنف هذا قياساً كما في «أساس القياس » ( ص ١٧ ) ، و المستصفى » ( ۱۱۷/۱ )
(2) أراد أجزاء صورة هذا النظم ؛ يعني : مجموع المقدمتين
The first section: On the construction of the syllogism. The first pattern: the pattern of equivalence, its organization is from three aspects. The first organization: that the cause ('illa) is a judgment in one premise and the subject of judgment in the other: like our saying: 'Every body is composed' and 'Every composed is originated' so it follows that 'Every body is originated'. And our saying in fiqh: 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' so it follows that 'Every nabidh is forbidden'. The habit of jurists in the formulation is to say: 'Nabidh is intoxicant, therefore it should be forbidden, by analogy to khamr.' But the cover is not removed and the demand is not cut off except by the organization we mentioned. And such syllogisms if they cannot be reduced to this organization, the conclusion is not necessary and the demand is not cut off. So when you understand the form of this organization, know that in this syllogism there are two premises: one is our saying 'Every nabidh is intoxicant', and the other 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. And every premise necessarily divides into two parts: subject and predicate, and the judgment yields the conclusion and the subject of judgment, so the sum of its parts is four things, except that one thing is repeated in the two premises, so it returns to three necessarily; because it... (1) Its converse - which is the fourth figure - has been excluded by Imam al-Ghazali, so the discussion is limited to the three figures only. What is meant by 'cause' ('illa) is that which is expressed by the middle term. (1) Therefore the author did not consider this a syllogism as in Asas al-Qiyas and al-Mustasfa. (2) He meant the parts of the form of this organization; i.e., the sum of the two premises.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage explains the first figure of the categorical syllogism, termed 'pattern of equivalence' (نمط التعادل). The author specifies the first organization within this pattern: the 'cause' (العلة), which is the middle term (الحد الأوسط), serves as a predicate in one premise and a subject in the other. Two examples are given: one from natural philosophy (every body is composed, every composed is originated, therefore every body is originated) and one from fiqh (every nabidh is intoxicant, every intoxicant is forbidden, therefore every nabidh is forbidden). The fiqh example reflects the common analogical reasoning of jurists, but the author insists that the logical form must be explicit to guarantee validity. Without reducing the argument to this syllogistic format, the conclusion is not necessary and the questioning continues. After presenting the form, the author analyzes the components: each premise has two parts (subject and predicate), leading to four parts total for two premises. However, because the middle term is repeated, the distinct terms are only three. The footnotes clarify that the fourth figure is omitted following al-Ghazali, and that the author's own works (Asas al-Qiyas, al-Mustasfa) consider such analogical expressions not as proper syllogisms. The final note explains that the 'four matters' refer to the parts of the two premises including the middle term.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illa)
النظم (al-nazm)
المقدمة (al-muqaddima)

The Necessity of a Common Term in Qiyas
Explains the requirement of a shared term in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the common (middle) term in a categorical syllogism and explain why it is necessary for a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Without a shared middle term, premises stay disconnected and no conclusion follows.

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental principle of syllogistic reasoning: the two premises must share a term. He illustrates this with examples from kalam and fiqh. By understanding this, you can determine whether any proposed syllogism is validly constructed.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two premises
Identify the two statements that are given as the basis for the conclusion. In the author's example: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicating is forbidden'.
The order of premises may vary; the logical structure is what matters.
2
List all terms in each premise
Each premise has a subject and a predicate. For the first premise: subject = 'nabidh', predicate = 'intoxicating'. For the second premise: subject = 'intoxicating', predicate = 'forbidden'.
Write them down to see patterns.
3
Find the term that appears in both premises
Look for a term that is present in both premise-sets. Here, 'intoxicating' appears in both: as predicate of the first and subject of the second. This is the common (middle) term.
The author calls this term the 'illa (cause).
4
Verify the conclusion includes the other two terms
The conclusion contains the two non-repeated terms: 'nabidh' (subject) and 'forbidden' (predicate). The conclusion is 'Nabidh is forbidden'.
The middle term does not appear in the conclusion.
5
Check the contrapositive
If no term were shared, as in the author's faulty example ('Nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Killing is forbidden'), the premises have no connection and no conclusion follows.
This shows why the common term is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد من اشتراك بين المقدمتين، مثال ذلك: قولنا: (كل جسم مؤلف) و(كل مؤلف حادث) فيلزم منه: (أن كل جسم حادث). وقولنا في الفقه: (كل نبيذ مسكر) و(كل مسكر حرام) فيلزم منه: (أن كل نبيذ حرام). وعادة الفقهاء في الصيغة أن يقولوا: (النبيد مسكر، فينبغي أن يكون حراماً؛ قياساً على الخمر). ولا ينكشف الغطاء ولا تنقطع المطالبة إلا بالنظم الذي ذكرناه (1). ومثل هذه الأقيسة إذا لم يمكن ردها إلى هذا النظم لم تكن النتيجة لازمة، ولم تنقطع المطالبة. فإذا فهمت صورة هذا النظم فاعلم أن في هذا القياس مقدمتين: إحداهما: قولنا: (كل نبيذ مسكر). والأخرى: قولنا: (كل مسكر حرام). وكل مقدمة تنقسم إلى جزأين بالضرورة؛ مبتدأ وخبر، وحكم وتحصل النتيجة ومحكوم عليه، فيكون مجموع أجزائها أربعة أمور (2)، إلا أن أمراً واحداً يتكرر في المقدمتين، فتعود إلى ثلاثة بالضرورة؛ لأنها لو بقيت أربعة لم تشترك المقدمتان في شيء، وبطل الازدواج بينهما، فلا تتولد النتيجة. فإنك إذا قلت: (النبيذ مسكر) ولم تتعرض في المقدمة الثانية لا للنبيذ ولا للمسكر، ولكن قلت: (والقتل حرام) أو (والعالم حادث) فلا ترتبط إحداهما بالأخرى، فبالضرورة لا بد من أن يكون أحد الأجزاء الأربعة متكرراً في المقدمتين، فيرجع إلى ثلاثة. فلنصطلح على تسمية المكرر في المقدمتين: علة، وهو الذي يمكن أن يقترن بقولك: (لأنه) في جواب المطالبة، فإنه إذا قيل لك: لم قلت: إن النبيذ حرام؟ فتقول: لأنه مسكر، ولا تقول: لأنه نبيذ، ولا تقول: لأنه حرام، فما يقترن به (لأن) هو العلة. ولنسم ما يجري مجرى النبيذ: محكوماً عليه، وما يجري مجرى الحرام: حكماً، فإنا نقول في النتيجة: (فالنبيذ حرام) فنحكم على النبيذ بأنه حرام. ولنشتق للمقدمتين اسمين مختلفين من الأجزاء والمعاني التي تشتمل عليها؛ لتسهل علينا الإشارة إليهما في التفهيم والمخاطبة (1)، ولا يمكن اشتقاق اسمين مختلفين لهما من العلة؛ فإن العلة داخلة فيهما جميعاً، فنشتقه من الجزأين الآخرين.
It is necessary that there is a commonality between the two premises. For example: our statement: 'Every body is composed' and 'Everything composed is temporally originated' results in: 'Every body is temporally originated.' And our statement in jurisprudence: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicating is forbidden' results in: 'Every nabidh is forbidden.' And the custom of jurists in the formulation is to say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating, so it should be forbidden, by analogy with wine.' The matter is not uncovered nor the demand cut off except by the system we have mentioned. And such syllogisms, if they cannot be reduced to this system, the conclusion is not necessary and the demand is not cut off. So if you understand the form of this system, know that in this syllogism there are two premises: one: our statement: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating,' and the other: our statement: 'Every intoxicating is forbidden.' And every premise is necessarily divided into two parts: a subject and a predicate, a ruling and the result, and the thing ruled upon, so the total of its parts is four matters, except that one matter is repeated in the two premises, so it returns to three necessarily; because if it remained four, the two premises would share nothing, and the pairing between them would be invalid, and the result would not be generated. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and you do not mention in the second premise either nabidh or intoxicating, but you say: 'Killing is forbidden' or 'The world is temporally originated,' then one is not connected to the other. So necessarily, one of the four parts must be repeated in the two premises, so it returns to three. So let us agree to call the repeated thing in the two premises: the cause, which is that which can be associated with your statement: 'because it' in response to the demand. For if it is said to you: 'Why did you say that nabidh is forbidden?' You say: 'Because it is intoxicating'; you do not say: 'Because it is nabidh,' nor do you say: 'Because it is forbidden.' So what is associated with 'because' is the cause. And let us call what runs like nabidh: the thing ruled upon, and what runs like the forbidden: the ruling. For we say in the conclusion: 'Nabidh is forbidden,' so we pass the ruling upon nabidh that it is forbidden. And let us derive for the two premises two different names from the parts and meanings they contain, to facilitate our reference to them in teaching and addressing. It is not possible to derive two different names for them from the cause, for the cause is contained in both of them, so we derive it from the other two parts.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage defines the essential structure of a categorical syllogism and introduces its technical terminology. The author begins by asserting the necessity of a common term (ishterāk) between the two premises. He provides two examples: one from kalam (every body is composed, everything composed is originated) and one from fiqh (every nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicating is forbidden). The fiqh example is presented as the usual formulation of jurists: 'Nabidh is intoxicating, so it should be forbidden, by analogy with wine.' The author emphasizes that the conclusion is only necessary when the syllogism is reduced to this systematic form. He then analyzes the syllogism into two premises, each having two parts (subject and predicate), yielding four components in total. However, one component repeats across the premises, reducing the distinct parts to three. If no component were repeated, the premises would be unrelated and no conclusion would follow. He illustrates this by contrasting a proper syllogism with a faulty one where the second premise mentions neither nabidh nor intoxicating but something else like 'killing is forbidden.' The repeated component is termed 'illa (cause), because it is the answer to the question 'why?'—for example, 'Why is nabidh forbidden? Because it is intoxicating.' The remaining two components are named: the thing ruled upon (mahkūm 'alayh) analogous to 'nabidh,' and the ruling (ḥukm) analogous to 'forbidden.' Finally, the author proposes deriving names for the two premises from their other parts (i.e., the non-repeated parts), since the cause appears in both and cannot differentiate them. This sets the stage for naming the premises 'sughra' and 'kubra' based on their subject and predicate.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Terminology of the Syllogism: 'Illah, Mahkūm 'Alayh, Hukm
Introduces the parts and terminology of a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to label the parts of a categorical syllogism using the terms 'illa, mahkum 'alayh, hukm and explain how the premises are named sughra and kubra.

Core Idea
The repeated term is 'illa, the subject is mahkūm 'alayh, the predicate is hukm; from these, the premises are named.

After establishing the necessity of a common term, the author introduces precise terminology for the parts of a syllogism. These terms are essential for clear discourse in logic and fiqh. By learning them, you can systematically analyze any syllogistic argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is the statement that follows from the premises. In the example, it is 'Nabidh is forbidden'.
The conclusion is the target of the syllogism.
2
Determine the subject of the conclusion (mahkum 'alayh)
The subject of the conclusion is the thing being ruled upon. Here it is 'nabidh'. The author calls this mahkum 'alayh.
It is the 'thing ruled upon'.
3
Determine the predicate of the conclusion (hukm)
The predicate of the conclusion is the ruling applied. Here it is 'forbidden'. The author calls this hukm.
It is the 'ruling' or judgment.
4
Identify the repeated term (al-'illa)
The term that appears in both premises is the 'illa. In the example, 'intoxicating' appears in both premises. The author explains that this term answers 'why?'.
The 'illa is also called the middle term.
5
Name the premises: sughra and kubra
The premise that contains the mahkum 'alayh (subject) is called al-sughra (minor premise). The premise that contains the hukm (predicate) is called al-kubra (major premise). In the example, 'Every nabidh is intoxicating' is sughra; 'Every intoxicating is forbidden' is kubra.
The 'illa is in both, so it cannot distinguish the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بد من اشتراك بين المقدمتين، مثال ذلك: قولنا: (كل جسم مؤلف) و(كل مؤلف حادث) فيلزم منه: (أن كل جسم حادث). وقولنا في الفقه: (كل نبيذ مسكر) و(كل مسكر حرام) فيلزم منه: (أن كل نبيذ حرام). وعادة الفقهاء في الصيغة أن يقولوا: (النبيد مسكر، فينبغي أن يكون حراماً؛ قياساً على الخمر). ولا ينكشف الغطاء ولا تنقطع المطالبة إلا بالنظم الذي ذكرناه (1). ومثل هذه الأقيسة إذا لم يمكن ردها إلى هذا النظم لم تكن النتيجة لازمة، ولم تنقطع المطالبة. فإذا فهمت صورة هذا النظم فاعلم أن في هذا القياس مقدمتين: إحداهما: قولنا: (كل نبيذ مسكر). والأخرى: قولنا: (كل مسكر حرام). وكل مقدمة تنقسم إلى جزأين بالضرورة؛ مبتدأ وخبر، وحكم وتحصل النتيجة ومحكوم عليه، فيكون مجموع أجزائها أربعة أمور (2)، إلا أن أمراً واحداً يتكرر في المقدمتين، فتعود إلى ثلاثة بالضرورة؛ لأنها لو بقيت أربعة لم تشترك المقدمتان في شيء، وبطل الازدواج بينهما، فلا تتولد النتيجة. فإنك إذا قلت: (النبيذ مسكر) ولم تتعرض في المقدمة الثانية لا للنبيذ ولا للمسكر، ولكن قلت: (والقتل حرام) أو (والعالم حادث) فلا ترتبط إحداهما بالأخرى، فبالضرورة لا بد من أن يكون أحد الأجزاء الأربعة متكرراً في المقدمتين، فيرجع إلى ثلاثة. فلنصطلح على تسمية المكرر في المقدمتين: علة، وهو الذي يمكن أن يقترن بقولك: (لأنه) في جواب المطالبة، فإنه إذا قيل لك: لم قلت: إن النبيذ حرام؟ فتقول: لأنه مسكر، ولا تقول: لأنه نبيذ، ولا تقول: لأنه حرام، فما يقترن به (لأن) هو العلة. ولنسم ما يجري مجرى النبيذ: محكوماً عليه، وما يجري مجرى الحرام: حكماً، فإنا نقول في النتيجة: (فالنبيذ حرام) فنحكم على النبيذ بأنه حرام. ولنشتق للمقدمتين اسمين مختلفين من الأجزاء والمعاني التي تشتمل عليها؛ لتسهل علينا الإشارة إليهما في التفهيم والمخاطبة (1)، ولا يمكن اشتقاق اسمين مختلفين لهما من العلة؛ فإن العلة داخلة فيهما جميعاً، فنشتقه من الجزأين الآخرين.
It is necessary that there is a commonality between the two premises. For example: our statement: 'Every body is composed' and 'Everything composed is temporally originated' results in: 'Every body is temporally originated.' And our statement in jurisprudence: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicating is forbidden' results in: 'Every nabidh is forbidden.' And the custom of jurists in the formulation is to say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating, so it should be forbidden, by analogy with wine.' The matter is not uncovered nor the demand cut off except by the system we have mentioned. And such syllogisms, if they cannot be reduced to this system, the conclusion is not necessary and the demand is not cut off. So if you understand the form of this system, know that in this syllogism there are two premises: one: our statement: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating,' and the other: our statement: 'Every intoxicating is forbidden.' And every premise is necessarily divided into two parts: a subject and a predicate, a ruling and the result, and the thing ruled upon, so the total of its parts is four matters, except that one matter is repeated in the two premises, so it returns to three necessarily; because if it remained four, the two premises would share nothing, and the pairing between them would be invalid, and the result would not be generated. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and you do not mention in the second premise either nabidh or intoxicating, but you say: 'Killing is forbidden' or 'The world is temporally originated,' then one is not connected to the other. So necessarily, one of the four parts must be repeated in the two premises, so it returns to three. So let us agree to call the repeated thing in the two premises: the cause, which is that which can be associated with your statement: 'because it' in response to the demand. For if it is said to you: 'Why did you say that nabidh is forbidden?' You say: 'Because it is intoxicating'; you do not say: 'Because it is nabidh,' nor do you say: 'Because it is forbidden.' So what is associated with 'because' is the cause. And let us call what runs like nabidh: the thing ruled upon, and what runs like the forbidden: the ruling. For we say in the conclusion: 'Nabidh is forbidden,' so we pass the ruling upon nabidh that it is forbidden. And let us derive for the two premises two different names from the parts and meanings they contain, to facilitate our reference to them in teaching and addressing. It is not possible to derive two different names for them from the cause, for the cause is contained in both of them, so we derive it from the other two parts.

The Syllogism
beginner

The Repeated Term and Naming Premises
Author explains the need for a repeated term and derives names for premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the repeated term (al-'illa) in a categorical syllogism and correctly label the premises as first (minor) and second (major) based on the conclusion's subject and predicate.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires a repeated term that serves as the cause for the conclusion.

This passage lays the foundational structure of a categorical syllogism: three terms, not four. The middle term is called the 'cause' because it provides the reason for the conclusion. By learning how to identify this term and name the premises accordingly, you will be able to analyze any syllogism's structure and verify its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four potential parts
In a two-premise argument, each premise has two parts (subject and predicate), making four distinct parts. If all four are different, the premises share nothing in common.
Example: 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Killing is forbidden' have no common term.
2
Recognize the need for a repeated term
Without a common term, the premises are unrelated and no conclusion follows. One part must be repeated in both premises, reducing the distinct terms to three.
The repeated term is what you would cite when asked 'why?' for the conclusion.
3
Locate the 'illa (cause)
The repeated term is called the 'cause ('illa) because it serves as the reason in answer to 'why?'. For example, if asked 'Why is nabidh forbidden?', you answer 'Because it is intoxicating'. The term 'intoxicating' is the 'illa.
The 'illa is the middle term connecting the two premises.
4
Label the premises based on the conclusion
The conclusion has a subject (mahkum 'alayh) and a predicate (hukm). The premise containing the subject is called the first premise (minor), and the premise containing the predicate is called the second premise (major).
The cause appears in both premises, so it cannot be used to name them uniquely.
5
Apply to an example
Take the syllogism: 'Nabidh is intoxicating; every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' The repeated term is 'intoxicating' (the 'illa). The first premise (minor) is 'Nabidh is intoxicating' (contains subject 'nabidh'), and the second premise (major) is 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (contains predicate 'forbidden').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو بقيَتْ أربعة .. لم تشتركِ المقدّمتان في شيء ، وبطل الازدواج بينهما ، فلا تتولد النتيجة فإِنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( النبيذُ مسكر ) ولم تتعرض في المقدمة الثانية لا للنبيذ ولا للمسكرِ ، ولكن قلت : ( والقتل حرام ) ، أو ) والعالم حادث ) .. فلا ترتبط إحداهما بالأخرى ، فبالضرورة لا بدَّ مِنْ أن يكونَ أحد الأجزاء الأربعة متكرّراً في المقدمتين ، فيرجع إلى ثلاثة . علة ، وهو فلنصطلح على تسمية المكرر في المقدمتين : الذي يمكن أن يقترن بقولِكَ : ( لأنَّهُ ) في جواب المطالبة ، فإِنَّهُ إذا قيلَ لكَ : لِمَ قلتَ : إِنَّ النبيذ حرام ؟ فتقولُ : لأنَّه مسكر ، ولا تقولُ : لأنَّهُ نبيذُ ، ولا تقولُ : لأنَّهُ حرام ، فما يقترنُ بهِ : ( لأنَّ ) هو العلة . ولنسم ما يجري مجرى النبيذ : محكوماً عليه ، وما يجري مجرى الحرام : حُكْماً ، فإنَّا نقولُ في النتيجة : ( فالنبيد حرام ) فنحكم على النبيذ بأنه حرام . ولنشتق للمقدمتين اسمين مختلفينِ مِنَ الأجزاء والمعاني تعيين المقدمتين الصغرى والكبرى التي تشتمل عليها ؛ لتسهل علينا الإشارة إليهما في التفهيم من والمخاطبة ، ولا يمكن اشتقاق اسمين مختلفين لهما مِنَ العلَّةِ ؛ فإنَّ العلَّةَ داخلة فيهما جميعاً ، فنشتقهُ مِنَ الجزأين الآخرين ، فالمقدِّمة التي فيها تعرُّض للمحكوم عليه نسمّيها : المقدمة الأولى ، والتي فيها الحكم نسمّيها : المقدمة الثانية ؛ اشتقاقاً مِنْ ترتيب أجزاء النتيجة . فإنَّا نقولُ في النتيجة : ( فالنبيذُ حرام ) فيكونُ ( النبيذُ ( أولاً ، و( الحرام ) ثانياً والمقدمة التي فيها المحكوم عليه .. لا يُتصوَّرُ أن يكونَ فيها الحكم وهي مقدمة ، والتي فيها الحكم .. لا يُتصوَّرُ أن يكون فيها المحكوم عليه وهي مقدمةٌ ، بل هما خاصتان للمقدمتين .
If four [parts] remained, then the two premises would share nothing, and the pairing between them would be invalid, so no conclusion would be generated. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and you do not mention in the second premise either nabidh or intoxicating, but you say: 'And killing is forbidden,' or 'And the world is originated,' then one does not relate to the other. Therefore necessarily one of the four parts must be repeated in the two premises, so it reduces to three. It is the cause. Let us agree to call the repeated term in the two premises that which can be linked with your saying 'because' in response to a demand. For if it is said to you: 'Why do you say that nabidh is forbidden?' you say: 'Because it is intoxicating,' and you do not say: 'Because it is nabidh,' nor 'Because it is forbidden.' So what is linked with 'because' is the cause. And let us call what is like nabidh: the subject of judgment, and what is like 'forbidden': the judgment. For we say in the conclusion: 'So nabidh is forbidden,' thus we judge about nabidh that it is forbidden. And let us derive for the two premises two different names from the parts and meanings—the determination of the minor and major premises—to facilitate referring to them in teaching and discourse. It is not possible to derive two different names for them from the cause, because the cause is included in both of them. So we derive them from the other two parts. Thus the premise which mentions the subject of judgment we call: the first premise, and the one which contains the judgment we call: the second premise, derived from the order of the parts of the conclusion. For we say in the conclusion: 'So nabidh is forbidden'; 'nabidh' is first and 'forbidden' is second. The premise which contains the subject of judgment cannot contain the judgment while it is a premise, and the premise which contains the judgment cannot contain the subject of judgment while it is a premise. Rather they are exclusive to the two premises.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage explains the essential structure of a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). The author begins by showing that a syllogism must have exactly three terms, not four. If the two premises have no term in common, they are unrelated and no conclusion follows. The repeated term is called the 'cause' ('illa) because it is what you cite when asked 'why?'—as in: 'Why is nabidh forbidden? Because it is intoxicating.' The term 'nabidh' is the subject of judgment (mahkum 'alayh) and 'forbidden' is the judgment (hukm). The premises are named based on which part of the conclusion they contain: the premise with the subject is called the first premise (muqaddima ula) or minor premise; the one with the predicate is the second premise (muqaddima thaniya) or major premise. The cause (middle term) appears in both premises, so it cannot be used to name them. This is a clear exposition of the standard terminology in Aristotelian logic as adopted by Muslim logicians.

محكوم عليه

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism must have a repeated middle term called the 'illa, and the premises are named based on whether they contain the subject (first/minor) or predicate (second/major) of the conclusion.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Accepted Premises and Proof Methods in Syllogisms
Author explains the epistemic condition of premises and their proof methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the epistemic requirements for premises in intellectual vs. jurisprudential syllogisms and identify the appropriate method of proof (sense/experience or transmitted report) for each premise.

Core Idea
A syllogism's conclusion depends on accepted premises — certainty for intellectual matters, probability for legal ones.

You now know that a syllogism must have two premises and a valid form. But even a perfectly formed syllogism is useless if the premises themselves are not true or accepted. The author here adds an epistemological condition: the premises must be 'accepted' (musallamatan), and the level of acceptance depends on the type of conclusion sought. He also explains how to prove each premise when challenged.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The author states that the conclusion follows only if the two premises are accepted (musallamatan). This is a condition for the syllogism to yield a result. Note that 'accepted' here means the premises are taken as true, either with certainty or probability.
2
Distinguish the epistemic level by domain
If the matter under investigation (al-matlab) is intellectual ('aqli) — like proving the existence of God — the premises must be certain (yaqinan). If it is jurisprudential (fiqhi) — like the prohibition of nabidh — probability (zannan) suffices.
This reflects the different epistemic standards in theology and law.
3
Recognize the proof methods for each premise
The author gives an example: the syllogism 'Nabidh is intoxicating, every intoxicating is forbidden, therefore nabidh is forbidden.' If the opponent disputes the minor premise ('every nabidh is intoxicating'), it is proven by sense and experience (bil-hiss wa al-tajriba). If he disputes the major premise, it is proven by transmitted report (bil-naql), specifically the hadith.
This pairing of premises with proof methods is essential for grounding syllogisms in empirical and scriptural evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ النتيجةَ إِنَّما تلزمُ مِنْ هذا القياس إذا كانَتِ أو ظن للمقدمتين شرط للروم النتيجة المقدمتان مسلَّمتين؛ يقيناً إنْ كانَ المطلوب عقليّاً ، أو ظنّاً إِنْ كان المطلوب فقهيّاً . فإن نازعَكَ الخصمُ في قولِكَ : ( كل نبيذ مسكر ) .. فإثبات هذه المقدمة بالحس والتجربة، وإن نازعَكَ في قولِكَ : ( كلُّ مسكر حرام ) .. فإثباتُهُ بالنقل ؛ وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كُلُّ مُسْكِرِ حَرَامٌ . فإن لم تتمكَّنْ مِنْ تحقيق تلك المقدمة بحسن ولا غيره ، ولا مِنْ إثباتِ الثاني بنقل أو غيرِهِ .. لم ينفعك القياسُ
Know that the conclusion only follows from this syllogism if [the two premises are accepted?] as a condition for the conclusion to follow; the two premises are accepted, with certainty if the request is intellectual, or with probability if the request is jurisprudential. For if your opponent disputes your saying: 'Every nabidh is intoxicating' – then the proof of this premise is by sense and experience. And if he disputes your saying: 'Every intoxicating is forbidden' – then its proof is by transmitted report; it is his saying peace be upon him: 'Every intoxicating is forbidden'. If you cannot verify that premise by sense or otherwise, nor prove the second by transmitted report or otherwise, then the syllogism does not benefit you.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is epistemological and expands on a condition of the syllogism. The author explains that for the conclusion to follow, the two premises must be accepted (musallamatan) by the disputant. The type of acceptance depends on the nature of the matter: if the conclusion is about an intellectual matter (e.g., the existence of God), the premises must be certain (yaqinan); if it is a jurisprudential matter (e.g., the prohibition of nabidh), probability (zannan) suffices. The author then illustrates how to prove each premise when disputed: the minor premise 'Every nabidh is intoxicating' is proven by sensory experience and experimentation (al-hiss wa al-tajriba). The major premise 'Every intoxicating is forbidden' is proven by transmitted report (al-naql), specifically the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Every intoxicating is forbidden'. If one cannot establish the minor premise by sense or any other means, nor the major by transmission or otherwise, then the syllogism is useless. This highlights the foundational role of empirical and scriptural evidence in Islamic legal reasoning.

Key Terms
التسليم
المطلوب
بالحس والتجربة
بالنقل

Checkpoint

Necessity of Conclusion from Premises: The Attribute-Described Link
Author argues that conclusion follows necessarily from accepted premises via attribute-description link.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the conclusion necessarily follows when both premises are accepted, using the attribute-description relationship in a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Accepting the premises forces the conclusion — no rational escape.

This passage demonstrates a foundational principle: if you truly accept both premises of a valid syllogism, you must accept the conclusion. The author illustrates this with the relationship between an attribute and its described subject, using a classic Islamic legal example: nabidh (date-wine) and intoxication. Understanding this necessity protects us from self-contradiction in reasoning about Islamic rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Find the two premises and the conclusion in the author's example: minor premise: 'Nabidh is intoxicating'; major premise: 'Every intoxicating is forbidden'; conclusion: 'Nabidh is forbidden'.
2
Trace the attribute-description link
Notice that the major premise judges the attribute (intoxicating), and the minor premise describes nabidh as having that attribute. The conclusion then applies the judgment to the described (nabidh). This is the logical bridge.
The attribute in the minor becomes the subject in the major.
3
Understand the reductio argument
The author shows that denying the conclusion while accepting the premises leads to a contradiction: it would imply an intoxicating thing (nabidh) that is not forbidden, falsifying the universal major premise. Since the major is true, the denial is impossible.
This proves the conclusion must be true.
4
Recognize the logical necessity
The conclusion is not an arbitrary addition; it is already implicit in the conjunction of premises. Any rational mind that brings the premises together must affirm the conclusion.
This is the essence of syllogistic validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومهما سُلِمَتا .. لمْ يُتصوَّرِ النزاع في النتيجة ألبتةَ ، بلْ كُلُّ عقل صدَّقَ بالمقدمتين .. فهو مضطر إلى التصديق بالنتيجة مهما أحضرَهُما في الذهن ، وأحضر مجموعهما بالبال . على : أن الحكم على الصفة الحكمـ الصفة حكم على فقد الموصوف بالضرورة وحاصل وجهِ الدلالة في هذا النظم حكم على الموصوفِ ؛ فإِنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( النبيذُ مسكر ) . . فقد جعلت المسكر وصفاً ، فإذا قلت : ( المسكر حرام ) . . فقد حكمْتَ على الوصف ، فبالضرورة يدخلُ فيهِ الموصوفُ ؛ فإِنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( النبيذُ مسكرُ ) و ( كلُّ مسكر حرامٌ ( فإِنَّهُ إِنْ بطل قولنا : ( النبيذُ حرام ( معَ أَنَّهُ سُلّمَ أَنَّهُ مسكرٌ .. بطل قولنا : ( إِنَّ كلَّ مسكر حرام ) إِذْ ظهر لنا مسكر ليس بحرام ! ومهما صدقَتِ القضيَّةُ العامَّةُ .. لمْ يمكن أن يخرج منها بعضُ المسمياتِ
And whenever the two [premises] are accepted, then dispute in the conclusion is absolutely inconceivable. Rather, every intellect that affirms the two premises is compelled to affirm the conclusion whenever it summons them in the mind and brings their conjunction to attention. [Know] that the judgment upon the attribute is a judgment upon the described by necessity. The gist of the indication in this composition is judgment upon the described. For when you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating,' you have made 'intoxicating' an attribute. Then when you say: 'The intoxicating is forbidden,' you have judged upon the attribute, so necessarily the described enters into it. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicating is forbidden,' then if our saying 'Nabidh is forbidden' is invalid while it has been accepted that it is intoxicating, then our saying 'Every intoxicating is forbidden' would be invalid, because there would appear to us an intoxicating that is not forbidden! And whenever the universal proposition is true, it is impossible that some of its individuals fall outside.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is syllogistic and explains the logical necessity of the conclusion when both premises are accepted. The author states that if both premises are granted, then the conclusion cannot be disputed; any rational mind that affirms the premises must affirm the conclusion, especially when the premises are brought together in thought. The author then provides the underlying rationale via the relationship between attribute (sifa) and described (mawsuf). He explains that the major premise 'Every intoxicating (attribute) is forbidden' judges the attribute; since the minor premise asserts that nabidh is described by that attribute, the judgment necessarily applies to the described (nabidh). The author uses a reductio argument: if one were to deny the conclusion 'Nabidh is forbidden' while accepting that nabidh is intoxicating and that every intoxicating is forbidden, then the universal major premise would be contradicted, because there would exist an intoxicating (nabidh) that is not forbidden. Since the universal is true, no individual can escape. This demonstrates the necessity of the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of a valid syllogism is not optional; it follows necessarily from the premises, and denying it leads to contradiction.

The Syllogism

Two Conditions for a Valid First-Figure Syllogism
Author states the two conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism: minor premise affirmative, major premise universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a first-figure categorical syllogism satisfies the conditions of affirmative minor and universal major, and explain why a violation prevents a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
A valid first-figure syllogism needs an affirmative minor and a universal major.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the only one that can yield all four types of conclusions directly. But it has strict conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal. Without these, the syllogism fails to produce a necessary conclusion. In this lesson, we examine why these conditions are essential, using the example from the text of vinegar and intoxicants.

Guided Path
1
State the two conditions
Recall the two conditions for a productive first-figure syllogism: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative, (2) the major premise must be universal. Write them down.
These are standard in Aristotelian logic and Islamic logic.
2
Analyze the negative minor example
Consider the example: 'No vinegar is intoxicating' (minor, negative) and 'Every intoxicating is forbidden' (major, universal). Since the minor is negative, the subject 'vinegar' is excluded from the middle term 'intoxicating'. The major premise only speaks about intoxicating things, so it cannot apply to vinegar. No conclusion follows.
This illustrates why the minor must be affirmative.
3
Test a valid example
Now consider a valid first-figure syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major, universal) and 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor, affirmative). Here, the minor is affirmative, so nabidh is included under intoxicant. The conclusion 'Every nabidh is forbidden' follows necessarily.
Both conditions are satisfied, so the syllogism is productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا النظم له شرطانِ حتَّى يكون منتجاً : شروط انتاج الشكل الأول شرطٌ في المقدمة الأولى : وهو أن تكون مثبتةٌ : فإنْ كانَتْ . نافية .. لم ينتج ؛ لأنَّكَ إذا نفيتَ شيئاً عَنْ شيء .. لم يكن الأول الأول : إيجاد الصغرى الحكم على المنفي حكماً على المنفي عنه ؛ فإنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( لا خل واحد مسكر ) ، و ( كلُّ مسكر حرام ) .. لم يلزم منه حكم في الخل ؛ إذ وقعَتِ المباينة بين الخل والمسكر ، فحكمك على المسكر بالنفي أو الإثباتِ لا يتعدى إلى الخل . ألبتة . الشرط الثاني : أن تكون المقدمة الثانية عامة : حتَّى يدخل الثاني : كلية الكبرى الثاني
This composition has two conditions to be productive: Conditions for the production of the first figure: A condition in the first premise: it must be affirmative. If it is negative, it does not produce, because when you negate something from something, the judgment upon the negated is not a judgment upon the thing negated from. For if you say: 'No vinegar is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicating is forbidden,' no judgment about vinegar follows, because dissimilarity (mubāyana) occurs between vinegar and intoxicating. So your judgment upon intoxicating, whether negative or positive, does not extend to vinegar at all. The second condition: the second premise must be universal, so that it includes. Second: universality of the major.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage states the two conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism (iqtirānī) to be productive (muntij). The conditions are: (1) The first premise (minor premise) must be affirmative (muthbata). If it is negative, no conclusion follows. The author explains why: if the minor premise is negative, e.g., 'No vinegar is intoxicating', then the subject of the minor (vinegar) is excluded from the middle term (intoxicating). Since the major premise ('Every intoxicating is forbidden') only talks about intoxicating, it does not apply to vinegar. So no conclusion about vinegar can be drawn. (2) The second premise (major premise) must be universal (kulliyya). This ensures that the major term applies to all individuals of the middle term, so that the subject of the minor (which is a subset of the middle) is included. The author uses the example of vinegar (khall) and nabidh to illustrate. This is standard Aristotelian teaching for the first figure.

Key Terms
المنتج
المثبتة
النافية
كلية الكبرى
المباينة

Key Takeaway
For a first-figure categorical syllogism to yield a necessary conclusion, the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal.
