The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Two Conditions for a Productive First-Figure Syllogism
Author states two conditions for a productive first-figure syllogism and tests them with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify whether a categorical syllogism in the first figure is productive by applying al-Ghazali's two conditions: affirmative minor and universal major.

Core Idea
The first figure yields a necessary conclusion only when the minor is affirmative and the major is universal.

Al-Ghazali explains that for a first-figure categorical syllogism to produce a certain conclusion, two conditions must be met: the first premise (minor) must be affirmative, and the second premise (major) must be universal. Without these conditions, the conclusion does not follow necessarily, as illustrated by the author using examples from Islamic jurisprudence such as nabidh, vinegar, and quince.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first (minor) premise
Find the premise that contains the subject of the conclusion. Check whether it is affirmative or negative. A productive first figure requires an affirmative minor.
In al-Ghazali's example, 'Nabidh is intoxicating' is affirmative.
2
Identify the second (major) premise
Find the premise that contains the predicate of the conclusion. Check whether it is universal (e.g., 'every' or 'no') or particular (e.g., 'some'). A productive first figure requires a universal major.
The example 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is universal.
3
Test with the author's examples
Apply the conditions to the given examples: (1) Nabidh example works. (2) Vinegar example fails because minor is negative. (3) Quince example fails because major is particular.
If either condition is violated, no necessary conclusion follows.
4
Conclude whether the syllogism is productive
If both conditions are met, the conclusion is necessary. Otherwise, the syllogism is not productive in the first figure.
The author emphasizes that when the universal premise is true, no individual can fall outside.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سُلِمَتا .. لمْ يُتصوَّرِ النزاع في النتيجة ألبتةَ ، بلْ كُلُّ عقل صدَّقَ بالمقدمتين .. فهو مضطر إلى التصديق بالنتيجة مهما أحضرَهُما في الذهن ، وأحضر مجموعهما بالبال . على أن الحكم على الصفة حكم على الموصوف بالضرورة وحاصل وجه الدلالة في هذا النظم حكم على الموصوفِ ؛ فإِنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( النبيذُ مسكر ) . . فقد جعلت المسكر وصفاً ، فإذا قلت : ( المسكر حرام ) . . فقد حكمْتَ على الوصف ، فبالضرورة يدخلُ فيهِ الموصوفُ ؛ فإِنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( النبيذُ مسكرُ ) و ( كلُّ مسكر حرامٌ ) فإِنَّهُ إِنْ بطل قولنا : ( النبيذُ حرام ) معَ أَنَّهُ سُلّمَ أَنَّهُ مسكرٌ .. بطل قولنا : ( إِنَّ كلَّ مسكر حرام ) إِذْ ظهر لنا مسكر ليس بحرام ! ومهما صدقَتِ القضيَّةُ العامَّةُ .. لمْ يمكن أن يخرج منها بعضُ المسمياتِ . وهذا النظم له شرطانِ حتَّى يكون منتجاً : شرطٌ في المقدمة الأولى : وهو أن تكون مثبتةٌ : فإنْ كانَتْ نافية .. لم ينتج ؛ لأنَّكَ إذا نفيتَ شيئاً عَنْ شيء .. لم يكن الحكم على المنفي حكماً على المنفي عنه ؛ فإنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( لا خل واحد مسكر ) ، و ( كلُّ مسكر حرام ) .. لم يلزم منه حكم في الخل ؛ إذ وقعَتِ المباينة بين الخل والمسكر ، فحكمك على المسكر بالنفي أو الإثباتِ لا يتعدى إلى الخل . ألبتة . الشرط الثاني : أن تكون المقدمة الثانية عامة : حتَّى يدخل بسبب عمومها المحكوم عليه فيه ، فإِنَّكَ لَوْ قلتَ : ( كُلُّ سفرجل مطعوم ) ، و ( بعض المطعوم ربوي ) . . لم يلزم منه كون السفرجل ربويّاً ؛ إذ ليس مِنْ ضرورةِ الحكم على بعض المطعوم أن يتناول السفرجل ، بلْ ربَّما كان الربوي بعضاً آخر . نعم ؛ إذا قلت : ( وكلُّ مطعوم ربوي ) .. لزم حكم الربا في السفرجل ، ولكن يحتاجُ إلى إثبات المقدمة بعموم قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « لَا تَبِيعُوا الطَّعَامَ بِالطَّعَام » ، أو بمدرك آخر ، وسنذكرُ مدارك المقدّماتِ في الطرف الثاني مِنْ هذا الفن . فإن قلت : بماذا فارقَ هذا النظم النظمين بعدَهُ ؟ فاعلم : أنَّ العلَّةَ إِمَّا أنْ توضعَ محكوماً عليه في المقدمتين ، أو توضع محكوماً بها في المقدمتين أو توضع بحيث يكون حكماً في إحدى المقدمتين ، محكوماً عليه في الأخرى ، وهذا الأخير هو النظمُ الأوَّلُ ، وهو الأوضحُ ؛ فإِنَّ الثاني والثالث لا يتضحُ غاية الاتضاح بالبرهان المحقق إلا بالرد إليه .
If the [premises] are granted, dispute about the conclusion is not at all conceived; rather, every intellect that affirms the two premises is compelled to affirm the conclusion whenever it brings them to mind and brings their combination to memory. Moreover, the judgment upon the attribute is a judgment upon the described necessarily. The summary of the indication in this syllogistic form is a judgment upon the described. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating,' you have made intoxicating an attribute. Then if you say: 'The intoxicating is forbidden,' you have judged the attribute, so necessarily the described enters into it. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden,' then if our statement 'Nabidh is forbidden' is false while it is acknowledged that it is intoxicating, our statement that 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' becomes false, because an intoxicant that is not forbidden appears to us. And whenever the universal proposition is true, it is impossible that some of its individuals fall outside. This syllogistic form has two conditions to be productive: A condition in the first premise: that it be affirmative. For if it is negative, it does not produce, because when you negate something from something, the judgment of the negation is not a judgment upon the thing negated from. For if you say: 'No vinegar is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden,' no judgment about vinegar follows, because separation occurs between vinegar and intoxicant, so your judgment about intoxicant, whether negative or affirmative, does not extend to vinegar at all. The second condition: that the second premise be universal, so that what is judged upon enters due to its universality. For if you say: 'Every quince is edible' and 'Some edible is subject to riba,' it does not follow that quince is subject to riba, because it is not necessary that a judgment upon some edible includes the quince; rather, perhaps the riba-subject is another part. Yes, if you say: 'Every edible is subject to riba,' then the ruling of riba applies to quince, but this requires establishing the premise by the generality of his statement, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell food for food' or by another source. We will mention the sources of premises in the second part of this discipline. If you say: 'How does this syllogistic form differ from the two forms after it?' Know: the cause is either placed as what is judged upon in both premises, or placed as what is judged by in both premises, or placed such that it is a judgment in one premise and what is judged upon in the other. This last is the first form, and it is the clearest. Indeed, the second and third do not become fully clear by real demonstration except by reduction to it.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage explains the necessary conditions for the first figure of the categorical syllogism to be productive (i.e., yield a conclusion) and distinguishes it from the second and third figures. The author begins by asserting that once the premises are granted, any rational person who affirms them must also affirm the conclusion—this is the essence of syllogistic necessity. He illustrates with an example from Islamic jurisprudence: nabidh (a date drink) is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore nabidh is forbidden. The underlying logical principle is that a judgment on an attribute (intoxicating) necessarily transfers to the described subject (nabidh). If the conclusion were false, the universal premise would be falsified. Two conditions are then given for the first figure: (1) the minor premise (المقدمة الأولى) must be affirmative; if negative, no conclusion follows. The author illustrates with vinegar: 'No vinegar is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' yields no conclusion about vinegar because the subject of the minor (vinegar) is external to the middle term (intoxicant). (2) The major premise (المقدمة الثانية) must be universal (kulliya). An example with quince (safarjal): 'Every quince is edible' and 'Some edible is subject to riba' does not prove quince is subject to riba because the universal covers only part of the edible category; the quince might be outside that part. Only a universal major ('Every edible is subject to riba') would make the conclusion follow, but then the premise must be established by a hadith or other evidence. Finally, the author addresses a question: how does this first form differ from the other two forms? The answer is based on the role of the 'cause' or middle term (العلة) in the premises. In the first form (الشكل الأول), the cause is a judgment in one premise and what is judged upon in the other (i.e., it is predicate in the minor and subject in the major). In the second form (الشكل الثاني), the cause is what is judged upon in both premises (i.e., subject of both). In the third form (الشكل الثالث), the cause is what is judged by in both premises (i.e., predicate of both). The first form is the clearest, and the others require reduction to it for complete clarity. This distinction is fundamental to syllogistic theory.

Key Terms
النَّظْمُ (al-naẓm): The syllogistic arrangement or form (i.e., the particular ordering of premises in a syllogism). In this passage it refers to the structure of the first figure. [Note: In later logic, 'shakl' (figure) is more commonly used, but al-Ghazali uses 'naẓm' interchangeably.]
المُقَدِّمَةُ الأُولَى (al-muqaddima al-ūlā): The first premise, which in a two-premise syllogism corresponds to the minor premise (الصغرى). [Note: No significant shift.]
المُقَدِّمَةُ الثَّانِيَةُ (al-muqaddima al-thāniya): The second premise, which corresponds to the major premise (الكبرى) in the standard first figure. [Note: No significant shift.]
الشَّكْلُ الأَوَّلُ (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. The author states it is the clearest and the only one that can be proven without reduction. [Note: Al-Ghazali recognizes only three figures, unlike later logicians who adopt four figures following Avicenna.]
العِلَّةُ (al-ʿilla): The cause or middle term that mediates the connection between the two extremes. In syllogistic, it is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, ʿilla refers to the effective cause of a legal ruling; here it is a purely logical middle term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No vinegar is intoxicating. Every intoxicant is forbidden. Therefore, no vinegar is forbidden.
B) Every quince is edible. Some edible is subject to riba. Therefore, quince is subject to riba.
C) Nabidh is intoxicating. Every intoxicant is forbidden. Therefore, nabidh is forbidden.
D) Some intoxicant is not forbidden. All nabidh is intoxicating. Therefore, some nabidh is not forbidden.

Key Takeaway
A first-figure categorical syllogism is productive only when its minor premise is affirmative and its major premise is universal.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Distinguishing the First Figure from the Second and Third Figures
Author distinguishes the first figure from the second and third figures by the role of the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a categorical syllogism into the first, second, or third figure based on the position of the middle term (cause) in the premises.

Core Idea
The three figures are defined by whether the middle term is predicate, subject, or both in the premises.

Al-Ghazali explains that the first figure is the clearest and most fundamental, while the second and third figures must be reduced to the first for full certainty. The distinction lies in the role of the 'cause' (middle term) in the two premises: in the first figure, it is a judgment in one and what is judged in the other; in the second, it is what is judged upon in both; in the third, it is what is judged by in both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term (cause)
Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term.
In the nabidh example, the middle term is 'intoxicating'.
2
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise
For the first premise (minor), is the middle term the subject or predicate? For the second premise (major), is it the subject or predicate?
In the first figure, the middle term is predicate of the minor and subject of the major.
3
Classify the figure
Apply al-Ghazali's criteria: If the middle term is predicate in the minor and subject in the major → first figure. If it is subject in both → second figure. If it is predicate in both → third figure.
These correspond to the standard definitions in Aristotelian logic.
4
Understand why the first figure is clearest
Because the middle term links the extremes in a natural way, the conclusion follows immediately without conversion or reduction.
The author notes that the second and third figures require reduction to the first for full clarity.

سُلِمَتا .. لمْ يُتصوَّرِ النزاع في النتيجة ألبتةَ ، بلْ كُلُّ عقل صدَّقَ بالمقدمتين .. فهو مضطر إلى التصديق بالنتيجة مهما أحضرَهُما في الذهن ، وأحضر مجموعهما بالبال . على أن الحكم على الصفة حكم على الموصوف بالضرورة وحاصل وجه الدلالة في هذا النظم حكم على الموصوفِ ؛ فإِنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( النبيذُ مسكر ) . . فقد جعلت المسكر وصفاً ، فإذا قلت : ( المسكر حرام ) . . فقد حكمْتَ على الوصف ، فبالضرورة يدخلُ فيهِ الموصوفُ ؛ فإِنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( النبيذُ مسكرُ ) و ( كلُّ مسكر حرامٌ ) فإِنَّهُ إِنْ بطل قولنا : ( النبيذُ حرام ) معَ أَنَّهُ سُلّمَ أَنَّهُ مسكرٌ .. بطل قولنا : ( إِنَّ كلَّ مسكر حرام ) إِذْ ظهر لنا مسكر ليس بحرام ! ومهما صدقَتِ القضيَّةُ العامَّةُ .. لمْ يمكن أن يخرج منها بعضُ المسمياتِ . وهذا النظم له شرطانِ حتَّى يكون منتجاً : شرطٌ في المقدمة الأولى : وهو أن تكون مثبتةٌ : فإنْ كانَتْ نافية .. لم ينتج ؛ لأنَّكَ إذا نفيتَ شيئاً عَنْ شيء .. لم يكن الحكم على المنفي حكماً على المنفي عنه ؛ فإنَّكَ إذا قلت : ( لا خل واحد مسكر ) ، و ( كلُّ مسكر حرام ) .. لم يلزم منه حكم في الخل ؛ إذ وقعَتِ المباينة بين الخل والمسكر ، فحكمك على المسكر بالنفي أو الإثباتِ لا يتعدى إلى الخل . ألبتة . الشرط الثاني : أن تكون المقدمة الثانية عامة : حتَّى يدخل بسبب عمومها المحكوم عليه فيه ، فإِنَّكَ لَوْ قلتَ : ( كُلُّ سفرجل مطعوم ) ، و ( بعض المطعوم ربوي ) . . لم يلزم منه كون السفرجل ربويّاً ؛ إذ ليس مِنْ ضرورةِ الحكم على بعض المطعوم أن يتناول السفرجل ، بلْ ربَّما كان الربوي بعضاً آخر . نعم ؛ إذا قلت : ( وكلُّ مطعوم ربوي ) .. لزم حكم الربا في السفرجل ، ولكن يحتاجُ إلى إثبات المقدمة بعموم قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « لَا تَبِيعُوا الطَّعَامَ بِالطَّعَام » ، أو بمدرك آخر ، وسنذكرُ مدارك المقدّماتِ في الطرف الثاني مِنْ هذا الفن . فإن قلت : بماذا فارقَ هذا النظم النظمين بعدَهُ ؟ فاعلم : أنَّ العلَّةَ إِمَّا أنْ توضعَ محكوماً عليه في المقدمتين ، أو توضع محكوماً بها في المقدمتين أو توضع بحيث يكون حكماً في إحدى المقدمتين ، محكوماً عليه في الأخرى ، وهذا الأخير هو النظمُ الأوَّلُ ، وهو الأوضحُ ؛ فإِنَّ الثاني والثالث لا يتضحُ غاية الاتضاح بالبرهان المحقق إلا بالرد إليه .
If the [premises] are granted, dispute about the conclusion is not at all conceived; rather, every intellect that affirms the two premises is compelled to affirm the conclusion whenever it brings them to mind and brings their combination to memory. Moreover, the judgment upon the attribute is a judgment upon the described necessarily. The summary of the indication in this syllogistic form is a judgment upon the described. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating,' you have made intoxicating an attribute. Then if you say: 'The intoxicating is forbidden,' you have judged the attribute, so necessarily the described enters into it. For if you say: 'Nabidh is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden,' then if our statement 'Nabidh is forbidden' is false while it is acknowledged that it is intoxicating, our statement that 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' becomes false, because an intoxicant that is not forbidden appears to us. And whenever the universal proposition is true, it is impossible that some of its individuals fall outside. This syllogistic form has two conditions to be productive: A condition in the first premise: that it be affirmative. For if it is negative, it does not produce, because when you negate something from something, the judgment of the negation is not a judgment upon the thing negated from. For if you say: 'No vinegar is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden,' no judgment about vinegar follows, because separation occurs between vinegar and intoxicant, so your judgment about intoxicant, whether negative or affirmative, does not extend to vinegar at all. The second condition: that the second premise be universal, so that what is judged upon enters due to its universality. For if you say: 'Every quince is edible' and 'Some edible is subject to riba,' it does not follow that quince is subject to riba, because it is not necessary that a judgment upon some edible includes the quince; rather, perhaps the riba-subject is another part. Yes, if you say: 'Every edible is subject to riba,' then the ruling of riba applies to quince, but this requires establishing the premise by the generality of his statement, peace be upon him: 'Do not sell food for food' or by another source. We will mention the sources of premises in the second part of this discipline. If you say: 'How does this syllogistic form differ from the two forms after it?' Know: the cause is either placed as what is judged upon in both premises, or placed as what is judged by in both premises, or placed such that it is a judgment in one premise and what is judged upon in the other. This last is the first form, and it is the clearest. Indeed, the second and third do not become fully clear by real demonstration except by reduction to it.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage explains the necessary conditions for the first figure of the categorical syllogism to be productive (i.e., yield a conclusion) and distinguishes it from the second and third figures. The author begins by asserting that once the premises are granted, any rational person who affirms them must also affirm the conclusion—this is the essence of syllogistic necessity. He illustrates with an example from Islamic jurisprudence: nabidh (a date drink) is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden; therefore nabidh is forbidden. The underlying logical principle is that a judgment on an attribute (intoxicating) necessarily transfers to the described subject (nabidh). If the conclusion were false, the universal premise would be falsified. Two conditions are then given for the first figure: (1) the minor premise (المقدمة الأولى) must be affirmative; if negative, no conclusion follows. The author illustrates with vinegar: 'No vinegar is intoxicating' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' yields no conclusion about vinegar because the subject of the minor (vinegar) is external to the middle term (intoxicant). (2) The major premise (المقدمة الثانية) must be universal (kulliya). An example with quince (safarjal): 'Every quince is edible' and 'Some edible is subject to riba' does not prove quince is subject to riba because the universal covers only part of the edible category; the quince might be outside that part. Only a universal major ('Every edible is subject to riba') would make the conclusion follow, but then the premise must be established by a hadith or other evidence. Finally, the author addresses a question: how does this first form differ from the other two forms? The answer is based on the role of the 'cause' or middle term (العلة) in the premises. In the first form (الشكل الأول), the cause is a judgment in one premise and what is judged upon in the other (i.e., it is predicate in the minor and subject in the major). In the second form (الشكل الثاني), the cause is what is judged upon in both premises (i.e., subject of both). In the third form (الشكل الثالث), the cause is what is judged by in both premises (i.e., predicate of both). The first form is the clearest, and the others require reduction to it for complete clarity. This distinction is fundamental to syllogistic theory.

Key Terms
النَّظْمُ (al-naẓm): The syllogistic arrangement or form (i.e., the particular ordering of premises in a syllogism). In this passage it refers to the structure of the first figure. [Note: In later logic, 'shakl' (figure) is more commonly used, but al-Ghazali uses 'naẓm' interchangeably.]
المُقَدِّمَةُ الأُولَى (al-muqaddima al-ūlā): The first premise, which in a two-premise syllogism corresponds to the minor premise (الصغرى). [Note: No significant shift.]
المُقَدِّمَةُ الثَّانِيَةُ (al-muqaddima al-thāniya): The second premise, which corresponds to the major premise (الكبرى) in the standard first figure. [Note: No significant shift.]
الشَّكْلُ الأَوَّلُ (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. The author states it is the clearest and the only one that can be proven without reduction. [Note: Al-Ghazali recognizes only three figures, unlike later logicians who adopt four figures following Avicenna.]
العِلَّةُ (al-ʿilla): The cause or middle term that mediates the connection between the two extremes. In syllogistic, it is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, ʿilla refers to the effective cause of a legal ruling; here it is a purely logical middle term.]

The Second Figure of the Categorical Syllogism
Author classifies the three figures and elaborates the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three figures of the categorical syllogism, construct a valid second-figure syllogism, and explain why it requires reduction to the first figure for full clarity.

Core Idea
The second figure's middle term is predicate in both premises; its validity is proven only when reduced to the first figure.

In this passage, the author classifies the three arrangements (نظم) of the categorical syllogism and then focuses on the second figure, where the middle term appears as a predicate in both premises. Using a theological example — the Creator is not a body because He is not composed, and everything composed is a body — he demonstrates how the second figure works and why it is less clear than the first. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three arrangements
The author lists three possible positions of the middle term: (1) subject in both premises, (2) predicate in both premises, (3) subject in one and predicate in the other. The last is the first figure; the second figure is option (2).
The author uses 'nazm' (نظم) interchangeably with 'shakl' (شكل).
2
Distinguish the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term is a predicate (محكوماً بها) in both premises. It is never a subject. This is the defining characteristic.
The author calls it 'النظم الثاني'.
3
Analyze the example
Break down the example: 'The Creator is not composed' (minor premise, negative), 'Everything composed is a body' (major premise, affirmative). The middle term 'composed' is predicate in both. The conclusion 'The Creator is not a body' follows.
Note the mood: Camestres (AEE in second figure).
4
Understand reduction to the first figure
The author states the second figure does not attain full clarity except by reduction to the first figure. This means converting one premise or rearranging to make the middle term subject in one and predicate in the other, which makes the logical connection more obvious.
Reduction methods include conversion of the major premise in this example.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: بماذا فارق هذا النظم النظمين بعده؟ فاعلم: أن العلة إما أن توضع محكوماً عليه في المقدمتين، أو توضع محكوماً بها في المقدمتين، أو توضع بحيث يكون حكماً في إحدى المقدمتين، محكوماً عليه في الأخرى، وهذا الأخير هو النظم الأول، وهو الأوضح؛ فإن الثاني والثالث لا يتضح غاية الاتضاح بالبرهان المحقق إلا بالرد إليه. فلذلك قدمته وسميته النظم الأول الأوضح. النظم الثاني للقياس: أن تكون العلة - أعني: المعنى المتكرّر في المقدمتين - حكماً في المقدمتين: أعني: أن يكون خبراً فيهما، ولا يكون مبتدأ في إحداهما وخبراً في الأخرى، ولا مبتدأ فيهما جميعاً. مثاله: قولنا: إن الباري تعالى ليس بجسم؛ لأن الباري ليس بمؤلف، وكل مؤلّف جسم؛ فالباري ليس بجسم. فها هنا ثلاثة معان: الباري، والمؤلّف، والجسم، والمتكرّر في المقدمتين هو المؤلف، فهو العلة، وتراه خبراً في المقدمتين، غير مبتدأ به، بخلاف المتكرّر في النظم الأول؛ إذ كان خبراً في إحداهما، مبتدأ في الأخرى. ووجه لزوم النتيجة يمكن تفهيمه مجملاً، ومفصلاً ومحققاً: أما المجمل: فهو أن كل شيئين ثبت لأحدهما ما انتفى عن الآخر.. فلا يكون بينهما التقاء واتصال؛ أي: لا يجوز أن يخبر بأحدهما عن الآخر، فالتأليف ثابت للجسم، ومنفي عن الباري، فلا يكون بين معنى الجسم والباري التقاء، بل هما متباينان.
If you say: By what did this arrangement differ from the two arrangements after it? Know that the middle term is either placed as subject in the two premises, or placed as predicate in the two premises, or placed such that it is a predicate in one of the two premises and a subject in the other. This last is the first arrangement, and it is the clearest; for the second and third do not attain the utmost clarity in verified demonstration except by reduction to it. Therefore I have presented it first and called it the first, clearest arrangement. The second arrangement of the syllogism is that the middle term—meaning the meaning repeated in the two premises—be a predicate in the two premises: meaning that it be a predicate in both, and it is not a subject in one of them and a predicate in the other, nor a subject in both. Its example: our saying: Indeed the Creator, exalted is He, is not a body, because the Creator is not composed, and everything composed is a body; therefore the Creator is not a body. So here there are three meanings: the Creator, the composed, and the body. The repeated term in the two premises is 'composed', so it is the middle term, and you see it as predicate in both premises, not as subject, unlike the repeated term in the first arrangement, for it was predicate in one and subject in the other. The way the conclusion follows can be understood summarily, in detail, and with verification. As for the summary: it is that any two things such that one has what the other lacks... then there is no conjunction and connection between them; i.e., it is not permissible to predicate one of the other. So composition is affirmed of the body and negated of the Creator; thus there is no conjunction between the meaning of body and the Creator; rather they are mutually exclusive.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a sustained explanation of the three figures of the categorical syllogism, with a focus on the second figure. The author begins by answering an implicit question: how does the first figure differ from the second and third? He states that the middle term (al-illa) can be placed in three ways relative to the two premises: (1) as subject in both (third figure), (2) as predicate in both (second figure), or (3) as subject in one and predicate in the other (first figure). The first figure is the clearest because the second and third require reduction to it to be fully evident. The author then defines the second figure explicitly: the middle term must be a predicate in both premises. He provides an example from Islamic theology: 'The Creator is not a body, because He is not composed, and everything composed is a body.' The middle term 'composed' appears as predicate in both premises ('the Creator is not composed' and 'everything composed is a body'), confirming the second figure. The author then explains how the conclusion follows: if one thing (composition) is affirmed of body but negated of the Creator, then body and Creator are mutually exclusive—they cannot be predicated of each other. This explanation is given in summary form: two things that do not share a predicate in common have no connection. The passage thus provides both a classification of syllogistic figures and a detailed analysis of the second figure, grounding the reasoning in a concrete theological example.

Second Mode of Categorical Syllogism (Predicate in Both Premises)
Author defines the second mode of categorical syllogism with example of the Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second mode of categorical syllogism and verify its validity using the exclusion principle.

Core Idea
In the second mode, the middle term is predicate in both premises, leading to mutual exclusion.

In the first mode of categorical syllogism, the middle term (cause) is subject in one premise and predicate in the other. The second mode flips this: the middle term is predicate in both premises. This positioning creates a different logical force—if two terms are opposite in relation to the middle, they cannot be connected. The author uses a theological example about the Creator to illustrate this, reinforcing that logical forms apply even in sacred discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
In the example, find the three distinct meanings: the Creator (subject of the conclusion), composite (middle term), and body (predicate of the conclusion). Write them down.
2
Locate the middle term in both premises
Check where 'composite' appears. In the first premise: 'The Creator is not composite' — composite is predicate (after 'not'). In the second: 'Every composite is a body' — composite is subject here? Wait, the author says it is predicate in both premises. Actually read carefully: 'كل مؤلف جسم' — here 'مؤلف' is subject. There seems to be a nuance: the author considers the middle term as predicate in the sense that it is the predicate of the first premise (since it comes after the negative copula) and in the second premise it is the subject of the universal affirmative? The author explicitly says it is predicate in both. Let's re-express: In the first premise 'الباري ليس بمؤلف', 'مؤلف' is the predicate (the predicate of negation). In the second 'كل مؤلف جسم', 'مؤلف' is the subject. The author's classification might be based on the logical predicate role: in categorical propositions, the predicate is what is affirmed or denied of the subject. In 'الباري ليس بمؤلف', 'مؤلف' is indeed the predicate. In 'كل مؤلف جسم', 'مؤلف' is the subject and 'جسم' is the predicate. So the middle term is not predicate in both; it is predicate in one and subject in the other. This contradicts the definition. However, the passage states 'خبراً فيهما', meaning it is a predicate (khabar) in both. How? Perhaps the author considers the predicate in a broader sense: the term that is repeated, regardless of its grammatical role? Or there might be an alternative reading. Let's trust the source: the author says it is predicate in both. For the guided path, we will follow the author's claim and note the discrepancy. But to be pedagogically sound, we must clarify. The common understanding of second figure is that the middle term is predicate in both premises (e.g., Cesare: No P is M, All S is M, therefore No S is P). Here No Creator is composite (P is M negative? Actually not: 'The Creator is not composite' can be read as 'No Creator is composite' which is E proposition with subject Creator and predicate composite. 'Every composite is a body' is A with subject composite and predicate body. In logic, middle term is composite: in first premise it is predicate, in second it is subject. That is not predicate in both. So the author's classification differs from standard Western logic. The source says: 'فها هنا ثلاثة معانٍ: الباري، والمؤلف، والجسم، والمتكرّر في المقدمتين هو المؤلفُ، فهوَ العلَّةُ، وتراه خبراً في المقدمتين، غير مبتدأ به' — 'So here there are three meanings: the Creator, the composite, and the body. The repeated term in the two premises is composite, so it is the cause, and you see it as predicate in both premises, not as subject.' This is puzzling because in the second premise it appears as subject. Perhaps the author considers 'كل مؤلف' as a universal quantifier and treats the predicate position after the copula? Or there is a different analysis. Given the confusion, we will present the guided path as the author intended, and note the potential misunderstanding in common_misunderstanding. For pedagogical faithfulness, we stick to the source.
The author states the middle term is predicate in both premises, though in the second premise it appears as grammatical subject. This may reflect a different logical analysis.
3
Apply the exclusion principle
The author explains: if two things are such that what is affirmed of one is negated of the other, they are mutually exclusive (متباينان) and cannot be predicated of each other. Here, composition is affirmed of body and negated of the Creator, so body and Creator are exclusive.
4
Verify the conclusion follows
Since body and Creator are exclusive, we can conclude 'The Creator is not a body' (and also 'The body is not the Creator'). Check that the conclusion is valid given the premises.
The conclusion follows necessarily, confirming the validity of this syllogistic mode.

للقياس الاقتراني
النظم الثاني للقياس : أن تكون العلَّة - أعني : المعنى المتكرِّر الشكل في المقدمتين - حكماً في المقدمتين : أعني : أن يكون خبراً فيهما ، ولا يكون مبتدأ في إحداهما وخبراً في الأخرى ، ولا مبتدأ فيهما جميعاً . مثالُهُ : قولنا : إِنَّ الباري تعالى ليس بجسم ؛ لأنَّ الباري ليس بمؤلف ، وكلُّ مؤلَّفٍ جسم ؛ فالباري ليس بجسم . فها هنا ثلاثةُ معانٍ : الباري ، والمؤلّف ، والجسم ، والمتكرّر في المقدمتين هو المؤلفُ ، فهوَ العلَّةُ ، وتراه خبراً في المقدمتين ، غير مبتدأ به ، بخلافِ المتكرّر في النظمِ الأَوَّلِ إذْ كان خبراً في إحداهما ، مبتداً في الأخرى . ووجه لزوم النتيجة يمكن تفهيمه مجملاً ، ومفصلاً ومحققاً : أمَّا المجملُ : فهوَ أَنَّ كلَّ شيئين ثبتَ لأحدهما ما انتفى عن الآخر فهما متباينان فلا يكون بينَهُما التقاء واتصال أي : لا يجوز أن يخبر بأحدِهِما عن الآخرِ ، فالتأليف ثابتٌ للجسم ، ومنفي عَنِ الباري ، فلا يكون بين معنى الجسم والبـاري التقاء ، فلا يُقالُ : الباري جسمٌ ، ولا يقالُ : الجسم باري .
For categorical syllogism. The second mode of syllogism: that the cause (meaning the meaning repeated in the two premises) is a predicate in both premises, i.e., it is the predicate in both of them, and it is not subject in one and predicate in the other, nor subject in both. Its example: our saying: 'Indeed the Creator is not a body because the Creator is not composite, and every composite is a body, so the Creator is not a body.' Here there are three meanings: the Creator, composite, and body. The repeated term in the two premises is composite, so it is the cause, and you see it as predicate in both premises, not as subject, contrary to the repeated term in the first mode where it was predicate in one and subject in the other. The explanation of the necessity of the conclusion can be understood in summary and in detail and with verification. As for the summary: that for any two things such that what is negated from one is affirmed of the other, then they are mutually exclusive, so there is no connection and linkage between them, meaning it is not permissible to predicate one of the other. So composition is affirmed for body and negated from the Creator, so there is no connection between the meaning of body and Creator, so it is not said: 'The body is Creator' nor 'The Creator is body.'
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage defines the second mode of categorical syllogism, also called the second figure? Note: The categorization here is based on the position of the middle term. In the first mode (nazm awwal), the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other. In the second mode (nazm thani), the middle term is predicate in both premises. The author gives an example: 'The Creator is not a body because the Creator is not composite, and every composite is a body.' This is a categorical syllogism of the second figure (Cesare) with mood EAE. The middle term 'composite' is predicate in both premises: in the first premise, it is the predicate of 'Creator' (negatively), in the second premise, it is the predicate of 'every' (affirmatively). The summary explanation of the conclusion's necessity is that if two things are such that one has what the other lacks, they are mutually exclusive, so no predicate relationship exists between them. The passage then provides the general principle: if two things are mutually exclusive in terms of a property, they cannot be predicated of each other. Note the example is problematic because it speaks of God in a way that implies possibility, which the author later critiques.

العلة
المقدمة

B) Predicate in both premises

D) Not present in both premises

Key Takeaway
The second mode of categorical syllogism is defined by the middle term being the predicate in both premises, and its validity rests on the principle of mutual exclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid second-figure categorical syllogism on a fiqh issue. For example: 'No gharar (uncertainty) transaction is valid. Every bay' al-mulamasah (selling by touch) is a gharar transaction. Therefore, no bay' al-mulamasah is valid.' Identify the middle term and verify it is predicate in both premises.
?
Analyze the given syllogism: 'No act of worship is invalid without evidence. Every innovative act is an act of worship without evidence? Actually, create another: 'Everything that changes is created. The soul does not change. Therefore, the soul is not created.' Is this second mode? Check the position of the middle term 'changes'.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Reducing a Second-Mode Syllogism to the First Mode via Premise Conversion
Author provides a better example and reduces it to first mode via conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a valid second-figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by converting the universal negative premise and verify that the converted syllogism is valid.

Core Idea
A universal negative premise converts to itself, making reduction to the first figure straightforward.

In this passage, the author demonstrates how to take a syllogism in the second figure (where the middle term is the predicate in both premises) and convert the universal negative premise to transform it into the first figure, known as al-nazm al-awwal. This is a practical skill for checking the validity of syllogisms, as the first figure is the most perspicuous. The example used is soundly Islamic: 'Every body is composite, nothing eternal is composite, therefore no body is eternal.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Look at the premises: 'Every body is composite' (All S are M) and 'No eternal is composite' (No P are M). The middle term 'composite' is predicate in both, so it's second figure.
The original conclusion is 'No body is eternal' (No S are P).
2
Locate the universal negative premise
The universal negative is 'No eternal is composite'. It is a universal negative (E proposition).
Only universal negatives can be converted simply to another universal negative.
3
Apply conversion to the universal negative
Convert 'No eternal is composite' to 'No composite is eternal'. Remember: universal negative converts to itself (E→E).
This step follows the rule: 'al-nafiya al-amma tan'akis mithl nafsiha'.
4
Reconstruct the syllogism
Now we have: 'Every body is composite' (All S are M) and 'No composite is eternal' (No M are P). The middle term 'composite' is now subject in one premise and predicate in the other, making it first figure (Celarent).
The first figure has the middle term as subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor.
5
Derive the conclusion
From the first-figure premises, the conclusion is 'No body is eternal' (No S are P). This is the same as the original conclusion, showing the conversion is valid.
The first-figure syllogism Celarent (EAE) is always valid.

Source Text

وأما بالتحقيق والتفصيل : فينبغي أن يرد إلى النظم الأوَّلِ بعكس المقدمةِ النافيةِ . ولنعدل إلى مثالٍ آخر تصاوناً عن ترديدِ لفظ ( الباري ) فإنَّ قول القائل : ( إِنَّهُ ليس بجسم ) كأنَّهُ سوء أدب ، كما أنَّ قولَهُ : ( هو جسمٌ ) كفر ، فإِنَّ مَنْ قالَ للملكِ : إِنَّهُ ليس بحجَّامِ ولا بحائك .. فقد أساء الأدبَ ؛ إذْ أوهم إمكانَ ما صَرَّحَ بنفيهِ ؛ إذْ لا يُتعرَّضُ إِلَّا لنفي ما لهُ إمكان في العادة ، والجسميَّة أشدُّ استحالة في ذاتِهِ - تعالى عن قول الزائغينَ - مِنَ الحياكة والحجامة في المَلِكِ . فنقولُ مثلاً : إِنَّ الأجسام ليسَتْ أزليَّةٌ ؛ إِذْ كلُّ جسم مؤلف ، ولا أزلي واحد مؤلَّفٌ ؛ فيلزمُ منه : لا جسم واحد أزليٌّ ؛ إِذْ صَارَ المؤلَّف ثابتاً للجسم ، مسلوباً عن الأزلي ، ولا يبقى بين الأزلي والجسم ارتباط الخبر والمخبر عنه . وتفصيلُهُ : بأن تعكس المقدمة النافية ؛ فإنَّها نافية عامَّةٌ ، وقد قدَّمنا أنَّ النافية العامَّةَ تنعكس مثل نفسها ، فإذا صدق قولنا : ( ولا أزليَّ واحد مؤلَّفٌ ) .. صدق قولنا : ( ولا مؤلَّفَ واحد أزليّ ) وهو عكسُهُ ، فنضيف إليه قولنا : ( وكلُّ جسم مؤلف ) .
As for the verification and detail: it must be reduced to the first mode by converting the negative premise. And let us turn to another example to avoid repeating the word 'al-Bari'. For the saying of the speaker: 'Indeed He is not a body' is like bad manners, just as his saying 'He is a body' is disbelief. For whoever says to a king: 'Indeed he is not a cupper nor a weaver' has indeed behaved rudely, because it suggests the possibility of what he explicitly negated, since one only addresses the negation of something that has possibility in normal circumstances. And corporeality is more intensely impossible in His essence (exalted above the saying of the deviants) than weaving and cupping in the king. So we say for example: 'Indeed bodies are not eternal, because every body is composite, and no eternal one is composite.' Hence it follows: 'No body is eternal.' For composition is affirmed for body and denied of the eternal, and there remains no connection between the eternal and body of predicate and subject. Its detail: by converting the negative premise; for it is a universal negative, and we have previously stated that the universal negative converts like itself. So if our statement: 'No eternal one is composite' is true, then our statement: 'No composite one is eternal' is true, which is its converse. Then we add to it our statement: 'And every body is composite.'

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining the detailed verification of the necessity of the conclusion in the second mode: it must be reduced to the first mode by converting the negative premise. The author then critiques the earlier example about the Creator, arguing that saying 'He is not a body' is inappropriate because it implies the possibility of being a body, akin to saying a king is not a cupper or weaver—such statements are rude because they suggest that such things could be possible. Since corporeality is impossible for God, one should not even mention it. Therefore, the author provides a better example: 'Bodies are not eternal, because every body is composite, and nothing eternal is composite.' This syllogism is valid and avoids the theological impropriety. Then the author explains the reduction: the universal negative premise 'No eternal is composite' converts to 'No composite is eternal' (universal negative). Then we can combine this with the second premise 'Every body is composite' to form a first-mode syllogism (Barbara? Actually it becomes: Every body is composite, No composite is eternal, therefore No body is eternal — which is first mode? No, that's actually first figure? In the first figure, the middle term is subject in the first premise and predicate in the second? Here the middle term 'composite' is predicate in 'Every body is composite' and subject in 'No composite is eternal', so that is first figure? Actually first figure requires the middle term to be subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor? Wait, standard first figure: middle term subject in major, predicate in minor. Here major premise is 'No composite is eternal' (universal negative, middle term 'composite' as subject), minor premise is 'Every body is composite' (universal affirmative, middle term 'composite' as predicate). That is indeed first figure (Celarent: No M is P, All S is M, so No S is P). So reduction works.

Choosing Theologically Appropriate Examples in Logical Arguments
Author critiques the earlier example about the Creator due to theological impropriety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether an example used in a logical argument violates theological adab and replace it with a suitable alternative.

Core Idea
Avoid examples that imply possibility of the impossible regarding Allah.

The author criticizes an earlier example that said 'Allah is not a body', arguing that such a statement, while true, implies that being a body is a conceivable possibility, which is not the case for Allah. This lesson teaches the principle of adab in example selection, drawing on the analogy of not saying a king is not a cupper. By choosing examples that avoid even the suggestion of imperfection in divine attributes, we uphold proper theological respect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic example
The example 'Allah is not a body' is singled out. Though logically true, it is considered inappropriate because of what it implies.
The author compares it to saying a king is not a cupper or weaver.
2
Analyze what it implies
By negating a predicate, one suggests that the predicate could possibly apply. Since corporeality is impossible for Allah, negating it introduces a false implication that it was possible.
This is a violation of theological adab (good manners or respect).
3
Compare with the king analogy
The author gives the analogy: saying a king is not a cupper or weaver is rude because it suggests the king could have been such a low profession. Similarly, saying Allah is not a body suggests corporeality could be possible.
The analogy helps make the point concrete.
4
Select a theologically safe example
The author offers a better example: 'Bodies are not eternal'. Here, the subject (bodies) does not involve divine attributes, so no theological impropriety arises.
The new example still serves the logical purpose without offending.
5
Apply the principle to other contexts
When constructing logical arguments, ensure that the terms used do not imply possibility of the impossible for sacred entities. Prefer examples about contingent beings.
This principle extends to all theological discussions.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Why the Second Figure Only Yields Negative Conclusions
Author explains why second figure only yields negative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a second-figure syllogism is valid by checking that the premises differ in affirmation/negation and that the conclusion is negative.

Core Idea
The second figure needs one negative premise to yield a negative conclusion; two affirmatives give nothing.

You have learned the structure of categorical syllogisms. Now we explore a special property of the second figure: it only produces negative conclusions. The classical logicians discovered this by analyzing the position of the middle term. In this lesson, we will walk through the reasoning behind this restriction and see how to reduce a second-figure syllogism to the more powerful first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure structure
Recall that in the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. In the author's example: 'Every body is composed' (middle: 'composed' as predicate) and 'No eternal thing is composed' (middle: 'composed' as predicate). The conclusion must link the remaining terms 'body' and 'eternal'.
The middle term is 'composed' – check its distribution if needed.
2
Check the premises for quality
The author states that for a conclusion to follow, the two premises must differ in quality: one affirmative, one negative. In the example, 'Every body is composed' is affirmative, and 'No eternal thing is composed' is negative. If both were affirmative (e.g., 'Every blackness is a color', 'Every whiteness is a color'), no conclusion follows.
The reason: the middle term is not distributed in affirmative premises (both are affirmative with predicate undistributed).
3
Predict the conclusion
When premises differ, the conclusion must be negative. The author's example yields 'No body is eternal'. Confirm that the conclusion is negative and that it follows necessarily.
The negative premise 'No eternal thing is composed' is universal, so the conclusion is also universal negative.
4
Convert the negative premise to first figure
The author shows that a universal negative premise converts to itself: 'No eternal thing is composed' becomes 'No composed thing is eternal'. Adding the other premise 'Every body is composed' gives a first-figure syllogism (Barbara or Celarent). This reduction proves the necessity of the conclusion.
Conversion of universal negative is a powerful tool for reducing figures.
5
Summarize the characteristic
The second figure's unique property: it only produces negative conclusions, and only when premises differ in quality. The first figure is more complete because it can produce all four proposition types.
This shows why logicians rank the first figure as the perfect syllogism.

فلا يُقالُ : الباري جسمٌ ، ولا يقالُ : الجسم باري . وأما بالتحقيق والتفصيل : فينبغي أن يرد إلى النظم الأوَّلِ بعكس المقدّمةِ النافيةِ . ولنعدل إلى مثالٍ آخر تصاوناً عن ترديدِ لفظ ( الباري ) فإنَّ قول القائل : ( إِنَّهُ ليس بجسم ) كأنَّهُ سوء أدب ، كما أنَّ قولَهُ : ( هو جسمٌ ) كفر ، فإِنَّ مَنْ قالَ للملكِ : إِنَّهُ ليس بحجَّامِ ولا بحائك .. فقد أساء الأدبَ ؛ إذْ أوهم إمكانَ ما صَرَّحَ بنفيهِ ؛ إذْ لا يُتعرَّضُ إِلَّا لنفي ما لهُ إمكان في العادة ، والجسميَّة أشدُّ استحالة في ذاتِهِ - تعالى عن قول الزائغينَ - مِنَ الحياكة والحجامة في المَلِكِ . فنقولُ مثلاً : إِنَّ الأجسام ليسَتْ أزليَّةٌ ؛ إِذْ كلُّ جسم مؤلف ، ولا أزلي واحد مؤلَّفٌ ؛ فيلزمُ منه : لا جسم واحد أزليٌّ ؛ إِذْ صَارَ المؤلَّف ثابتاً للجسم ، مسلوباً عن الأزلي ، ولا يبقى بين الأزلي والجسم ارتباط الخبر والمخبر عنه . وتفصيلُهُ : بأن تعكس المقدمة النافية ؛ فإنَّها نافية عامَّةٌ ، وقد قدَّمنا أنَّ النافية العامَّةَ تنعكس مثل نفسها ، فإذا صدق قولنا : ( ولا أزليَّ واحد مؤلَّفٌ ) .. صدق قولنا : ( ولا مؤلَّفَ واحد أزليّ ) وهو عكسُهُ ، فنضيف إليه قولنا : ( وكلُّ جسم مؤلف ) فيعود إلى النظم الأوَّلِ ، فيكون وجه دلالته ولزوم نتيجته ما تقدم . وخاصيَّةُ هذا النظم : أَنَّهُ لا ينتجُ إِلَّا القضيَّةَ النافيةَ ، أَمَّا الإثبات .. فلا ، وأمَّا النظمُ الأوَّلُ .. فهو أكمل ؛ لأنَّه ينتج القضايا الأربعةَ ؛ أعني : المثبتة العامَّةَ ، والمثبتة الخاصة ، والنافية العامةَ ، والنافية الخاصَّةَ ، ولم نفرد تفصيل هذه الآحاد في النظم الأَوَّلِ استثقالاً للتطويل . فإن قلت : فلمَ لا ينتج هذا النظم الإثبات ؟ فاعلم أنَّ هذا لا ينتجُ إلا النفي ، ومِنْ شرطِهِ : أنْ تختلف المقدمتانِ أيضاً في النفْيِ والإثباتِ ، فإن كانتا مثبتتين .. لم ينتجا ؛ لأنَّ حاصل هذا النظم يرجعُ إلى الحكم بشيء واحد على شيئين ، وليسَ مِنْ ضرورةِ كلّ شيئين يحكم عليهما بشيء واحدٍ أن يخبر بأحدِهِما عن الآخر، فإنَّا نحكم على السوادِ والبياض جميعاً باللونيَّةِ ، ولا يلزم أن يُخبَر عن السوادِ بأنَّهُ بياض ، ولا عن البياض بأنه سواد . ونظمُ القياس فيهِ : أن يُقالَ : كلُّ سوادٍ لون ، وكلُّ بياض لون ؛ فكلُّ سوادٍ بياضُ ، أو : كلُّ بياض سوادٌ ! فاللون هو المتكرّرُ ، وقدْ جُعِلَ خبراً في المقدمتينِ ، ولمْ ينتج بين المعنيين الآخرين لا اتصالاً ولا انفصالاً.
So it is not said: 'The Creator is a body', nor is it said: 'The body is the Creator'. As for the detailed investigation: it should be reduced to the first form by converting the negative premise. Let us turn to another example to avoid repeating the word 'al-Bari', for the statement of someone: 'He is not a body' is like bad manners, just as his statement: 'He is a body' is disbelief. For if someone says to the king: 'He is not a cupper nor a weaver', he has been disrespectful, because he implies the possibility of what he explicitly negates, since one only addresses the negation of what is normally possible, and corporeality is more impossible in His essence – exalted above the sayings of the deviators – than weaving and cupping for the king. So we say, for example: 'Bodies are not eternal; for every body is composed, and no eternal thing is composed.' Hence it follows: 'No body is eternal'; since 'composed' is affirmed of body and denied of the eternal, and there remains no relation of predicate and subject between the eternal and the body. Its detailed explanation: by converting the negative premise; for it is a universal negative, and we have already stated that the universal negative converts to itself. So if our statement: 'No eternal thing is composed' is true, then our statement: 'No composed thing is eternal' is true, which is its converse. Then we add to it our statement: 'Every body is composed', and it returns to the first form, so the manner of its implication and the necessity of its conclusion are as previously. The characteristic of this form is that it only produces a negative proposition; as for affirmation, no. As for the first form, it is more complete because it produces four propositions: namely, universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. We did not detail these individual cases in the first form out of reluctance to lengthen the discussion. If you say: 'Why does this form not produce affirmation?' Know that this produces only negation, and part of its condition is that the two premises also differ in negation and affirmation. If both are affirmative, they do not produce a conclusion; because the result of this form reduces to judging one thing of two things, and it is not necessary for every two things of which one thing is judged that one of them be predicated of the other. For we judge both blackness and whiteness as being colors, yet it is not necessary that blackness be said to be whiteness, nor whiteness to be blackness. The syllogistic arrangement in it is that one says: 'Every blackness is a color, and every whiteness is a color; therefore, every blackness is whiteness, or every whiteness is blackness!' The color is the repeated term, and it was made predicate in both premises, and no connection or disconnection is produced between the other two terms.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage explains a property of the second figure of the categorical syllogism: it only yields negative conclusions. The author begins with a caution about using the term 'al-Bari' (the Creator) in logical propositions to avoid disrespect or blasphemy. He then presents an example: 'Bodies are not eternal; every body is composed, and no eternal thing is composed.' He shows that this argument can be reduced to the first figure by converting the negative premise. The conversion of a universal negative ('No eternal is composed') yields another universal negative ('No composed is eternal'). Adding the second premise 'Every body is composed' gives a first-figure syllogism with a negative conclusion. The distinctive feature of the second figure is that it only produces negative conclusions. The author explains that if both premises are affirmative, no conclusion follows. He illustrates this with the example: 'Every blackness is a color, every whiteness is a color.' The middle term 'color' is predicate in both premises, but one cannot conclude 'Every blackness is whiteness' or vice versa, because there is no necessary connection between blackness and whiteness merely because they share the predicate 'color'. Thus, the second figure requires that the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) to produce a conclusion, and that conclusion will always be negative. The first figure is more powerful because it can produce all four types of propositions.

النافية
المقدمة النافية

The second figure always yields a negative conclusion because the middle term is predicate in both premises, requiring one premise to be negative to avoid the fallacy of undistributed middle.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Why the Second Figure Only Produces Negative Conclusions
Author explains why second figure only yields negative conclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure of categorical syllogism and explain why it cannot yield affirmative conclusions.

Core Idea
The second figure's structure forces a negative conclusion because the middle term is predicate in both premises.

The second figure of categorical syllogism has a unique property: it only yields negative conclusions. Unlike the first figure, which can produce all four types of categorical propositions, the second figure is limited to negation. Why? Because the middle term is the predicate in both premises. If both premises were affirmative, we cannot infer any relation between the subjects, as the author illustrates with the example of blackness and whiteness. For a valid conclusion, one premise must be negative, and the result is always a negative proposition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the second figure structure
The second figure is defined by the middle term being the predicate in both premises. For example, in 'Every A is B' and 'No C is B', the middle term 'B' is predicate in both.
2
Test with two affirmative premises
If both premises are affirmative, e.g., 'Every blackness is a color' and 'Every whiteness is a color', no valid conclusion follows. The middle term being predicated of both subjects does not connect the subjects themselves.
The author shows this invalid inference: 'Every blackness is whiteness' or vice versa.
3
Identify the necessary condition for validity
For a valid second figure syllogism, the premises must differ in quality: one affirmative and one negative. This condition ensures that the subjects are related through negation.
If one premise affirms a predicate of one subject and the other denies it of another, separation (negation) follows.
4
Understand the proof via conversion
To see why a negative conclusion follows, convert the negative premise to obtain a first-figure syllogism. For example, from 'No C is B' we get 'No B is C' (simple conversion). Then 'Every A is B, No B is C' yields 'No A is C' in the first figure (Celarent).
This shows that the second figure's validity depends on reduction to the first figure.

وخاصيَّةُ هذا النظم : : أَنَّهُ لا ينتجُ إِلَّا القضيَّةَ النافيةَ ، أَمَّا الشكل الثاني لا الإثبات .. فلا ، وأمَّا النظمُ الأوَّلُ .. فهو أكمل ؛ لأنَّه ينتج القضايا الأربعةَ ؛ أعني : المثبتة العامَّةَ ، والمثبتة الخاصة ، والنافية العامةَ ، والنافية الخاصَّةَ ، ولم نفرد تفصيل هذه الآحاد في النظم الأَوَّلِ استثقالاً للتطويل .
فإن قلت : فلمَ لا ينتج هذا النظم الإثبات ؟
فاعلم أنَّ هذا لا ينتجُ إلا النفي ، ومِنْ شرطِهِ : أنْ تختلف المقدمتانِ أيضاً في النفْيِ والإثباتِ ، فإن كانتا مثبتتين .. لم ينتجا ؛ لأنَّ حاصل هذا النظم يرجعُ إلى الحكم بشيء واحد على شيئين ، وليسَ مِنْ ضرورةِ كلّ شيئين يحكم عليهما بشيء واحدٍ أن يخبر بأحدِهِما عن الآخر، فإنَّا نحكم على السوادِ والبياض جميعاً باللونيَّةِ ، ولا يلزم أن يُخبَر عن السوادِ بأنَّهُ بياض ، ولا عن البياض بأنه سواد .
ونظمُ القياس فيهِ : أن يُقالَ : كلُّ سوادٍ لون ، وكلُّ بياض لون ؛ فكلُّ سوادٍ بياضُ ، أو : كلُّ بياض سوادٌ ! فاللون هو المتكرّرُ ، وقدْ جُعِلَ خبراً في المقدمتينِ ، ولمْ ينتج بين المعنيين الآخرين لا اتصالاً ولا انفصالاً.
نعم ؛ كلُّ شيئين أُخبر عن أحدِهِما بما يُخبرُ عَنِ الآخرِ بنفيه .. يجب أن يكونَ بينَهُما انفصال ، وهو النفْي ، وبرهانه عكس المقدمة النافية؛ ليعود إلى النظم الأوَّلِ .
And the characteristic of this figure is that it only produces the negative proposition. As for the second figure, it does not produce affirmation at all. And as for the first figure, it is more perfect because it produces the four propositions: universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. We did not detail these individual cases in the first figure to avoid prolixity. If you say: Why does this figure not produce affirmation? Know that this [figure] only produces negation, and one of its conditions is that the two premises also differ in negation and affirmation. If both are affirmative, they do not produce [a conclusion] because the essence of this figure reduces to judging one thing of two things, and it is not necessary for every two things judged by one thing that one of them be reported of the other. For we judge both blackness and whiteness by color-ness, but it does not necessarily follow that blackness is reported as whiteness or whiteness as blackness. The form of the syllogism in this [figure] is to say: Every blackness is a color, and every whiteness is a color; therefore every blackness is whiteness, or every whiteness is blackness! The color is the repeated term, and it was made the predicate in both premises, but it produced neither connection nor separation between the other two concepts. Yes, every two things of which one is reported by what the other is reported by its negation, there must be separation between them, which is negation. Its proof is the conversion of the negative premise to return to the first figure.

Commentary
This passage explains the characteristic of the second figure of categorical syllogism (النظم الثاني). The author states that this figure only yields negative conclusions, while the first figure yields all four types of categorical propositions (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative). The author then addresses a potential question: why can't the second figure produce affirmative conclusions? The answer: the second figure's structure involves two premises where the middle term is the predicate in both. For a valid conclusion, one premise must be negative. If both premises were affirmative, then we would have a situation like 'Every blackness is a color' and 'Every whiteness is a color'; from these we cannot validly infer any relation between blackness and whiteness because they are two different things that share the same predicate. In other words, the middle term being predicated of both subjects does not establish a link between the subjects themselves. The conclusion must therefore be negative when the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The author gives an example: if one premise says something is predicated of one thing and the other premise denies it of another, then negation results. The proof of this is by converting the negative premise to obtain a first-figure syllogism, which is the canonical form.

Third figure yields particular conclusions
Author explains the third figure's structure and its particular conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure of categorical syllogism and explain why it only yields particular conclusions.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises, forcing only particular conclusions.

The third figure of categorical syllogism has a unique property: its conclusion is always particular, never universal. In this lesson, you will learn its structure and why this limitation holds, using examples from Islamic fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term's position
In the third figure, the middle term (called al-illa here) is the subject in both premises. Find it in the examples: in 'Every blackness is an accident, every blackness is a color', the middle term is 'blackness' (subject in both).
2
Recognize the conclusion's quantity
The conclusion is always particular (some S is P). In the first example, the conclusion is 'some accident is a color'—not 'every accident is a color'.
3
Apply conversion to verify
The author suggests converting one premise to see why a universal conclusion is impossible. In the wheat example, convert 'Every wheat is edible' to 'Some edible is wheat', then combine with 'Every wheat is ribawi' to get 'Some edible is ribawi' via first figure.
Conversion changes the premise from universal affirmative (A) to particular affirmative (I), which is valid.
4
Check the syllogism's validity
The third figure with two universal affirmative premises (AA) yields a particular affirmative conclusion (I)—mood Darapti, valid. Confirm that the premises force a particular, not universal, conclusion.

النظم الثالث : أن تكون العلة مبتداً في المقدمتين جميعاً : فهذا إذا جُمِعَ شروطه .. كانَ منتجاً ، ولكن نتيجةً خاصةً لا عامة .
مثاله : قولنا : كلُّ سوادٍ عرض ، وكلُّ سوادٍ لون ، فيلزمُ منهُ : أَنَّ بعض العرض لون .
وكذلك إذا قلت : كلُّ بُر مطعوم ، وكلُّ بُر ربوي ؛ فيلزم منه بالضرورة : أنَّ بعض المطعوم ربوي .
قوات الحكم العام وبيان وجهِ دلالته ولزوم النتيجة منه بالإجمـــال : أنَّ الربوي لا يمنع من إثبات الحكم الخاص
والمطعوم حكمنا بهما على شيءٍ واحدٍ وهوَ البُرُّ ، فيلزمُ بالضرورة بينَهُما التقاء، وأقلُّ درجاتِ الالتقاء : أن يوجب حكماً خاصاً وإن لم يكن عاماً ، فأمكن أن يُقالَ : بعض المطعوم ربوي ، وبعضُ الربوي مطعوم ، وإن لم يمكن أن يُقالَ بمجرَّدِ هذا : كلُّ مطعوم ربوي ، أو : كل ربوي مطعوم .
وأمَّا تفصيلُ تفهيمهِ : فهو بأن تعكس المقدمة التي ذكرناها أولاً ، وهي قولنا : كلُّ بُر مطعومٌ ، وقد ذكرنا أنَّ المثبتة العامَّةَ
The third figure: that the middle term is the subject in both premises. So this, when its conditions are fulfilled, is productive, but only a particular conclusion, not a universal one. Its example: our saying: Every blackness is an accident, and every blackness is a color; therefore some accident is a color. Similarly, if you say: Every wheat is an edible thing (maṭ'ūm), and every wheat is a ribawī (usurious); therefore necessarily some edible thing is ribawī. The rules of general judgment and an explanation of the manner of its implication and the necessity of its conclusion in summary: that the ribawī does not prevent the establishment of a particular judgment. And the edible thing: we have judged both by one thing, which is wheat, so a meeting between them is necessarily implied. The least degree of meeting is that it necessitates a particular judgment, even if not a universal one. So it is possible to say: Some edible thing is ribawī, and some ribawī is edible, even if it is not possible to say solely from this: Every edible thing is ribawī, or every ribawī is edible. As for the detailed understanding of it: it is by converting the premise we mentioned first, which is our saying: Every wheat is edible, and we have mentioned that the universal affirmative...

Commentary
This passage explains the third figure of categorical syllogism (النظم الثالث). The author states that the condition of this figure is that the middle term (called here 'the cause' or العلة, meaning the term that is repeated) is the subject in both premises. When its conditions are met, it yields a conclusion, but the conclusion is particular, not universal. The author provides two examples: (1) Every blackness is an accident, every blackness is a color; therefore some accident is a color. (2) Every wheat (burr) is an edible thing (maṭ'ūm), every wheat is a ribawī (an item subject to ribā/usury); therefore some edible thing is ribawī. The author then elaborates on why the conclusion is particular: since we have judged both the edible thing and the ribawī of the same subject (wheat), they necessarily intersect, but the intersection is only partial. We cannot conclude that every edible thing is ribawī or every ribawī is edible; the most we can assert is a particular proposition. The author also mentions that the way to demonstrate this is by converting one of the premises (specifically the universal affirmative) to the first figure, implying that after conversion, we get a valid first-figure syllogism yielding a particular conclusion.

النتيجة الخاصة
العكس
العِلَّة

Key Takeaway
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion because the middle term serves as subject in both premises, forcing only partial overlap.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Third Figure of Categorical Syllogism
Author defines the third figure, gives examples, and explains its production condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure of a categorical syllogism and derive its particular conclusion.

Core Idea
The third figure only yields particular conclusions because the middle term is subject in both premises.

In this passage, the author defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term serves as the subject in both premises. Unlike the first figure, which can produce universal conclusions, the third figure is restricted to particular conclusions. The author provides examples from colors and from the Islamic legal concept of riba (usury) to illustrate this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term repeated in both premises. In the example, 'wheat' (al-burr) appears as subject in both premises.
2
Determine the figure
Check the position of the middle term. In the third figure, it is the subject in both premises.
3
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion must be particular (some X is Y), never universal. Combine the predicates of the two premises with a particular quantifier.
4
Verify the production condition
Ensure the first premise (the one containing the subject of the conclusion) is affirmative. If it is negative, no conclusion follows.

الشكل الثالث من القياس الاقتراني: نعم؛ كلُّ شيئين أُخبر عن أحدِهِما بما يُخبرُ عَنِ الآخرِ بنفيه .. يجب أن يكونَ بينَهُما انفصال، وهو النفْي، وبرهانه عكس المقدمة النافية؛ ليعود إلى النظم الأوَّلِ. النظم الثالث: أن تكون العلة مبتداً في المقدمتين جميعاً: فهذا إذا جُمِعَ شروطه .. كانَ منتجاً، ولكن نتيجةً خاصةً لا عامة. مثاله: قولنا: كلُّ سوادٍ عرض، وكلُّ سوادٍ لون، فيلزمُ منهُ: أَنَّ بعض العرض لون. وكذلك إذا قلت: كلُّ بُر مطعوم، وكلُّ بُر ربوي؛ فيلزم منه بالضرورة: أنَّ بعض المطعوم ربوي. قوات الحكم العام وبيان وجه دلالته ولزوم النتيجة منه بالإجمال: أنَّ الربوي لا يمنع من إثبات الحكم الخاص والمطعوم حكمنا بهما على شيءٍ واحدٍ وهوَ البُرُّ، فيلزمُ بالضرورة بينَهُما التقاء، وأقلُّ درجاتِ الالتقاء: أن يوجب حكماً خاصاً وإن لم يكن عاماً، فأمكن أن يُقالَ: بعض المطعوم ربوي، وبعضُ الربوي مطعوم، وإن لم يمكن أن يُقالَ بمجرَّدِ هذا: كلُّ مطعوم ربوي، أو: كل ربوي مطعوم. وأمَّا تفصيلُ تفهيمهِ: فهو بأن تعكس المقدمة التي ذكرناها أولاً، وهي قولنا: كلُّ بُر مطعومٌ، وقد ذكرنا أنَّ المثبتة العامَّةَ تنعكس مثبتةً خاصةً، فإذا صدق قولنا: ( كلُّ بُر مطعوم ) .. صدق قولنا: ( بعض المطعوم بُر) فتبقى المقدمة الثانية؛ وهي: أنَّ كلَّ بُرِ ربوي، ويرجعُ إلى النظم الأوَّلِ؛ إذ يصير المطعوم الذي هو المتكرر - مبتداً في إحدى المقدمتين، خبراً في الأخرى. وشرطُ الإنتاج في هذا النظم: أن تكون المقدمة الأولى التي فيها المحكوم عليه مثبتةً، ولا تكون نافيةً، كما شرطنا ذلك في النظمِ الأَوَّلِ، فإِنْ كانَتْ نافيةً .. لم تلزم النتيجة، ولا يضرُّ أنْ تكون خاصةً.
The third figure of the categorical syllogism: Yes; every two things of which one is informed by what is informed of the other by its negation, there must be between them separation, which is negation, and its proof is the conversion of the negative premise to return to the first arrangement. The third arrangement: that the middle term is subject in both premises: so when its conditions are gathered, it produces, but a particular conclusion, not a universal. Example: our saying: every blackness is an accident, and every blackness is a color, so it follows from it: that some accident is a color. And likewise if you say: every wheat is a foodstuff, and every wheat is ribawi, then it follows necessarily: that some foodstuff is ribawi. The strength of the general judgment and the clarification of its indication and the necessity of the conclusion from it in summary: that ribawi does not prevent establishing the particular judgment, and foodstuff – we have ruled both on one thing which is wheat, so there necessarily occurs between them a meeting, and the least degree of meeting is that it necessitates a particular judgment even if not general, so it can be said: some foodstuff is ribawi, and some ribawi is foodstuff, though it cannot be said by this alone: every foodstuff is ribawi, or: every ribawi is foodstuff. As for the detailed explanation: it is by converting the premise we mentioned first, which is our saying: every wheat is foodstuff, and we have mentioned that the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative, so if our saying (every wheat is foodstuff) is true, our saying (some foodstuff is wheat) is true, and the second premise remains: that every wheat is ribawi, and it returns to the first arrangement; because foodstuff, which is the repeated term, becomes subject in one of the premises and predicate in the other. And the condition of production in this arrangement: that the first premise which contains the subject of the conclusion be affirmative, and not negative, as we conditioned that in the first arrangement, for if it is negative, the conclusion does not follow, and it does not harm that it be particular.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by stating the third figure and gives a general principle: when two things are such that one is described by what the other is denied, there must be separation (i.e., negation). The proof is to convert the negative premise to return to the first figure. Then he describes the third arrangement: the middle term is subject in both premises. When its conditions are fulfilled, it produces a conclusion, but only a particular one, not universal. He provides two examples: one from colors and accidents, and one from wheat, foodstuff, and ribawi (usurious). He then explains in detail why the conclusion is particular: since both premises share the same subject (wheat), there is a meeting point, and the least degree of meeting yields a particular judgment. He then shows how to convert one premise to get back to the first figure. Finally, he states the condition for production in this figure: the first premise (the one containing the subject of the conclusion) must be affirmative; if negative, no conclusion follows. It does not matter if it is particular.

Key Takeaway
The third figure of categorical syllogism produces only particular conclusions, and the first premise must be affirmative.

The Syllogism
beginner

Two Conditions for a Valid Syllogism
Author states two conditions that apply to all figures for a conclusion to follow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given pair of premises satisfies the two universal conditions for syllogistic production in any figure.

Core Idea
No conclusion from two particulars or two negatives.

In this passage, the author of the Mīzān presents two conditions that apply to every valid categorical syllogism, regardless of figure. These are the first and most basic checks before applying figure-specific rules. Understanding them will prevent you from attempting to draw conclusions from impossible premise pairs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the passage, locate the two conditions: (1) among the two premises there must be at least one universal proposition (qaḍiyya 'āmma), and (2) among them there must be at least one affirmative proposition.
The author explicitly says 'so a conclusion never follows from two particulars' and 'so a conclusion does not follow from two negatives'.
2
Apply condition 1: check for universality
Examine both premises: if both are particular (juz'iyya), the pair automatically fails. Even if one is particular and the other universal, condition 1 is satisfied.
Universal includes both universal affirmative and universal negative.
3
Apply condition 2: check for affirmativeness
Examine both premises: if both are negative (sāliba), the pair fails. At least one premise must be affirmative.
Affirmative includes both universal affirmative and particular affirmative.
4
Combine both checks
Both conditions must hold simultaneously. Only premise pairs that have at least one universal and at least one affirmative can potentially yield a conclusion; violations rule out the possibility entirely.
These are necessary conditions, not sufficient; figure-specific rules must also be satisfied.
5
Test on examples from Islamic sciences
Take a real kalam or fiqh argument and test whether it meets the conditions. For instance, 'Every created thing has a creator' (universal affirmative) and 'The world is created' (particular affirmative) satisfies both. But 'Some substances are eternal' and 'Some eternal beings are immaterial' (two particulars) violates condition 1; 'No good deed is wasted' and 'No wasted deed is beneficial' (two negatives) violates condition 2.
Practicing on real examples builds familiarity with the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يشترك فيه كلُّ نظم أمران: أحدُهُما: أَنَّهُ لا بدَّ أن يكون في جملة المقدمتين قضية عامة، فلا تلزم نتيجةً مِنْ خاصَّتين ألبتةَ. والثاني: أن يكون فيهما مثبتةٌ، فلا تلزم نتيجةً مِن نافيتينِ. ولهذا مزيد شرح، لكني أظنُّكَ تهتدي إلى ذلكَ بنفسِكَ مهما ساعدَكَ الجدُّ في التأمُّل، والمثابرة على الممارسة؛ إذ عليكَ وظيفتان: إحداهما: تأمُّلُ هذه الأمور الدقيقة والأخرى: الأُنْسُ بهذه الألفاظ الغريبة؛ فإنِّي اخترعتُ أكثرها.
And what is shared among all arrangements are two things: one of them: that there must be, in the total of the two premises, a universal proposition, so a conclusion never follows from two particulars. And the second: that there be an affirmative among them, so a conclusion does not follow from two negatives. And for this there is more explanation, but I think you will be guided to that by yourself, as long as earnestness in contemplation and perseverance in practice help you; for you have two tasks: one: contemplation of these subtle matters, and the other: familiarity with these strange terms; for I have invented most of them.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents two conditions that are common to all figures of the categorical syllogism. The author states that in any syllogism, one premise must be universal; two particular premises never yield a conclusion. Secondly, one premise must be affirmative; two negative premises never yield a conclusion. He then adds that there is more explanation for this, but he believes the diligent student will deduce it with focused study and practice. He assigns the student two tasks: to contemplate these subtle matters and to become familiar with these unfamiliar terms, most of which he has coined himself.

C) No evil is good; No good is virtuous.

Key Takeaway
For any categorical syllogism, a conclusion can only follow if the premises include at least one universal and at least one affirmative proposition.
