Condition for the Third Figure
Author gives condition for third figure and demonstrates reduction to first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the requirement for the first premise in a third-figure syllogism and explain how conversion can reduce it to the first figure.

Core Idea
Third figure: first premise must be affirmative; negatives kill the conclusion.

In the third figure of the conjunctive syllogism, one specific condition governs whether a conclusion follows. The author illustrates this with a fiqh example involving barley and riba, showing how a converted premise can transform the argument into the more reliable first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third figure structure
The third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises. In the example, 'food' is the middle term: 'Every barley is food' and 'Every barley is ribawi' — here 'barley' is subject in both? Actually, the author shows conversion to make 'food' the middle term. So after conversion, the third figure becomes first. Focus on the condition: the first premise (containing the subject) must be affirmative.
2
Apply the condition to the example
The author states that the first premise, which is 'Every barley is food' (affirmative), satisfies the condition. If it were negative (e.g., 'No barley is food'), no conclusion would follow.
It does not matter if the premise is particular; e.g., 'Some barley is food' would also be acceptable as long as it is affirmative.
3
Check the reduction to the first figure
The author shows that after conversion, the middle term 'food' becomes subject in one premise and predicate in the other, transforming the third figure into the first figure, which has its own well-known conditions.
This demonstrates how figures can be interchanged through logical operations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و تنعكس مثبتةً خاصةً ، فإذا صدق قولنا : ( كلُّ بُر مطعوم ) .. صدق قولنا : ( بعض المطعوم بُر) فتبقى المقدمة الثانية ؛ وهي : أنَّ كلَّ بُرِ ربوي ، ويرجعُ إلى النظم الأوَّلِ ؛ إذ يصير المطعوم الذي هو المتكرر - مبتداً في إحدى المقدمتين ، خبراً في الأخرى. وشرطُ الإنتاج في هذا النظم : أن تكون المقدمة الأولى التي فيها المحكوم عليه مثبتةً ، ولا تكون نافيةً ، كما شرطنا ذلك في النظمِ الأَوَّلِ ، فإِنْ كانَتْ نافيةً .. لم تلزم النتيجة ، ولا يضرُّ أنْ تكون خاصةً . والذي يشترك فيه كلُّ نظم أمران : أحدُهُما : أَنَّهُ لا بدَّ أن يكون في جملة المقدمتين قضية عامة ، فلا تلزم نتيجةً مِنْ خاصَّتين ألبتةَ . والثاني : أن يكون فيهما مثبتةٌ ، فلا تلزم نتيجةً مِن نافيتينِ . ولهذا مزيد شرح ، لكني أظنُّكَ تهتدي إلى ذلكَ بنفسِكَ مهما ساعدَكَ الجدُّ في التأمُّل ، والمثابرة على الممارسة ؛ إذ عليكَ وظيفتان : إحداهما : تأمُّلُ هذه الأمور الدقيقة والأخرى : الأُنْسُ بهذه الألفاظ الغريبة ؛ فإنِّي اخترعتُ أكثرها مِنْ تلقاءِ نفسي ؛ لأنَّ الاصطلاحاتِ في هذا الفن ثلاثة : اصطلاح المتكلمينَ ، والفقهاء ، والمنطقيينَ ، ولَمْ أُوثرْ أنْ أتبع واحداً منهم فيقصر فهمُكَ عليه ، ولا تفهم اصطلاح الفريقين الآخرين ، ولكن استعملتُ مِنَ الألفاظ ما رأيتُهُ كالمتداول بينَ جميعِهِمْ ، واخترعتُ ألفاظاً لم يشتركوا في استعمالها ، حتَّى إذا فهمت المعاني بهذهِ فما تصادفه في سائر الكتب يمكنك أن تردَّهُ إليها وتطلع على مرادِهِمْ منها .
And it converts as affirmative particular; for if our statement 'Every barley is food' is true, then our statement 'Some food is barley' is true. So the second premise remains, which is that every barley is ribawi, and it returns to the first arrangement; because the food which is the repeated term becomes, being a subject in one premise, a predicate in the other. The condition of production in this arrangement is that the first premise, in which the subject is, be affirmative, and not negative, as we conditioned that in the first arrangement; if it were negative, the conclusion does not follow, and it does not harm if it is particular. And that which is common to every arrangement are two matters: one of them: it is necessary that among the two premises there be a universal proposition, for a conclusion never follows from two particulars. And the second: that there be an affirmative among them, for a conclusion does not follow from two negatives. And for this there is further explanation, but I think you will be guided to that by yourself whenever serious reflection and perseverance in practice help you; for you have two duties: one: the contemplation of these subtle matters, and the other: becoming familiar with these strange terms; for I have invented most of them on my own initiative; because the terminologies in this art are three: the terminology of the mutakallimun, the fuqaha, and the logicians, and I did not prefer to follow one of them so that your understanding would be limited to it, and you would not understand the terminology of the other two groups; rather I used from the expressions what I saw as common among all of them, and I invented expressions that they did not agree upon using, so that when you understand the meanings through these, whatever you encounter in other books you can refer to them and become aware of their intended meaning.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the third figure of the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) and then states two universal conditions for all syllogistic forms, concluding with a note on the author's terminological method.

The author begins with an example involving barley (bur) and food (mat‘um) from fiqh, illustrating conversion: if 'Every barley is food' is true, then 'Some food is barley' is true (i.e., the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative). This is a standard logical conversion. He then mentions the second premise 'Every barley is ribawi' (i.e., subject to the prohibition of usury). This premise, after conversion, joins with the first premise to form a syllogism in the first figure (the 'first arrangement') because the middle term 'food' becomes subject in one premise and predicate in the other. This is a clever demonstration of how syllogistic figures can be interchanged.

Next, he specifies the condition for production (i.e., valid inference) in 'this arrangement' – the third figure. The condition is that the first premise (the one containing the subject) must be affirmative. If it is negative, no conclusion follows. Importantly, it does not matter if that premise is particular; particular affirmative is acceptable. This refines the rules specific to the third figure.

The author then gives two general conditions common to every figure of the conjunctive syllogism. First: one of the two premises must be universal (a general proposition). Two particular premises never yield a conclusion. Second: one of the premises must be affirmative. Two negative premises produce no conclusion. These are the well-known rules for categorical syllogisms: ex mere particularibus nihil sequitur, and ex mere negativis nihil sequitur. The text thus guards against the common mistake of thinking that two particulars or two negatives can be valid.

He notes that there is more detailed explanation possible but trusts the diligent reader to grasp it through contemplation and practice. He assigns the student two duties: careful reflection on these subtle matters and habituation to the strange vocabulary. This leads to his justification for inventing many terms. Since there are three distinct terminological traditions in Islamic logic – those of the mutakallimun, the fuqaha, and the logicians – he did not want to follow one exclusively, which would limit the reader's understanding of the other two. Instead, he used expressions that are common across all groups and invented new terms where they did not share a term. This approach ensures that once the reader understands the meanings through these terms, they can interpret any other books by relating the terms back to these concepts.

Key Terms
نظم
مثبتة
خاصة
قضية عامة
اصطلاحات

C) It must be negative.
D) It must be particular.

Key Takeaway
The third figure requires the first premise to be affirmative; particular affirmatives are acceptable, negatives invalidate the conclusion.

General Conditions and the Author's Terminological Method
Author states two universal rules for all conjunctive syllogisms and explains his terminological methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two universal conditions for any valid conjunctive syllogism (one premise must be universal, one must be affirmative) and explain why the author invented his own terms.

Core Idea
No conclusion from two particulars or two negatives; the author's terms free you from sectarian jargon.

Every valid conjunctive syllogism, regardless of figure, must satisfy two general conditions: at least one premise must be universal, and at least one must be affirmative. The author also reveals his terminological strategy: by blending the vocabularies of the mutakallimun, fuqaha, and logicians, he ensures readers can navigate any text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first general condition
The author states: 'it is necessary that among the two premises there be a universal proposition, for a conclusion never follows from two particulars.' This is the first condition.
Universal means covering all individuals (e.g., 'all', 'no'). Particular means 'some' or 'some not'.
2
Identify the second general condition
The second condition: 'there be an affirmative among them, for a conclusion does not follow from two negatives.' At least one premise must be affirmative.
A negative premise denies something. Two negatives do not connect the terms sufficiently to produce a necessary conclusion.
3
Test these conditions on examples
Take two particulars: 'Some animals are dogs' and 'Some dogs are black'. No universal premise, so no conclusion. Take two negatives: 'No wine is halal' and 'Some drinks are not wine'. No affirmative, so no conclusion. The conditions are easily verified.
These rules are foundational in Aristotle's system and are preserved in Islamic logic.
4
Understand the author's terminological method
The author notes that there are three terminological systems: those of theologians (mutakallimun), jurists (fuqaha), and logicians. Rather than follow one, he uses common terms and invents new ones where needed. This ensures the reader can understand any of the three systems.
The author's goal is pedagogical: to make the student self-sufficient in reading diverse works.

And it converts as affirmative particular; for if our statement 'Every barley is food' is true, then our statement 'Some food is barley' is true. So the second premise remains, which is that every barley is ribawi, and it returns to the first arrangement; because the food which is the repeated term becomes, being a subject in one premise, a predicate in the other. The condition of production in this arrangement is that the first premise, in which the subject is, be affirmative, and not negative, as we conditioned that in the first arrangement; if it were negative, the conclusion does not follow, and it does not harm if it is particular. And that which is common to every arrangement are two matters: one of them: it is necessary that among the two premises there be a universal proposition, for a conclusion never follows from two particulars. And the second: that there be an affirmative among them, for a conclusion does not follow from two negatives. And for this there is further explanation, but I think you will be guided to that by yourself whenever serious reflection and perseverance in practice help you; for you have two duties: one: the contemplation of these subtle matters, and the other: becoming familiar with these strange terms; for I have invented most of them on my own initiative; because the terminologies in this art are three: the terminology of the mutakallimun, the fuqaha, and the logicians, and I did not prefer to follow one of them so that your understanding would be limited to it, and you would not understand the terminology of the other two groups; rather I used from the expressions what I saw as common among all of them, and I invented expressions that they did not agree upon using, so that when you understand the meanings through these, whatever you encounter in other books you can refer to them and become aware of their intended meaning.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the third figure of the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) and then states two universal conditions for all syllogistic forms, concluding with a note on the author's terminological method.

The author begins with an example involving barley (bur) and food (mat‘um) from fiqh, illustrating conversion: if 'Every barley is food' is true, then 'Some food is barley' is true (i.e., the universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative). This is a standard logical conversion. He then mentions the second premise 'Every barley is ribawi' (i.e., subject to the prohibition of usury). This premise, after conversion, joins with the first premise to form a syllogism in the first figure (the 'first arrangement') because the middle term 'food' becomes subject in one premise and predicate in the other. This is a clever demonstration of how syllogistic figures can be interchanged.

Next, he specifies the condition for production (i.e., valid inference) in 'this arrangement' – the third figure. The condition is that the first premise (the one containing the subject) must be affirmative. If it is negative, no conclusion follows. Importantly, it does not matter if that premise is particular; particular affirmative is acceptable. This refines the rules specific to the third figure.

The author then gives two general conditions common to every figure of the conjunctive syllogism. First: one of the two premises must be universal (a general proposition). Two particular premises never yield a conclusion. Second: one of the premises must be affirmative. Two negative premises produce no conclusion. These are the well-known rules for categorical syllogisms: ex mere particularibus nihil sequitur, and ex mere negativis nihil sequitur. The text thus guards against the common mistake of thinking that two particulars or two negatives can be valid.

He notes that there is more detailed explanation possible but trusts the diligent reader to grasp it through contemplation and practice. He assigns the student two duties: careful reflection on these subtle matters and habituation to the strange vocabulary. This leads to his justification for inventing many terms. Since there are three distinct terminological traditions in Islamic logic – those of the mutakallimun, the fuqaha, and the logicians – he did not want to follow one exclusively, which would limit the reader's understanding of the other two. Instead, he used expressions that are common across all groups and invented new terms where they did not share a term. This approach ensures that once the reader understands the meanings through these terms, they can interpret any other books by relating the terms back to these concepts.

مثبتة
خاصة
قضية عامة
اصطلاحات

Conditional Syllogism: The Pattern of Entailment
Author defines the conditional syllogism and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in a conditional syllogism and verify whether the conclusion follows via modus ponens.

Core Idea
The conditional syllogism deduces a consequent by affirming the antecedent, not by invoking a cause.

The author now introduces the second pattern of qiyas, which he calls 'namat al-talazum' (pattern of entailment). This is the conditional syllogism familiar to logicians. Unlike the first pattern that involved a cause and ruling, this pattern works by affirming or negating one part of a conditional statement to deduce the other. Let us walk through the structure using his examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the first premise: it is a conditional statement containing two sub-propositions linked by 'if...then'. In the author's example: 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator.'
This premise is called 'al-sharti al-muttasil' (conjunctive conditional).
2
Separate antecedent and consequent
Remove the conditional particle to reveal two propositions: the first is the antecedent (al-muqaddam) and the second is the consequent (al-lazim). Here, antecedent = 'the world is originated', consequent = 'it has a creator'.
The author calls the consequent also 'al-tabi' (the follower).
3
Identify the second premise
The second premise affirms or negates one of the two sub-propositions. In the example, it affirms the antecedent: 'and it is known that the world is originated'.
This is the istithna' (exception) that triggers the deduction.
4
Deduce the conclusion
If the antecedent is affirmed (as here), the consequent is deduced. The conclusion is 'therefore it has a creator'. This is modus ponens. If the consequent were negated, the antecedent would be negated (modus tollens).
The author emphasizes that this pattern does not involve an 'illa (cause); it relies on entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النمط الثاني من القياس : نمط التلازم
ألا يكون فيه علةٌ وحكم ومحكوم عليه كما سبق ، بل تكونُ فيه مقدّمتانِ ، والمقدمة الأولى تشتمل على قضيتين ، والمقدمة الثانية تشتمل على ذكر إحدى تينك القضيتين الأوليين تسليماً إمَّا بالنفي أو بالإثباتِ ، حتَّى تستنتج منه إحدى تلك القضيتين أو نقيضها ، ولنسمّ هذا النمط : نمط التلازم ومثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَ العالم حادثاً .. فلهُ مُحدِثٌ ، ومعلومٌ أنَّهُ حادثُ ؛ فتلزمُ منه نتيجةً وهيَ : أَنَّ لَهُ مُحدِثاً بالضرورة . فالمقدمة الأولى : قولنا : إنْ كانَ العالم حادثاً .. فله محدِثٌ ، وهما قضيتانِ إِنْ حُذِفَ قولنا : ( إِنْ كانَ ) :
إحداهما : قولنا : ( العالم حادث ) ولنسمه المقدَّمَ .
والثانيةُ : قولنا : ( فلهُ مُحدِثٌ ) ولنسمِّهِ اللازم أو التابع
والمقدمة الثانية اشتملَتْ على تسليم عين القضيَّة التي سميناها مقدماً ، وهو قولنا : ( ومعلوم أن العالم حادث ) فتلزم منه نتيجة ، وهيَ : ( أنَّ للعالمِ مُحدِثاً ) ، وهو عين اللازم ومثاله في الفقه : قولنا : إنْ كانَ الوِتر يؤدَّى على الراحلة بكل مثال فقهي للشرطي
وهو المسمَّى عند عامة المنطقيين بـ ( الشرطي المتصل ) ، فلا يخلو هذا القياس عن حرف شرط أو ما يؤدي معناه من الشرطية الاتصالية
The second pattern of qiyas: the pattern of entailment. It does not contain a cause, ruling, and subject as previously; rather, it contains two premises. The first premise includes two propositions. The second premise includes the mention of one of those two first propositions, affirming it either by negation or affirmation, so that one of those two propositions or its contradictory is deduced. Let us name this pattern: the pattern of entailment. Its example: our saying: 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator,' and it is known that it is originated; so a result follows from it, which is: that it necessarily has a creator. So the first premise: our saying: 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator'—these are two propositions if our saying 'If...' is removed: one of them: our saying 'the world is originated'—let us call it the antecedent; and the second: our saying 'then it has a creator'—let us call it the consequent or the follower. The second premise includes the affirmation of the very proposition we called the antecedent, which is our saying: 'and it is known that the world is originated.' So a result follows, which is: 'that the world has a creator'—and it is the very consequent. Its example in fiqh: our saying: 'If the witr prayer is performed on a riding animal...'—with every fiqh example of the conditional. It is called by the generality of logicians 'the conjunctive conditional.' So this qiyas must contain a conditional particle or what performs its meaning from the conjunctive conditional.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the second pattern of qiyas, which the author calls 'namat al-talazum' (pattern of entailment). This corresponds to what logicians term the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) of the conjunctive type (muttasil). The author contrasts it with the first pattern (not shown here) that involved 'illa (cause), hukm (ruling), and mahkum 'alayhi (subject). Here, the structure consists of two premises: the first premise is a conditional statement containing two propositions (the antecedent and consequent), and the second premise affirms or negates one of those propositions, leading to the deduction of the other or its contradictory. The author gives a theological example: 'If the world is originated, then it has a creator; and it is known that it is originated; therefore it has a creator.' The antecedent is 'the world is originated' (al-muqaddam), and the consequent is 'it has a creator' (al-lazim or al-tabi'). The second premise affirms the antecedent, so the consequent is deduced. He also gives a fiqh example: 'If witr can be performed on a riding animal...' (the example is cut off). The author notes that this type is called 'sharti muttasil' (conjunctive conditional) by most logicians, and it must include a conditional particle. This is a standard exposition of modus ponens in the Islamic logical tradition.

Definition of the Conditional Syllogism and Its Components
Author defines the conditional syllogism and explains its two valid forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent, consequent, and affirming premise in a conditional syllogism and explain its structure.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism uses a conditional major premise and an affirming or denying minor premise.

In this passage, the author introduces the second pattern of syllogism: the conditional syllogism. Unlike the categorical syllogism which has a cause, judgment, and subject, this pattern is built from a conditional statement (if...then) and a second premise that affirms one part. By understanding its parts, you will recognize the type of argument used in many theological and legal proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
Find the conditional statement (if...then) in the examples. It contains two propositions.
For example: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator.'
2
Label the antecedent and consequent
In the conditional, the part after 'if' is the antecedent; the part after 'then' is the consequent.
Antecedent: 'the world is temporally originated'; Consequent: 'it has a creator'.
3
Find the minor premise
The second premise either affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent.
Example: 'and it is known that it is temporally originated' affirms the antecedent.
4
Look for the conclusion
The conclusion follows from the two premises. It is either the consequent or the contradictory of the antecedent.
Conclusion: 'therefore it has a creator' (the consequent).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النمط الثاني من القياس : نمط التلازم ألا يكون فيه علةٌ وحكم ومحكوم عليه كما سبق ، بل تكونُ فيه مقدّمتانِ ، والمقدمة الأولى تشتمل على قضيتين ، والمقدمة الثانية تشتمل على ذكر إحدى تينك القضيتين الأوليين تسليماً إمَّا بالنفي أو بالإثباتِ ، حتَّى تستنتج منه إحدى تلك القضيتين أو نقيضها ، ولنسمّ هذا النمط : نمط التلازم ومثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَ العالم حادثاً .. فلهُ مُحدِثٌ ، ومعلوم أنَّهُ حادثُ ؛ فتلزمُ منه نتيجةً وهيَ : أَنَّ لَهُ مُحدِثاً بالضرورة . فالمقدمة الأولى : قولنا : إنْ كانَ العالم حادثاً .. فله محدِثٌ ، وهما قضيتانِ إِنْ حُذِفَ قولنا : ( إِنْ كانَ ) : إحداهما : قولنا : ( العالم حادث ) ولنسمه المقدَّمَ . والثانيةُ : قولنا : ( فلهُ مُحدِثٌ ) ولنسمِّهِ اللازم أو التابع والمقدمة الثانية اشتملَتْ على تسليم عين القضيَّة التي سميناها مقدماً ، وهو قولنا : ( ومعلوم أن العالم حادث ) فتلزم منه نتيجة ، وهيَ : ( أنَّ للعالمِ مُحدِثاً ) ، وهو عين اللازم ومثاله في الفقه : قولنا : إنْ كانَ الوِتر يؤدَّى على الراحلة بكل حال .. فهو نفل ، ومعلوم أنَّهُ يُؤدَّى على الراحلة بكلّ حالٍ ؛ فثبت أَنَّهُ نفل . وهذا النمط يتطرَّقُ إليهِ أربعُ تسليمات ؛ ينتج منها اثنان ، ولا ينتج اثنان . أمَّا المنتج .. فتسليم عين القضيَّةِ التي سميناها مقدَّماً ؛ فإنَّهُ ينتج عين اللازم . مثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَتْ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحةً .. فالمصلّي متطهر ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحة ، فيلزم أن يكون المصلّي متطهراً . ومثالُهُ مِنَ الحسِّ : قولُنا : إن كان هذا سواداً .. فهو لون ، ومعلوم أنَّه سوادٌ ؛ فهو إذاً لون . وأمَّا المنتج الآخرُ .. فهو تسليم نقيض اللازم ؛ فإِنَّهُ ينتجُ نقيض المقدَّم . ومثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَتْ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحةً .. فالمصلّي متطهر ، ومعلوم أن المصلّي غير متطهر ؛ فينتج : أنَّ الصلاة ليسَتْ صحيحة . فانظر كيف أنتج تسليم نقيض اللازم نقيض المقدَّمِ . ومثاله أيضاً : قولنا : إنْ كانَ بيعُ الغائبِ صحيحاً .. فهو يلزمُ بصريح الإلزام ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذا اللازم باطل ؛ فإِنَّهُ ليس يلزم بصريح
The second pattern of syllogism: the pattern of entailment. It does not have a cause, a judgment, and a subject of judgment as before, but rather it consists of two premises. The first premise includes two propositions, and the second premise includes mention of one of those two first propositions by way of affirmation, either by negation or affirmation, so that one of those two propositions or its contradictory is deduced. Let us name this pattern: the pattern of entailment. Its example: our saying: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator,' and it is known that it is temporally originated; so a result necessarily follows, which is that it has a creator. So the first premise: our saying: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator' – these are two propositions if we remove our saying 'if': one of them is our saying 'the world is temporally originated' – let us call it the antecedent – and the second is our saying 'it has a creator' – let us call it the consequent or follower. The second premise includes affirmation of the very proposition which we called antecedent, which is our saying 'and it is known that the world is temporally originated,' so a result follows, which is 'that the world has a creator,' and it is the very consequent. Its example in fiqh: our saying: 'If the witr prayer is performed on a riding animal in any case, then it is supererogatory,' and it is known that it is performed on a riding animal in any case; so it is established that it is supererogatory. This pattern admits four types of affirmations; two produce a conclusion and two do not produce. As for the productive: affirmation of the very proposition which we called antecedent – it produces the very consequent. Its example: our saying: 'If this prayer is valid, then the one praying is in a state of ritual purity,' and it is known that this prayer is valid, so it follows that the one praying is in a state of ritual purity. Its example from sense perception: our saying: 'If this is black, then it is a color,' and it is known that it is black; so it is therefore a color. As for the other productive: it is affirmation of the contradictory of the consequent – for it produces the contradictory of the antecedent. Its example: our saying: 'If this prayer is valid, then the one praying is in a state of ritual purity,' and it is known that the one praying is not in a state of ritual purity; so it produces: that the prayer is not valid. See how affirming the contradictory of the consequent produced the contradictory of the antecedent. Its example also: our saying: 'If selling the absent thing is valid, then it necessitates explicit obligation,' and it is known that this consequent is false; for it does not necessitate explicit obligation.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the second pattern of syllogism: the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). Unlike the first pattern (categorical syllogism) which involves a cause, judgment, and subject, this pattern consists of two premises. The first premise is a conditional statement containing two propositions: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (lazim). The second premise affirms either the antecedent (affirming the antecedent) or the contradictory of the consequent (denying the consequent), leading to the deduction of the consequent or the contradictory of the antecedent respectively. The author gives a general example using the world's creation, then an example from fiqh (witr prayer as supererogatory). He then explains that there are four possible affirmations, but only two are productive: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). The other two (denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent) are not productive. He illustrates the two productive forms with examples from prayer validity and sense perception (black/color), and from fiqh (selling absent property).

Key Terms
نمط التلازم
مقدم
لازم
تسليم

Key Takeaway
The conditional syllogism consists of a conditional premise and a premise that affirms one of its parts, leading to a necessary conclusion.

Valid and Invalid Forms of the Conditional Syllogism
Author defines the conditional syllogism and explains its two valid forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish modus ponens from modus tollens and recognize why affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are fallacies.

Core Idea
Only affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yield valid conclusions in a conditional syllogism.

The author explains that four affirmations are possible but only two produce a conclusion. This lesson will help you identify which forms are logically sound and which are not, using Islamic examples from the text. Mastering this is crucial for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
List the four possible affirmations
The four are: affirm antecedent, deny antecedent, affirm consequent, deny consequent. Only two are valid.
The author calls them 'taslimat' (affirmations).
2
Define modus ponens
Affirming the antecedent yields the consequent. Example: 'If prayer is valid, then the one praying is pure; prayer is valid; therefore he is pure.'
This is the first productive form.
3
Define modus tollens
Denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. Example: 'If prayer is valid, then the one praying is pure; he is not pure; therefore prayer is not valid.'
This is the second productive form.
4
Identify the invalid forms
Denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent do not yield necessary conclusions. For example, 'If it is a cat, it is an animal; it is not a cat; therefore it is not an animal' is invalid.
The author implies these are rejected.

النمط الثاني من القياس : نمط التلازم ألا يكون فيه علةٌ وحكم ومحكوم عليه كما سبق ، بل تكونُ فيه مقدّمتانِ ، والمقدمة الأولى تشتمل على قضيتين ، والمقدمة الثانية تشتمل على ذكر إحدى تينك القضيتين الأوليين تسليماً إمَّا بالنفي أو بالإثباتِ ، حتَّى تستنتج منه إحدى تلك القضيتين أو نقيضها ، ولنسمّ هذا النمط : نمط التلازم ومثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَ العالم حادثاً .. فلهُ مُحدِثٌ ، ومعلوم أنَّهُ حادثُ ؛ فتلزمُ منه نتيجةً وهيَ : أَنَّ لَهُ مُحدِثاً بالضرورة . فالمقدمة الأولى : قولنا : إنْ كانَ العالم حادثاً .. فله محدِثٌ ، وهما قضيتانِ إِنْ حُذِفَ قولنا : ( إِنْ كانَ ) : إحداهما : قولنا : ( العالم حادث ) ولنسمه المقدَّمَ . والثانيةُ : قولنا : ( فلهُ مُحدِثٌ ) ولنسمِّهِ اللازم أو التابع والمقدمة الثانية اشتملَتْ على تسليم عين القضيَّة التي سميناها مقدماً ، وهو قولنا : ( ومعلوم أن العالم حادث ) فتلزم منه نتيجة ، وهيَ : ( أنَّ للعالمِ مُحدِثاً ) ، وهو عين اللازم ومثاله في الفقه : قولنا : إنْ كانَ الوِتر يؤدَّى على الراحلة بكل حال .. فهو نفل ، ومعلوم أنَّهُ يُؤدَّى على الراحلة بكلّ حالٍ ؛ فثبت أَنَّهُ نفل . وهذا النمط يتطرَّقُ إليهِ أربعُ تسليمات ؛ ينتج منها اثنان ، ولا ينتج اثنان . أمَّا المنتج .. فتسليم عين القضيَّةِ التي سميناها مقدَّماً ؛ فإنَّهُ ينتج عين اللازم . مثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَتْ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحةً .. فالمصلّي متطهر ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحة ، فيلزم أن يكون المصلّي متطهراً . ومثالُهُ مِنَ الحسِّ : قولُنا : إن كان هذا سواداً .. فهو لون ، ومعلوم أنَّه سوادٌ ؛ فهو إذاً لون . وأمَّا المنتج الآخرُ .. فهو تسليم نقيض اللازم ؛ فإِنَّهُ ينتجُ نقيض المقدَّم . ومثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَتْ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحةً .. فالمصلّي متطهر ، ومعلوم أن المصلّي غير متطهر ؛ فينتج : أنَّ الصلاة ليسَتْ صحيحة . فانظر كيف أنتج تسليم نقيض اللازم نقيض المقدَّمِ . ومثاله أيضاً : قولنا : إنْ كانَ بيعُ الغائبِ صحيحاً .. فهو يلزمُ بصريح الإلزام ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذا اللازم باطل ؛ فإِنَّهُ ليس يلزم بصريح
The second pattern of syllogism: the pattern of entailment. It does not have a cause, a judgment, and a subject of judgment as before, but rather it consists of two premises. The first premise includes two propositions, and the second premise includes mention of one of those two first propositions by way of affirmation, either by negation or affirmation, so that one of those two propositions or its contradictory is deduced. Let us name this pattern: the pattern of entailment. Its example: our saying: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator,' and it is known that it is temporally originated; so a result necessarily follows, which is that it has a creator. So the first premise: our saying: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator' – these are two propositions if we remove our saying 'if': one of them is our saying 'the world is temporally originated' – let us call it the antecedent – and the second is our saying 'it has a creator' – let us call it the consequent or follower. The second premise includes affirmation of the very proposition which we called antecedent, which is our saying 'and it is known that the world is temporally originated,' so a result follows, which is 'that the world has a creator,' and it is the very consequent. Its example in fiqh: our saying: 'If the witr prayer is performed on a riding animal in any case, then it is supererogatory,' and it is known that it is performed on a riding animal in any case; so it is established that it is supererogatory. This pattern admits four types of affirmations; two produce a conclusion and two do not produce. As for the productive: affirmation of the very proposition which we called antecedent – it produces the very consequent. Its example: our saying: 'If this prayer is valid, then the one praying is in a state of ritual purity,' and it is known that this prayer is valid, so it follows that the one praying is in a state of ritual purity. Its example from sense perception: our saying: 'If this is black, then it is a color,' and it is known that it is black; so it is therefore a color. As for the other productive: it is affirmation of the contradictory of the consequent – for it produces the contradictory of the antecedent. Its example: our saying: 'If this prayer is valid, then the one praying is in a state of ritual purity,' and it is known that the one praying is not in a state of ritual purity; so it produces: that the prayer is not valid. See how affirming the contradictory of the consequent produced the contradictory of the antecedent. Its example also: our saying: 'If selling the absent thing is valid, then it necessitates explicit obligation,' and it is known that this consequent is false; for it does not necessitate explicit obligation.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the second pattern of syllogism: the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). Unlike the first pattern (categorical syllogism) which involves a cause, judgment, and subject, this pattern consists of two premises. The first premise is a conditional statement containing two propositions: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (lazim). The second premise affirms either the antecedent (affirming the antecedent) or the contradictory of the consequent (denying the consequent), leading to the deduction of the consequent or the contradictory of the antecedent respectively. The author gives a general example using the world's creation, then an example from fiqh (witr prayer as supererogatory). He then explains that there are four possible affirmations, but only two are productive: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). The other two (denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent) are not productive. He illustrates the two productive forms with examples from prayer validity and sense perception (black/color), and from fiqh (selling absent property).

Key Terms

مقدم
لازم
تسليم

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a valid conditional syllogism?
A) If it is a cat, it is an animal. It is an animal. Therefore it is a cat.
B) If it is a cat, it is an animal. It is not a cat. Therefore it is not an animal.
C) If it is a cat, it is an animal. It is a cat. Therefore it is an animal.
D) If it is a cat, it is an animal. It is not an animal. Therefore it is a cat.

Key Takeaway
The two valid forms of conditional syllogism are modus ponens (affirm antecedent to get consequent) and modus tollens (deny consequent to get denial of antecedent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the fiqh example from the passage: 'If selling the absent thing is valid, then it necessitates explicit obligation. It is known that it does not necessitate explicit obligation.' What conclusion follows? Is this a valid form? Identify which form it is (modus ponens or modus tollens).

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Affirming the Antecedent (Modus Ponens) in Conditional Syllogism
Explanation of the first productive form: affirming antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and verify a conditional syllogism that affirms the antecedent (modus ponens) using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
To derive a conclusion in a conditional, affirm the 'if' part.

In this lesson, we examine the first productive form of the connected conditional syllogism. The author demonstrates that when you admit the antecedent, the consequent necessarily follows. We will see this through examples from Islamic ritual purity and logical colour relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
Find the statement with 'if...then...' structure in the passage. Example: 'If this prayer is valid, then the prayer-goer is in a state of purity.'
2
Locate antecedent and consequent
The antecedent is the part after 'if' (prayer is valid). The consequent is the part after 'then' (prayer-goer is pure).
3
Admit the antecedent as minor premise
The minor premise affirms the antecedent: 'This prayer is valid.' This is called taslim 'ayn al-muqaddam.
4
Derive the consequent as conclusion
From the conditional and the admitted antecedent, the conclusion must be the consequent: 'Therefore, the prayer-goer is pure.'
5
Check for fallacies
Note that affirming the consequent is invalid. Only affirming antecedent yields certainty in this form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا النمط يتطرَّقُ إليهِ أربعُ تسليمات ؛ ينتج منها اثنان ، ولا ينتج اثنان . أمَّا المنتج .. فتسليم عين القضيَّةِ التي سميناها مقدَّماً ؛ فإنَّهُ ينتج عين اللازم . مثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَتْ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحةً .. فالمصلّي متطهر ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحة ، فيلزم أن يكون المصلّي متطهراً . ومثالُهُ مِنَ الحنِ : قولُنا : إن كان هذا سواداً .. فهو لون ، ومعلوم أنَّه سوادٌ ؛ فهو إذاً لون . وأمَّا المنتج الآخرُ .. فهو تسليم نقيض اللازم ؛ فإِنَّهُ ينتجُ نقيض المقدَّم . ومثاله : قولنا : إنْ كانَتْ هذهِ الصلاة صحيحةً .. فالمصلّي متطهر ، ومعلوم أن المصلّي غير متطهر ؛ فينتج : أنَّ الصلاة ليسَتْ صحيحة . فانظر كيف أنتج تسليم نقيض اللازم نقيض المقدَّمِ . ومثاله أيضاً : قولنا : إنْ كانَ بيعُ الغائبِ صحيحاً .. فهو يلزمُ بصريح الإلزام ، ومعلوم أنَّ هذا اللازم باطل ؛ فإِنَّهُ ليس يلزم بصريح الإلزام : فيلزمُ منه نقيضُ المقدَّمِ ؛ وهو أن البيعَ غير صحيح ومثاله أيضاً : قولنا : إنْ كانَ الباري تعالى على العرش .. فهوَ مقدَّرُ ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا مساو للعرش أو أصغرُ أو أكبرُ ، ومعلوم أنَّ اللازم محال ، وهوَ كونُهُ مقدَّراً ؛ فيكون المقدَّمُ محالاً . فهو محال
This pattern admits four admissions: two produce (a conclusion) and two do not. As for the one that produces: it is admission of the very proposition we called 'antecedent'; for it yields the very consequent. Example: our statement: 'If this prayer is valid, then the prayer-goer is in a state of purity (mutahhir);' and it is known that this prayer is valid, so it follows that the prayer-goer is in a state of purity. And its example from color: our statement: 'If this is blackness, then it is a color;' and it is known that it is blackness; therefore it is a color. As for the other productive one: it is admission of the contradictory of the consequent; for it yields the contradictory of the antecedent. Example: our statement: 'If this prayer is valid, then the prayer-goer is in a state of purity;' and it is known that the prayer-goer is not in a state of purity; so it yields: that the prayer is not valid. So see how admission of the contradictory of the consequent produced the contradictory of the antecedent. Also an example: our statement: 'If the sale of an absent (object) is valid, then it is binding by explicit obligation;' and it is known that this consequent is false; for it is not binding by explicit obligation; so the contradictory of the antecedent follows: that the sale is not valid. Also an example: our statement: 'If the Most High (Allah) is upon the Throne, then He is quantified (muqaddar); because He is either equal to the Throne or smaller or larger;' and it is known that the consequent is impossible, i.e., that He is quantified; so the antecedent is impossible. So it is impossible.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage explains the two productive (valid) forms of a connected conditional (qiyas shartiyy muttasil) syllogism. The author first states that there are four possible admissions (taslimat) regarding the premises, but only two yield a valid conclusion. The first productive form is affirming the antecedent (taslim 'ayn al-muqaddam). If we have a conditional 'If A then B', and we declare that A is true, then B necessarily follows. The author gives an example from Islamic prayer: 'If this prayer is valid, then the person praying is in a state of ritual purity (mutahhir).' If we admit that the prayer is indeed valid, then we conclude that the person is in a state of purity. Another example from logic of color: 'If this is blackness, then it is a color.' Admitting it is blackness yields it is a color. The second productive form is denying the consequent (taslim naqid al-lazim). Given the same conditional, if we assert that the consequent is false (naqiduhu), then the antecedent must be false. Example: If the prayer is valid, then the person is pure. Knowing the person is not pure, we conclude the prayer is not valid. Further examples from Islamic jurisprudence: sale of an absent object (bay' al-gha'ib) — if it were valid, it would be binding (lazim) by explicit obligation; but it is not binding by explicit obligation (as per consensus), so the sale is not valid. From Kalam: 'If Allah is upon the Throne, then He would be quantified (muqaddar)' — either equal to the Throne, smaller, or larger — all impossible for Allah; thus the antecedent ('upon the Throne') is impossible. The argument employs the principle that whatever leads to the impossible is itself impossible (al-mu'addi ila al-muhal muhal).

Key Terms
المقدّم
اللازم
تسليم
القياس الشرطي المتصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Admitting that the person is in a state of purity.
B) Admitting that the prayer is not valid.
C) Admitting that the prayer is valid.
D) Admitting that the person is not in a state of purity.

Key Takeaway
The first valid form of conditional syllogism is affirming the antecedent, which yields the consequent as conclusion.

Denying the Consequent (Modus Tollens) in Conditional Syllogism
Explanation of the second productive form: denying consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and verify a conditional syllogism that denies the consequent (modus tollens) using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
To disprove the antecedent, deny the consequent.

This lesson covers the second productive form of the connected conditional syllogism: denying the consequent. The author shows that if we admit the negation of the consequent, the negation of the antecedent necessarily follows. Examples from prayer, sale of an absent object, and the nature of Allah's attribute of istiwa' illustrate this form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
Use the same conditional as before: 'If this prayer is valid, then the prayer-goer is in a state of purity.'
2
Locate antecedent and consequent
Antecedent: prayer is valid. Consequent: prayer-goer is pure.
3
Admit the negation of the consequent as minor premise
The minor premise denies the consequent: 'The prayer-goer is not pure.' This is taslim naqid al-lazim.
4
Derive the negation of the antecedent as conclusion
From the conditional and the denial of the consequent, the conclusion must be the negation of the antecedent: 'Therefore, the prayer is not valid.'
5
Apply to other examples
The author provides two further examples: (1) Sale of absent object: if it were valid, it would be binding; but it is not binding, so it is not valid. (2) Allah on the Throne: if He were on the Throne, He would be quantified; but quantification is impossible for Allah, so the antecedent is impossible.

Structure of the First Pattern (Categorical Syllogism)
Author begins to distinguish conditional syllogism from categorical syllogism, but text is cut off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the structural difference between a categorical and a conditional syllogism based on the author's initial explanation.

Core Idea
Categorical syllogisms arrange a predicate (khabar) and a ruling to produce a conclusion.

The author begins to distinguish between the conditional syllogism and the categorical syllogism. Although the text is cut off, he clarifies that the first pattern (categorical) involves an arrangement: a statement (khabar) about something, followed by a ruling (hukm) upon that statement, from which a conclusion follows. This lesson examines that structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two patterns
The author contrasts the 'first pattern' (categorical) with the current one (conditional). Note the terms 'qiyas shartiyy' vs 'qiyas' (used narrowly for categorical).
2
Examine the categorical structure
The passage states that the first pattern has an arrangement: first a khabar about something, then a ruling on that khabar, leading to a conclusion.
This is a compressed description of a categorical syllogism: a subject-predicate proposition (khabar) and then applying a ruling to that proposition.
3
Recognize the incompleteness
The passage stops before explaining the conditional pattern. Understand that the conditional syllogism uses a conditional proposition as the major premise.
The fragment shows the author's method: first contrast, then detail each pattern.

Definition and Use of Ilzam (Modus Tollens)
Author defines ilzam and provides two illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate an ilzam argument (modus tollens) using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Ilzam uses the absurdity of the consequent to reject the antecedent.

Ilzam is a powerful tool in Islamic dialectic for refuting opponents. The passage defines it as deducing the contradictory of the antecedent from the falsehood of the consequent. You will learn to identify its structure and apply it to real theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
Find the 'if...then...' structure. In Arabic, look for 'in kāna... fa...'. For example: 'If the Creator is on the Throne, then He is measured.'
The conditional binds the antecedent (muqaddam) to the consequent (lāzim).
2
Extract the antecedent and consequent
Label P as the antecedent (e.g., 'the Creator is on the Throne') and Q as the consequent (e.g., 'He is measured').
Be precise: the consequent must follow logically from the antecedent.
3
Determine that the consequent is false or absurd
The author argues that the consequent is absurd (muḥāl): 'He is measured' is impossible for God. This is the key premise.
The absurdity must be established independently, often by consensus or clear reason.
4
Conclude the antecedent is false
If Q is false, then P must be false (modus tollens). The author concludes: 'so the antecedent is absurd.'
This step is the heart of ilzam: from the impossibility of the consequent, we refute the antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلزام : ؛ فيلزمُ منه نقيضُ المقدَّمِ ؛ وهو أن البيعَ غير صحيح ومثاله أيضاً : قولنا : إنْ كانَ الباري تعالى على العرش .. فهوَ مقدَّرُ ؛ لأنَّهُ إِمَّا مساو للعرش أو أصغرُ أو أكبرُ ، ومعلوم أنَّ اللازم محال ، وهوَ كونُهُ مقدَّراً ؛ فيكون المقدَّمُ محالاً . فهو محال . ووجه دلالةِ هذا : أنَّ المؤدّي إلى المحال محال ، وقولُ ما أدى إلى محال الخصم : : ( إِنَّهُ على العرش ( مؤد إلى المحال ؛ فهو محال ، وقوله : ( إِنَّ  َبيعَ الغائبِ صحيحٌ ) مؤد إلى المحال ، وهو أن يلزم بصريح الإلزامِ على خلافِ الإجماع ، والمؤدّي إلى المحال محال . فأمَّا الذي لا ينتجُ .. فهوَ تسليم عينِ اللازم ؛ فإنَّا إِذا قلنا : إنْ كانَتْ هذه الصلاة صحيحة .. فالمصلّي متطهر ، ومعلوم أنَّ المصلّي متطهّرُ ؛ فيلزمُ أنَّ الصلاة صحيحة ! وهو غير صحيح ؛ إذْ ربَّما تكونُ الصلاة باطلة بعلة أخرى سوى الطهارة . وكذلك تسليم نقيض المقدَّم لا ينتج لا عين اللازم ولا نقيضه ؛ فإِنَّكَ لو قلت : ومعلوم أنَّ الصلاة ليسَتْ بصحيحةٍ ؛ فلا يلزمُ مِنْ هذا أنَّ المصلّي متطهر ولا أَنَّهُ غير متطهر .
Ilzam: so from it follows the contradictory of the antecedent; and it is that the sale is invalid. And also an example of it: our statement: 'If the Creator is on the Throne... then He is measured; because He is either equal to the Throne, or smaller, or larger. And it is known that the consequent is absurd, which is His being measured; so the antecedent is absurd. So it is absurd.' And the proof of this is: that which leads to the absurd is absurd. And the statement of what leads to the absurd of the opponent: 'Indeed He is on the Throne' leads to the absurd; so it is absurd. And his statement: 'Indeed the sale of the absent is valid' leads to the absurd, which is that it entails by explicit ilzam contrary to consensus; and what leads to the absurd is absurd. As for that which does not produce a conclusion... it is the acknowledgement of the very consequent; for if we say: 'If this prayer is valid... then the worshiper is in a state of ritual purity,' and it is known that the worshiper is in a state of ritual purity; then it follows that the prayer is valid! But that is not correct, because the prayer may be invalid due to another cause besides ritual purity. And similarly, acknowledging the contradictory of the antecedent does not produce a conclusion, neither the very consequent nor its contradictory; for if you said: 'It is known that the prayer is not valid,' then from this it does not follow that the worshiper is in a state of ritual purity nor that he is not in a state of ritual purity.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines ilzam, which is a type of conditional syllogism where the contradictory of the antecedent follows from the falsehood of the consequent. The author gives two examples: first, the theological example that if the Creator is on the Throne, He would be measured (implying spatial dimensions), which is absurd, so the antecedent is absurd; second, a fiqh example about selling absent goods being valid leads to contradiction of consensus. The proof method is that whatever leads to absurdity is itself absurd. Then the author discusses two invalid forms: first, acknowledging the very consequent (taslim 'ayn al-lazim) – e.g., if the prayer is valid then the worshiper has wudu, and we know the worshiper has wudu, but that does not prove the prayer is valid because other invalidating factors may exist; second, acknowledging the contradictory of the antecedent (taslim naqid al-muqaddam) – e.g., if the prayer is invalid, it does not prove anything about wudu, because the conditional only asserts that validity entails wudu, not that invalidity entails lack of wudu. The key logical principle is that affirming the consequent does not prove the antecedent, and denying the antecedent does not disprove the consequent.

الإلزام
المقدَّم
اللازم
المحال

Ilzam (modus tollens) refutes a proposition by showing its necessary consequence is false or absurd.

Invalid Conditional Forms: Affirming the Consequent and Denying the Antecedent
Author identifies two invalid forms in conditional reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent are invalid in conditional arguments.

Core Idea
A true consequent does not prove the antecedent; a false antecedent does not disprove the consequent.

The passage warns against two common fallacies that appear valid but are not. Understanding these will protect you from flawed reasoning in debates and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify affirming the consequent (taslim 'ayn al-lazim)
The author gives the prayer example: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshiper has wudu.' Knowing the worshiper has wudu, one might conclude the prayer is valid. But this is invalid because validity may be hindered by other causes.
This fallacy takes the form: if P then Q, Q, therefore P.
2
Identify denying the antecedent (taslim naqid al-muqaddam)
From the same conditional, if we know the prayer is not valid, we cannot conclude anything about wudu. The author says it does not produce a conclusion.
This fallacy takes the form: if P then Q, not P, therefore not Q.
3
Understand why they are invalid using the text
The author explicitly states: 'it may be invalid due to another cause' for the first, and 'it does not follow' for the second. The conditional only guarantees that validity implies wudu, not the converse.
A conditional does not assert a biconditional relationship.
4
Apply the insight to another example
Think: 'If it is Friday, then the congregational prayer is held.' Knowing it is Friday does not guarantee that the prayer is held (there could be an emergency). Similarly, if it is not Friday, the prayer might still be held on another day? No, actually the prayer is only on Friday, so this is an interesting case. The author's point is that the conditional does not allow these inferences.
Use only Islamic examples in practice.

Source Text

Distinguishing Categorical and Conditional Syllogisms
Author distinguishes conditional from categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given syllogism is categorical or conditional based on its structure.

Core Idea
Categorical syllogisms have a shared term; conditional syllogisms show entailment between unrelated propositions.

In this passage, the author contrasts two types of syllogisms. The first (categorical) involves a predicate and subject with a middle term that can serve multiple roles. The second (conditional) simply declares that one proposition necessarily follows another, without a common term. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of syllogism
Look at the overall structure: does it have three terms (subject, predicate, middle) or just two propositions linked by 'if...then'?
2
Examine the role of the middle term
In categorical, the middle term appears in both premises; in conditional, there is no middle term.
3
Analyze the example given
The author gives 'If prayer is valid, then the worshiper has wudu.' Is there a common term? No, because 'prayer validity' and 'worshiper's wudu' are distinct subjects.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Consider 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; wine is an intoxicant; therefore wine is forbidden.' Is this categorical or conditional? Why?
It is categorical because it has a middle term (intoxicant).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين القياس الشرطي والقياس . والفرقُ بين هذا النمط والنمط الأوَّلِ : أَنَّ الأَولَ ترتيبه خبر الالا لالة عَنْ شيءٍ ، ثم حكم على الخبر بشيء ، فيلزم منه نتيجةً ، وهوَ وجه دلالة نمط التلازم الالتقاء بين المبتدأ الأوَّلِ والخبر الأخير ، وكلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأجزاء الثلاثة يصلحُ أن يُجعل وصفاً وخبراً وحُكماً للآخر في نظم هذا الكلام . وأمَّا هذا .. فإنَّهُ إظهارُ تلازم بين قضيتين غير متداخلتين ؛ فإِنَّ قولَنا : ( إنْ كانَتِ الصلاةُ صحيحة .. فالمصلّي متطهر ) بيانُ أنَّ كونَ المصلّي متطهّراً لازم لكون الصلاة صحيحة ، فلا يمكن أن يُجعل كونُ المصلّي متطهراً لا وصفاً للصلاة ، ولا وصفاً للصحة . والفرقُ مِنْ حيثُ الترتيب والنظم .. ظاهر . وأما وجه الدلالةِ : فهوَ أَنَّهُ مهما جُعِلَ شيء لازماً لشيء .. ألَّا يكونَ الملزومُ أعمَّ مِنَ اللازم ، بلْ إِمَّا أخص ، وإمَّا فينبغي مساوياً . ثبوت الأخص يلزم عنه وجود الأعمّ بالضرورة ؛ إذْ يلزمُ مِنْ ثبوتِ السوادِ ووجوده وجود اللون ، وهو الذي عنيناه بتسليم عينِ المقدَّمِ ، وانتفاء الأعمّ يوجب انتفاء الأخص بالضرورة ؛ إذْ يلزمُ مِن انتفاء اللونِ انتفاء السوادِ ، وهو الذي عنيناه بتسليم نقيض اللازم . فأمَّا ثبوتُ الأعم .. فلا يوجب ثبوت الأخص ؛ فإِنَّ ثبوت اللون ثبوت الأعم لا يلزم عنه ثبوت الأخص لا يدلُّ على ثبوتِ السوادِ ، فلذلك قلنا : تسليم عين اللازم لا ينتج لا نفي المقدَّمِ ولا ثبوته.
The difference between conditional syllogism and [categorical] syllogism. And the difference between this pattern and the first pattern is that the first one: its arrangement is a predicate of indication about something, then a ruling on the predicate with something, so a result follows from it, and it is the proof of the pattern of talazum: the meeting between the first subject and the last predicate, and each of the three parts is suitable to be made a description, predicate, and ruling for the other in the system of this speech. And as for this [pattern]... it is a showing of entailment between two non-intertwined propositions; for our statement: 'If the prayer is valid... then the worshiper is in a state of ritual purity' is a clarification that the worshiper's being in a state of ritual purity is necessary for the prayer's being valid. So it is not possible to make the worshiper's being in a state of ritual purity a description of the prayer, nor a description of validity. And the difference in terms of arrangement and system is apparent. As for the proof: it is that whenever something is made necessary for something, the necessitated thing (malzum) must not be more general than the necessary thing (lazim); rather it is either more specific or should be equal. The establishment of the more specific necessarily entails the existence of the more general; for from the establishment of blackness and its existence, the existence of color follows, and this is what we meant by acknowledging the very antecedent. And the negation of the more general necessitates the negation of the more specific necessarily; for from the negation of color, the negation of blackness follows, and this is what we meant by acknowledging the contradictory of the consequent. As for the establishment of the more general... it does not necessitate the establishment of the more specific; for the establishment of color, the more general, does not entail the establishment of the more specific; it does not indicate the establishment of blackness. Therefore we said: acknowledging the very consequent does not produce a conclusion, neither negation of the antecedent nor its affirmation.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between categorical syllogisms (qiyas hamli) and conditional syllogisms (qiyas sharti). The author explains that in a categorical syllogism, the arrangement involves a predicate (khabar) and a subject, with a middle term linking them, and each of the three terms can function as description, predicate, or judgment. In contrast, a conditional syllogism simply shows an entailment (talazum) between two independent propositions, like 'If prayer is valid, then the worshiper has wudu.' The consequent cannot be made a description of the antecedent or its validity. The author then elaborates the principle of talazum: the malzum (the thing that necessitates) must be either more specific (akhass) than, or equal to, the lazim (the necessary thing). The author uses the example of blackness and color: blackness (specific) entails color (general), so affirming blackness requires affirming color; negating color requires negating blackness. However, affirming color does not entail affirming blackness, because color is more general. This explains why acknowledging the consequent (taslim 'ayn al-lazim) is invalid: from the truth of 'the worshiper has wudu' we cannot infer the truth of 'the prayer is valid', because wudu is more general than valid prayer (i.e., there are other causes for wudu besides valid prayer). Similarly, acknowledging the contradictory of the antecedent (taslim naqid al-muqaddam) is invalid because negating the antecedent does not negate the consequent.

Key Terms
القياس الحملي
القياس الشرطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between categorical and conditional syllogisms?
A) Categorical syllogisms have a middle term; conditional do not.
B) Categorical syllogisms deal with actions; conditional deal with beliefs.
C) Conditional syllogisms have three terms; categorical have two.
D) Both are the same.

Key Takeaway
The fundamental difference lies in the presence of a common term (categorical) versus pure entailment (conditional).

?
Classify the following as categorical or conditional: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshiper is pure.' and 'The world is originated; every originated thing needs a creator; therefore the world needs a creator.'

The Principle of Talazum: Entailment between Specific and General
Author explains the entailment relationship between specific and general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine valid entailment relationships between specific and general concepts using the principle of talazum.

Core Idea
The specific entails the general, but the general does not entail any particular specific.

The author elaborates on the nature of entailment (talazum) by examining the relationship between the more specific (akhass) and the more general (a'amm). Using the example of blackness and color, he demonstrates that establishing the specific necessarily implies the general, but the converse is false. This principle is vital for evaluating arguments in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and theology (kalam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the malzum and lazim
In the example 'If prayer is valid, then the worshiper has wudu', which is the malzum (specific) and which is the lazim (general)? (Malzum: prayer validity; lazim: wudu).
2
Apply the specific-general rule
According to the author, malzum must be equal or more specific than lazim. Can you see why? (Because if malzum were more general, then it would not necessitate the specific.)
3
Test with blackness and color
Blackness (specific) entails color (general). Does color entail blackness? No, because color is general. This illustrates the invalidity of affirming the consequent.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider: 'If a person is a prophet, then he is truthful.' Is truthfulness more general or more specific than prophethood? Prophethood is more specific. So affirming prophethood implies truthfulness; denying truthfulness implies denial of prophethood. But affirming truthfulness does not imply prophethood.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين القياس الشرطي والقياس . والفرقُ بين هذا النمط والنمط الأوَّلِ : أَنَّ الأَولَ ترتيبه خبر الالا لالة عَنْ شيءٍ ، ثم حكم على الخبر بشيء ، فيلزم منه نتيجةً ، وهوَ وجه دلالة نمط التلازم الالتقاء بين المبتدأ الأوَّلِ والخبر الأخير ، وكلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأجزاء الثلاثة يصلحُ أن يُجعل وصفاً وخبراً وحُكماً للآخر في نظم هذا الكلام . وأمَّا هذا .. فإنَّهُ إظهارُ تلازم بين قضيتين غير متداخلتين ؛ فإِنَّ قولَنا : ( إنْ كانَتِ الصلاةُ صحيحة .. فالمصلّي متطهر ) بيانُ أنَّ كونَ المصلّي متطهّراً لازم لكون الصلاة صحيحة ، فلا يمكن أن يُجعل كونُ المصلّي متطهراً لا وصفاً للصلاة ، ولا وصفاً للصحة . والفرقُ مِنْ حيثُ الترتيب والنظم .. ظاهر . وأما وجه الدلالةِ : فهوَ أَنَّهُ مهما جُعِلَ شيء لازماً لشيء .. ألَّا يكونَ الملزومُ أعمَّ مِنَ اللازم ، بلْ إِمَّا أخص ، وإمَّا فينبغي مساوياً . ثبوت الأخص يلزم عنه وجود الأعمّ بالضرورة ؛ إذْ يلزمُ مِنْ ثبوتِ السوادِ ووجوده وجود اللون ، وهو الذي عنيناه بتسليم عينِ المقدَّمِ ، وانتفاء الأعمّ يوجب انتفاء الأخص بالضرورة ؛ إذْ يلزمُ مِن انتفاء اللونِ انتفاء السوادِ ، وهو الذي عنيناه بتسليم نقيض اللازم . فأمَّا ثبوتُ الأعم .. فلا يوجب ثبوت الأخص ؛ فإِنَّ ثبوت اللون ثبوت الأعم لا يلزم عنه ثبوت الأخص لا يدلُّ على ثبوتِ السوادِ ، فلذلك قلنا : تسليم عين اللازم لا ينتج لا نفي المقدَّمِ ولا ثبوته.
The difference between conditional syllogism and [categorical] syllogism. And the difference between this pattern and the first pattern is that the first one: its arrangement is a predicate of indication about something, then a ruling on the predicate with something, so a result follows from it, and it is the proof of the pattern of talazum: the meeting between the first subject and the last predicate, and each of the three parts is suitable to be made a description, predicate, and ruling for the other in the system of this speech. And as for this [pattern]... it is a showing of entailment between two non-intertwined propositions; for our statement: 'If the prayer is valid... then the worshiper is in a state of ritual purity' is a clarification that the worshiper's being in a state of ritual purity is necessary for the prayer's being valid. So it is not possible to make the worshiper's being in a state of ritual purity a description of the prayer, nor a description of validity. And the difference in terms of arrangement and system is apparent. As for the proof: it is that whenever something is made necessary for something, the necessitated thing (malzum) must not be more general than the necessary thing (lazim); rather it is either more specific or should be equal. The establishment of the more specific necessarily entails the existence of the more general; for from the establishment of blackness and its existence, the existence of color follows, and this is what we meant by acknowledging the very antecedent. And the negation of the more general necessitates the negation of the more specific necessarily; for from the negation of color, the negation of blackness follows, and this is what we meant by acknowledging the contradictory of the consequent. As for the establishment of the more general... it does not necessitate the establishment of the more specific; for the establishment of color, the more general, does not entail the establishment of the more specific; it does not indicate the establishment of blackness. Therefore we said: acknowledging the very consequent does not produce a conclusion, neither negation of the antecedent nor its affirmation.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between categorical syllogisms (qiyas hamli) and conditional syllogisms (qiyas sharti). The author explains that in a categorical syllogism, the arrangement involves a predicate (khabar) and a subject, with a middle term linking them, and each of the three terms can function as description, predicate, or judgment. In contrast, a conditional syllogism simply shows an entailment (talazum) between two independent propositions, like 'If prayer is valid, then the worshiper has wudu.' The consequent cannot be made a description of the antecedent or its validity. The author then elaborates the principle of talazum: the malzum (the thing that necessitates) must be either more specific (akhass) than, or equal to, the lazim (the necessary thing). The author uses the example of blackness and color: blackness (specific) entails color (general), so affirming blackness requires affirming color; negating color requires negating blackness. However, affirming color does not entail affirming blackness, because color is more general. This explains why acknowledging the consequent (taslim 'ayn al-lazim) is invalid: from the truth of 'the worshiper has wudu' we cannot infer the truth of 'the prayer is valid', because wudu is more general than valid prayer (i.e., there are other causes for wudu besides valid prayer). Similarly, acknowledging the contradictory of the antecedent (taslim naqid al-muqaddam) is invalid because negating the antecedent does not negate the consequent.

Key Terms
التلازم
الملزوم
اللازم
الأخص
الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, if we know that 'this object is black' (mithalu al-sawad), what can we conclude?
A) It is colored.
B) It is not colored.
C) It is white.
D) It has no color.

Key Takeaway
The specific necessarily implies the general, but the general does not imply any particular specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the entailment: 'If the person is a scholar, then he knows.' Which is the malzum and which is the lazim? Is knowing more general or more specific? Determine the valid inferences.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Pattern of Entailment in Conditional Syllogisms
Ibn Taymiyya explains the conditional syllogism's entailment pattern and its valid inferences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid inferences in conditional syllogisms based on the relation between antecedent and consequent (more specific, equal, or more general).

Core Idea
In a conditional, only affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent are valid if the antecedent is more specific or equal.

Ibn Taymiyya explains the logical pattern of entailment (talazum) in conditional propositions. He distinguishes it from coincidence and clarifies the conditions under which the four standard inferences are valid. This is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main parts of the passage
The first part distinguishes entailment from coincidence (iltiqa'), where in coincidence the parts can describe each other, while in entailment they are non-overlapping. The second part explains the inference rules.
The example 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is pure' illustrates non-overlapping propositions.
2
Understand the key analogy: blackness and color
Ibn Taymiyya uses 'blackness' as a more specific antecedent and 'color' as a more general consequent. This simplifies the logical relationships: blackness entails color, but not vice versa.
This is a generic example used to explain the abstract rules.
3
Recognize the four types of inference in conditional syllogisms
The four standard inferences are: affirming antecedent (valid if antecedent more specific or equal), denying consequent (valid if antecedent more specific or equal), affirming consequent (invalid unless antecedent equals consequent), denying antecedent (invalid unless antecedent equals consequent).
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that if the antecedent is more general, the only valid inference is the fallacy of making the more specific the consequent.
4
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
The passage gives the example 'If adultery of the married person exists, then stoning is obligatory'. Here, the antecedent and consequent are equal (one-to-one relation), so all four inferences are valid. Contrast with prayer-purity where they are not equal.
This shows how legal rulings depend on proper conditional reasoning.
5
Test with the equality case where all four are valid
When antecedent and consequent are equal, as in the effect-single cause example, affirming or denying either yields a valid conclusion. This is the full biconditional case.
Remember that equality is rare; most conditionals have one-way entailment.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجه دلالة نمط التلازم: الالتقاء بين المبتدأ الأوَّلِ والخبر الأخير، وكلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأجزاء الثلاثة يصلحُ أن يُجعل وصفاً وخبراً وحُكماً للآخر في نظم هذا الكلام. وأمَّا هذا .. فإنَّهُ إظهارُ تلازم بين قضيتين غير متداخلتين؛ فإِنَّ قولَنا: (إنْ كانَتِ الصلاةُ صحيحة .. فالمصلّي متطهر) بيانُ أنَّ كونَ المصلّي متطهّراً لازم لكون الصلاة صحيحة، فلا يمكن أن يُجعل كونُ المصلّي متطهراً لا وصفاً للصلاة، ولا وصفاً للصحة. والفرقُ مِنْ حيثُ الترتيب والنظم .. ظاهر. وأما وجه الدلالةِ: فهوَ أَنَّهُ مهما جُعِلَ شيء لازماً لشيء .. ألَّا يكونَ الملزومُ أعمَّ مِنَ اللازم، بلْ إِمَّا أخص، وإمَّا مساوياً. فينبغي: ثبوت الأخص يلزم عنه وجود الأعم بالضرورة؛ إذْ يلزمُ مِنْ ثبوتِ السوادِ وجود اللون، وهو الذي عنيناه بتسليم عينِ المقدَّمِ، وانتفاء الأعمّ يوجب انتفاء الأخص بالضرورة؛ إذْ يلزمُ مِن انتفاء اللونِ انتفاء السوادِ، وهو الذي عنيناه بتسليم نقيض اللازم. فأمَّا ثبوتُ الأعم .. فلا يوجب ثبوت الأخص؛ فإِنَّ ثبوت اللون لا يلزم عنه ثبوت السوادِ، فلذلك قلنا: تسليم عين اللازم لا ينتج لا نفي المقدَّمِ ولا ثبوته. وأمَّا انتفاء الأخصّ .. فلا يوجب انتفاء الأعم لا انتفاءً ولا ثبوتاً؛ فإنَّ انتفاءَ السوادِ لا يوجب انتفاء اللون ولا ثبوته، وهو نفي الأخص لا يلزم عنه نفي الأعم الذي عنيناه بقولنا: إنَّ تسليم نقيض المقدَّمِ لا ينتج لا عين اللازم ولا نقيضه أصلاً. وإنْ جُعِلَ الأخص لازماً للأعمّ .. فهو خطأ؛ كمَنْ يقولُ: إِنْ كانَ هذا لوناً .. فهو سوادٌ. وإنْ كانَ اللازم مساوياً للمقدَّم .. أنتج منه أربع تسليماتٍ؛ مساواة اللازم كقولنا: إنْ كانَ زنا المحصَنِ موجوداً .. فالرجمُ واجب، ومعلوم أنَّهُ موجودٌ؛ فإذا الرجمُ واجبٌ، أو: ومعلوم أنَّهُ غيرُ موجودٍ؛ فإذاً الرجم غيرُ واجب، أو: معلوم أنَّ الرجمَ واجبٌ؛ فإذا زنا المحصن موجود، أو: معلوم أنَّ الرجمَ غيرُ واجب؛ فإذا زنا المحصن غيرُ موجود. وكذلك كلُّ معلولٍ لهُ علَّةٌ واحدةٌ وهو مساو لعلَّتِهِ، ويلزمُ أحدهما الآخر .. فينتج فيه التسليماتُ الأربع. ومثالُهُ مِنَ المحسوس: إنْ كانَتِ الشمس طالعة .. فالنهارُ موجود، ولكنَّها طالعةٌ؛ فهو موجود، ولكنها غيرُ طالعةٍ؛ فهو غير موجود، ولكنَّ النهار موجود؛ فالشمس طالعةٌ، ولكنَّ النهار غير موجود؛ فالشمس غيرُ طالعة.
The aspect of indication of the pattern of entailment: The meeting between the first antecedent and the last consequent, and each one of the three parts is suitable to be made a description and predicate and judgment for the other in the order of this speech. As for this... it is a manifestation of entailment between two non-overlapping propositions; for our saying: 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is pure' is a statement that the worshipper being pure is a necessary consequent of the prayer being valid, so it is not possible for the worshipper being pure to be made a description of the prayer, nor a description of validity. And the difference in terms of arrangement and order is apparent. As for the aspect of indication: it is that whenever something is made a necessary consequent of something, the antecedent should not be more general than the consequent, but rather either more specific or equal. So it follows: the affirmation of the more specific necessarily entails the existence of the more general; for the affirmation of blackness entails the existence of color, and that is what we meant by 'affirming the very antecedent', and the negation of the more general necessitates the negation of the more specific necessarily; for the negation of color entails the negation of blackness, and that is what we meant by 'affirming the contradictory of the consequent'. As for the affirmation of the more general... it does not entail the affirmation of the more specific; for the affirmation of color does not entail the affirmation of blackness, so we said: affirming the very consequent does not produce either the negation of the antecedent nor its affirmation. And as for the negation of the more specific... it does not entail the negation of the more general, neither negation nor affirmation; for the negation of blackness does not entail the negation of color nor its affirmation, and it is the negation of the more specific does not entail the negation of the more general which we meant by our saying: 'affirming the contradictory of the antecedent does not produce either the very consequent nor its contradictory at all.' And if the more specific is made the consequent of the more general... it is an error; as one who says: 'If this is a color, then it is blackness.' And if the consequent is equal to the antecedent... it produces four affirmations; the equality of the consequent as our saying: 'If adultery of the married person exists, then stoning is obligatory', and it is known that it exists; so stoning is obligatory, or: it is known that it does not exist; so stoning is not obligatory, or: it is known that stoning is obligatory; so adultery of the married person exists, or: it is known that stoning is not obligatory; so adultery of the married person does not exist. And likewise every effect has a single cause and it is equal to its cause, and one entails the other... so the four affirmations result from it. And its example from the sensible: 'If the sun is risen, then daytime exists', and it is risen; so it exists, and it is not risen; so it does not exist, and daytime exists; so the sun is risen, and daytime does not exist; so the sun is not risen.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage, from Ibn Taymiyya's Mahakk Nazar, explains the pattern of entailment (talazum) in conditional syllogisms, distinguishing it from the pattern of coincidence (iltiqa'). The author first clarifies the difference: in coincidence, the parts can serve as descriptions for each other (like subject and predicate), whereas in entailment, the two propositions are non-overlapping and one is necessary consequence of the other. Using the example 'If the prayer is valid, then the worshipper is pure', he shows that the consequent (purity) cannot be a description of the antecedent (prayer) because they are distinct. The core of the passage is the exposition of the logical relations between antecedent (malzum) and consequent (lazim). The author states that the antecedent must not be more general than the consequent; it must be either more specific or equal. He then enumerates the possible inferences: (1) If the antecedent is more specific, then affirming the antecedent entails affirming the consequent (e.g., blackness affirms color), and negating the consequent entails negating the antecedent (e.g., negating color negates blackness). (2) Affirming the more general consequent does not entail anything about the more specific antecedent (color does not entail blackness). (3) Negating the more specific antecedent does not entail anything about the more general consequent (blackness being absent does not imply anything about color). If someone erroneously makes the more specific the consequent of the more general, it is a mistake (e.g., 'If color, then blackness'). When antecedent and consequent are equal, four valid inferences are possible: affirming antecedent affirms consequent, denying antecedent denies consequent, affirming consequent affirms antecedent, denying consequent denies antecedent. The author gives examples from Islamic law (stoning for adultery of a married person) and from the sensible world (sunrise and daytime). This passage is a standard exposition of the conditional syllogism's inference rules, using concrete Islamic examples to ground the abstract logical principles.

ملزوم
لازم
تسليم عين المقدم
تسليم نقيض اللازم
