Key Takeaway
The conditional syllogism's validity depends on the logical relation between antecedent and consequent: only when the antecedent is more specific or equal does affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yield certain conclusions.

The Four Valid Deductions from a Coextensive Consequent
Author demonstrates that when consequent equals antecedent, four conditional deductions are valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the four valid conditional deductions when the antecedent and consequent are coextensive, using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
When antecedent and consequent are equivalent, four deductions are valid, not just two.

In conditional syllogisms, when the consequent is coextensive (equal in meaning) with the antecedent, the relationship becomes biconditional. This means that affirming or denying either part yields a valid conclusion. The author illustrates this with a fiqh example about stoning for married adulterers and a natural example about sunrise and daytime. Understanding this pattern deepens your ability to reason with equivalent statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional with coextensive consequent
Look for a conditional statement where the consequent is logically equivalent to the antecedent. The author says 'if the consequent is coextensive with the antecedent, it yields four affirmations.' Locate the key examples: the fiqh conditional about zina of muhsan and stoning, and the sensible example about sunrise and daytime.
Coextensive means they imply each other – like a biconditional.
2
Enumerate the four valid deduction patterns
The four are: (a) affirming the antecedent yields the consequent, (b) denying the antecedent yields denial of the consequent, (c) affirming the consequent yields the antecedent, (d) denying the consequent yields denial of the antecedent. Notice that (b) and (c) are valid only because the consequent is coextensive.
In a regular conditional, only modus ponens (a) and modus tollens (d) are valid.
3
Trace the fiqh example for each deduction
Take the conditional: 'If the adultery of a married person exists, then stoning is obligatory.' Then apply each pattern: (1) it exists → stoning obligatory; (2) it does not exist → stoning not obligatory; (3) stoning obligatory → it exists; (4) stoning not obligatory → it does not exist.
The passage explicitly states all four.
4
Generalize to any effect with a single cause
The author notes that every effect that has one cause is coextensive with that cause. Thus, the same four deductions apply. Think of a distinct efficient cause in Islamic cosmology: if A causes B, then A and B are coextensive, so you can deduce both directions.
This principle is used in kalam arguments about causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنْ كانَ اللازم مساوياً للمقدَّم أنتج منه أربع تسليماتٍ؛ مساواة اللازم كقولنا: إنْ كانَ زنا المحصَنِ موجوداً فالرجمُ واجب، ومعلوم أنه موجود؛ فإذا الرجمُ واجب، أو: ومعلوم أنَّهُ غيرُ موجودٍ؛ فإذاً الرجم غيرُ واجب، أو: معلوم أنَّ الرجمَ واجبٌ؛ فإذا زنا المحصن موجود، أو: معلوم أنَّ الرجمَ غيرُ واجب؛ فإذا زنا المحصن غيرُ موجود. وكذلك كلُّ معلولٍ لهُ علَّةٌ واحدةٌ وهو مساو لعلَّتِهِ، ويلزمُ أحدهما الآخر فينتج فيه التسليماتُ الأربع. ومثالُهُ مِنَ المحسوس: إنْ كانَتِ الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، ولكنَّها طالعةٌ؛ فهو موجود، ولكنها غيرُ طالعةٍ؛ فهوَ غير موجود، ولكنَّ النهار موجود؛ فالشمس طالعةٌ، ولكنَّ النهار غير موجود؛ فالشمس غيرُ طالعة.
And if the consequent is coextensive with the antecedent, it yields from it four affirmations: the coextensiveness of the consequent, like our saying: 'If the adultery of a married person exists, then stoning is obligatory.' And it is known that it exists, so therefore stoning is obligatory. Or: 'And it is known that it does not exist, so therefore stoning is not obligatory.' Or: 'It is known that stoning is obligatory, so therefore the adultery of a married person exists.' Or: 'It is known that stoning is not obligatory, so therefore the adultery of a married person does not exist.' And likewise every effect has one cause and is coextensive with its cause, and one implies the other, so the four affirmations are produced. And an example from the sensible world: 'If the sun is risen, then daytime exists.' (Then the four deductions: but it is risen, so it exists; but it is not risen, so it does not exist; but daytime exists, so the sun is risen; but daytime does not exist, so the sun is not risen.)
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage explains the pattern of qiyas istithna'i when the consequent is coextensive (musawi) with the antecedent. The author gives two examples: one from fiqh (stoning for zina of muhsan) and one from the sensible world (sunrise and daytime). In this pattern, four deductions are valid: affirming the antecedent yields the consequent; denying the antecedent yields denial of the consequent (since they are equal, not because of the usual rule); affirming the consequent yields the antecedent; denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent. This is because the two are equivalent. The author notes that this applies to every effect with a single cause.

Key Terms
اللازم المساوي (al-lazim al-musawi)
تسليمات (taslimat)
المقدَّم (al-muqaddam)
اللازم (al-lazim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
When the consequent is coextensive with the antecedent, all four conditional deductions become valid, turning the conditional into a biconditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conditional 'If a sale involves riba, then it is invalid.' Assume that riba and invalidity are coextensive in this context. Write out the four valid deductions using the model from the lesson, and identify which deduction is being used in each step.

The Four Deductions of Mutual Exclusivity (Ta'anud)
Author defines mutual exclusivity and lists its four deductions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid deductions in a disjunctive syllogism (ta'anud) and apply them to a theological dichotomy such as the world's eternity or temporality.

Core Idea
Mutual exclusivity yields four deductions: each disjunct can be affirmed or denied.

In this passage, the author introduces the third pattern of conditional syllogism, which he calls ta'anud or mutual exclusivity. This is the logical foundation of the kalam method of sabr wa taqsim (division and examination). By mastering the four deductions, you will be able to reason correctly when faced with contradictory alternatives in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
Look for two contradictory propositions (e.g., 'the world is eternal' vs. 'the world is originated'). They must be mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
This is the premise of the disjunctive syllogism.
2
Understand the form 'either A or B'
Recognize this as a disjunctive conditional (sharti munfasil). The disjunction is exclusive: both cannot be true and both cannot be false.
This is what the author calls ta'anud.
3
Trace the four deductions
Work through each combination: affirm A → deny B; affirm B → deny A; deny A → affirm B; deny B → affirm A.
These correspond to the four affirmations (taslimat) the author lists.
4
Apply to the world example
Using the author's example: if we know the world is originated, we deduce it is not eternal; if it is not originated, it is eternal; etc.
All four are valid because the disjunction is exclusive.

النمط الثالث : نمط التعاند وهو على ضدّ نمط التلازم، والمتكلمون يسمونه : السبر والتقسيم، والمنطقيُّونَ يسمُّونَهُ : الشرطي المنفصل، ونحنُ سميناه : التعاند. ومثاله : العالم إمَّا قديم وإمَّا حادث فهذه مقدمة وهما قضيتان. الثانية : أن تسلّم إحدى القضيتين أو نقيضها، فيلزم منه - لا محالة - نتيجة، وينتج فيهِ أربع تسليماتٍ، فإنَّا نقول ومعلوم أنَّهُ حادثُ فيلزمُ نقيضُ المقدمة الأخرى؛ وهوَ أَنَّهُ ليس بقديم، أو نقولُ : ومعلوم أنَّه ليس بحادث فيلزم منه عين المقدمة الأخرى، أو نقولُ : ومعلومٌ أَنَّهُ قديمٌ فيلزم منه نقيضُ الأخرى؛ وهوَ أنَّهُ ليس بحادث، أو نقولُ : ومعلوم أنَّهُ ليس بقديم فيلزم منه عينُ الأخرى . فبالجملة : كلُّ قسمين متناقضين متقابلين إذا وُجِدَ فيهما
The third pattern: the pattern of mutual exclusivity, and it is opposite to the pattern of implication. The mutakallimun call it 'sabr wa taqsim' (division and analysis), and the logicians call it the 'disjunctive conditional', and we have named it 'ta'anud'. Its example: 'The world: either it is eternal or it is originated' – this is one premise, and they are two propositions. The second (step): that you affirm one of the two propositions or its negation, then a conclusion follows necessarily, and it yields four affirmations. For we say: 'And it is known that it is originated' so the negation of the other premise follows, which is that it is not eternal. Or we say: 'And it is known that it is not originated' so the very other premise follows. Or we say: 'And it is known that it is eternal' so the negation of the other follows, which is that it is not originated. Or we say: 'And it is known that it is not eternal' so the very other follows. In summary: any two contradictory opposites, when found in them...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage introduces the third pattern of conditional syllogism, which the author calls ta'anud (mutual exclusivity). It corresponds to the disjunctive conditional (sharti munfasil). The mutakallimun know it as sabr wa taqsim (division and testing). The example is the classic kalam dichotomy: the world is either eternal or originated. This is a disjunction that is exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw). In this pattern, four deductions are valid: if you affirm one disjunct (it is originated), you must deny the other (it is not eternal); if you deny one (it is not originated), you must affirm the other (it is eternal); similarly, affirming the other leads to denying the first; denying the other leads to affirming the first. The author ends with a general statement that any pair of contradictory opposites yields this pattern.

The Disjunctive Syllogism: Definition and Four Valid Inferences
Author defines the disjunctive mode (ta'ānuḍ) with its four valid inference patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the four valid inferences of a disjunctive syllogism in Islamic scholarly arguments.

Core Idea
Affirming one disjunct denies the other; denying one affirms the other.

This passage introduces the third mode of syllogism, the disjunctive syllogism, which is central to Kalam arguments about the world's temporality. The author defines it, gives its names in different traditions, and enumerates exactly four valid ways to draw conclusions from a disjunctive premise.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the disjunctive premise
Identify the first premise as a disjunction: 'The world is either eternal or originated.' The two disjuncts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
This is called al-sharṭī al-munfaṣil in logic.
2
Understand the second premise
The second premise affirms one disjunct or its negation. There are exactly four possibilities: affirm disjunct A, affirm disjunct B, negate A, negate B.
3
Derive the conclusion for each case
From affirming one disjunct, you deny the other; from denying one, you affirm the other. Verify each of the four patterns with the example.
For instance, affirming 'it is originated' yields 'it is not eternal'.
4
Generalise the rule
The author summarises: for any pair of contradictories meeting the conditions, affirming one negates the other and negating one affirms the other.
This rule applies only when the disjuncts are truly mutually exclusive and exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third mode: the mode of mutual opposition (ta'ānuḍ). It is opposite to the mode of concomitance. The mutakallimūn call it 'al-sabr wa al-taqsīm' (investigation and division), and the logicians call it 'al-sharṭī al-munfaṣil' (disjunctive conditional). We have named it 'al-ta'ānuḍ'. Its example: 'The world is either eternal or originated.' So this is the first premise. The second premise: that you affirm one of the two propositions or its negation, so necessarily a conclusion follows. Four affirmations are possible in it. For we say: 'It is known that it is originated' so the negation of the other premise follows, which is that it is not eternal. Or we say: 'It is known that it is not originated' so the exact other premise follows. Or we say: 'It is known that it is eternal' so the negation of the other follows, which is that it is not originated. Or we say: 'It is known that it is not eternal' so the exact other follows. In summary: Every pair of opposite contradictories, if the conditions of contradiction are present in them as previously mentioned, then affirming one entails negating the other, and negating one entails affirming the other.

Commentary
This passage defines the third mode of syllogism, known as mutual opposition (ta'ānuḍ). It opens by giving its names: in kalam it is called investigation and division (sabr wa taqsīm), in logic it is called the disjunctive conditional, and the author calls it ta'ānuḍ. The example used is the classic disjunction: the world is either eternal or originated. The passage concludes with a general rule: for any pair of contradictories that meet the conditions of contradiction, affirming one yields denial of the other and denying one yields affirmation of the other. This is the essence of the disjunctive syllogism.

The Condition of Exhaustive Division in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains exhaustive division condition and applies it to vision of Allah debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a disjunctive division is exhaustive and assess the validity of inferences based on it in Islamic theological arguments.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism yields certain conclusions only when its division covers all possibilities.

In disjunctive reasoning, it is not enough that the premises are mutually exclusive; they must also exhaust all options. The author of our text explains this through simple examples and then applies it to a famous theological debate about the vision of Allah. By mastering this condition, you will be able to evaluate the strength of arguments that use 'either-or' reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the 'either...or...' statement in the passage. The author gives examples such as 'this thing is either equal, or less, or more' and 'Zaid is either in Iraq or in the Hijaz'. Also, the theological argument: 'the corrector of vision is either a substance or an accident'.
2
Check for exhaustiveness
Determine whether the division covers all logical possibilities. The threefold division (equal/less/more) is exhaustive because any quantity must fit one of these. The Iraq/Hijaz division is not exhaustive because Zaid could be elsewhere. The substance/accident division may not be exhaustive unless additional categories are considered.
Exhaustiveness is what the author calls 'hāsira' or 'mahṣūr'.
3
Apply the inference rules
For an exhaustive division, affirming one disjunct negates all others; negating all but one affirms the remaining. The author states: 'affirming one produces the negation of the others, and refuting two produces the affirmation of the third'. For a non-exhaustive division, like the Iraq/Hijaz example, negating one does NOT affirm the other because a third option exists.
This distinction is crucial for validity.
4
Examine the theological application
The author critiques the argument for the vision of Allah that relies on the disjunction 'the corrector of vision is either substance or accident'. He notes that this division is almost not exhaustive, meaning the argument is invalid unless an exhaustive classification is provided. You must consider whether other categories (e.g., existence itself) could be the corrector.
This shows how logical rigor affects theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يشترط أن تنحصر المقدمة في قسمين، بل شرطه أن تُستَوفى أقسامهُ وإنْ كانَ ثلاثاً فإِنَّا نقولُ : ( هذا الشيءُ : إمَّا مساو ، وإمَّا أقلُّ ، وإما أكثرُ ) فهذهِ ثلاثةٌ ، ولكنها حاصرةٌ ، فإثبات واحدٍ ينتج نفي الآخرين ، وإبطال اثنين ينتج إثباتَ الثالث ، وإبطال واحدٍ ينتج انحصار الحقِّ في الآخرين أحدهما لا بعينه . والذي لا ينتج هوَ أَلَّا يكون محصوراً ؛ كقولك : ( زيد : إما بالعراقِ ، وإمَّا بالحجاز ) وهذا ممَّا يوجبُ إثباتُ واحد منهما عند النفي نفي الآخر ، فإِنَّهُ إِنْ ثبتَ أَنَّهُ بالعراقِ .. انتفى عن الحجاز وغيره ، وأما إبطال واحدٍ .. فلا ينتج إثبات الآخرِ ؛ إذْ رَبَّما يكونُ في صُقْعِ ثالث . ويكاد يكونُ كلامُ مَنْ يستدلُّ على إثباتِ رؤية الباري بإحالة تصحيح الرؤية على الوجودِ غير محصور، إلا أن نتكلَّفَ لحصرِهِ وَجْهاً بأن نقول : مصحِحُ الرؤيةِ لا يخلو : إما أن يكونَ كونه جوهراً فيبطل بالعرضِ ، أو كونُهُ عرضاً فيبطل بالجوهرِ
It is not a condition that the premise be limited to two divisions; rather, its condition is that its divisions be exhausted, even if they are three. For we say: 'This thing is either equal, or less, or more.' These are three, but they are exhaustive. So affirming one produces the negation of the others, and refuting two produces the affirmation of the third, and refuting one produces the restriction of truth to the other two, one of them not specified. What does not produce a conclusion is when it is not exhaustive, like your statement: 'Zaid is either in Iraq or in the Hijaz.' This requires that affirming one of them, when the other is negated, negates the other, because if it is established that he is in Iraq, then he is absent from the Hijaz and others. But refuting one does not produce the affirmation of the other, since perhaps he is in a third region. And the speech of whoever argues for establishing the vision of the Exalted by making the corrector of vision depend on existence is almost not exhaustive, unless we undertake to exhaust it by saying: The corrector of vision is either that it is a substance, which is invalidated by its being an accident, or that it is an accident, which is invalidated by its being a substance...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the disjunctive syllogism by focusing on the condition of exhaustiveness. It states that the premise need not be limited to two divisions; it must be exhaustive, even if there are three or more. An example of three exhaustive categories is given: equal, less, more. The inference rules are then outlined: affirming one negates the others; refuting two affirms the third; refuting one leaves the truth to the other two but unspecified. Next, the author presents a case where the division is not exhaustive, using the example of Zaid being either in Iraq or Hejaz. Here, affirming one does negate the other (since they are mutually exclusive), but denying one does not affirm the other because a third location is possible. Finally, the author applies this to the theological argument for the vision of Allah. The argument that the corrector of vision must be either substantiality or accidentality is critiqued as possibly non-exhaustive unless a proper exhaustive division can be made.

محصور (mahṣūr, limited/exhausted)

Exhaustiveness is a necessary condition for a disjunctive syllogism to yield a certain conclusion when denying one disjunct.

8 min
The Condition of Exhaustiveness in Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains the principle of exhaustiveness in disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a disjunctive premise is exhaustive and determine what conclusions follow from affirming or negating disjuncts.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism is only productive when its alternatives are exhaustive.

Disjunctive syllogisms are powerful tools for reasoning, but they only yield certain conclusions when the disjunction covers all possibilities. This lesson unpacks the condition of exhaustiveness (ḥaṣr) and shows how to handle divisions with more than two alternatives. The author provides simple examples, such as 'equal, less, more', as well as a non-exhaustive case to highlight the pitfall.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
Locate the disjunctive premise (muqaddima munfaṣila) in the argument. It will typically be of the form 'Either A, or B (or C...)'. In the passage, examples include 'This thing: either equal, less, or more' and 'Zayd: either in Iraq or in the Hejaz'.
The disjunction can have two or more disjuncts.
2
Check exhaustiveness
Ask: Is the list of disjuncts exhaustive? That is, does it cover all possible alternatives without exception? The author distinguishes the exhaustive (ḥāṣira) from the non-exhaustive. The three-way division of equal/less/more is exhaustive; the two-way division of Iraq/Hejaz is not, because Zayd could be elsewhere.
Exhaustiveness is not about the number of disjuncts but about covering all logical possibilities.
3
Determine what follows from an affirmation
If the disjunction is exhaustive, affirming one disjunct entails negating all the others. For example, if we prove the thing is equal, then it is proved not less and not more. If the disjunction is non-exhaustive, affirming one still negates only that specific alternative but not others not listed.
In the non-exhaustive case, proving Zayd is in Iraq still negates Hejaz and elsewhere, but that is a separate inference.
4
Determine what follows from a negation
If the disjunction is exhaustive, negating all but one disjunct proves that remaining disjunct. Negating only one leaves the truth distributed among the remaining ones, but indeterminately. If the disjunction is non-exhaustive, negating one disjunct does not prove any specific alternative because the truth might lie in an unlisted possibility.
The author says: negating one of three leaves the truth in the other two 'one of them indeterminately'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرائط التناقض كما سبق .. فينتجُ إثباتُ أحدِهِما نفي الآخرِ ونفي أحدِهِما إثبات الآخر . ولا يشترط أن تنحصر المقدمة في قسمين، بل شرطه أن تُستَوفى أقسامهُ وإنْ كانَ ثلاثاً فإِنَّا نقولُ : ( هذا الشيءُ : إمَّا مساو ، وإمَّا أقلُّ ، وإما أكثرُ ) فهذهِ ثلاثةٌ ، ولكنها حاصرةٌ ، فإثبات واحدٍ ينتج نفي الآخرين ، وإبطال اثنين ينتج إثباتَ الثالث ، وإبطال واحدٍ ينتج انحصار الحقِّ في الآخرين أحدهما لا بعينه . والذي لا ينتج هوَ أَلَّا يكون محصوراً ؛ كقولك : ( زيد : إما بالعراقِ ، وإمَّا بالحجاز ) وهذا ممَّا يوجبُ إثباتُ واحد منهما عند النفي نفي الآخر ، فإِنَّهُ إِنْ ثبتَ أَنَّهُ بالعراقِ .. انتفى عن الحجاز وغيره ، وأما إبطال واحدٍ .. فلا ينتج إثبات الآخرِ ؛ إذْ رَبَّما يكونُ في صُقْعِ ثالث . • • ويكاد يكونُ كلامُ مَنْ يستدلُّ على إثباتِ رؤية الباري بإحالة تصحيح الرؤية على الوجودِ غير محصور، إلا أن نتكلَّفَ لحصرِهِ وَجْهاً بأن نقول : مصحِحُ الرؤيةِ لا يخلو : إما أن يكونَ كونه جوهراً فيبطل بالعرضِ ، أوْ كونُهُ عرضاً فيبطل بالجوهرِ ، أوْ كونُهُ سواداً أو لوناً فيبطلُ بالحركةِ ، فلا تبقى شركة لهذهِ المختلفاتِ إِلَّا في الوجودِ ، فهو المصحِحُ ؛ إذْ يمكن أن يكونَ قد بقي أمرٌ آخرُ مشترك لم يعثر عليه الباحث سوى الوجودِ ؛ مثل كونه بجهةٍ مِنَ الرائي مثلاً ، فإنْ أُبطل هذا أيضاً .. فلعل ثَمَّ معنى آخرَ ، إلَّا أن نتكلَّفَ حصر المعاني ونتكلَّفَ نفي جميعها ، ونبيِّنَ أنَّ طلب مصحح الرؤيةِ لا بدَّ منه ، فعند ذلك تحصل النتيجه . فهذه ضروبُ الأقيسة ، فكلُّ استدلال لا يمكن ردُّه إلى هذهِ الضروبِ .. فهو غير منتج ، وسنزيده شرحاً في حق اللواحق
Conditions of contradiction as before... So proving one of them yields the negation of the other, and negating one of them yields the affirmation of the other. It is not required that the premise be limited to two divisions, rather its condition is that its divisions be exhausted even if they are three. For we say: 'This thing: either equal, or less, or more.' So these are three, but they are exhaustive. So proving one yields the negation of the others, and invalidating two yields the affirmation of the third, and invalidating one yields the restriction of truth to the other two, one of them indeterminately. And what does not yield a conclusion is when it is not exhaustive; like your saying: 'Zayd: either in Iraq or in the Hejaz.' And this is such that proving one of them entails, when negating, the negation of the other, for if it is established that he is in Iraq, then he is excluded from the Hejaz and elsewhere. But invalidating one does not yield the affirmation of the other, for perhaps he is in a third region. • • The speech of one who argues for the affirmation of seeing the Almighty by referring the correctness of vision to existence is almost not exhaustive, unless we undertake to find a way to exhaust it by saying: The corrector of vision cannot be free: either it is being a substance, which is invalidated by accident; or being an accident, invalidated by substance; or being blackness or color, invalidated by motion. So no commonality remains among these differences except existence, so it is the corrector. However, it is possible that there remains another common matter that the researcher did not discover besides existence, such as it being in a direction relative to the seer, for example. If this is also invalidated, then perhaps there is another meaning, unless we undertake to exhaust the meanings and undertake to negate all of them, and show that seeking the corrector of vision is necessary. Then at that point the conclusion is obtained. So these are the figures of syllogisms. So every inference that cannot be reduced to these figures is unproductive, and we will increase its explanation in the section on consequents.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for a productive disjunctive syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī munfaṣil). The author begins by stating the fundamental rule: in a true contradiction, affirming one entails negating the other, and negating one entails affirming the other. However, for a disjunctive premise, it is not necessary that it have only two disjuncts; it is sufficient that the disjuncts be exhaustive (ḥāṣira), even if there are three or more. The author gives an example: 'This thing is either equal, or less, or more.' These three are exhaustive, so proving one negates the other two, negating two proves the third, but negating only one leaves the truth in the other two indeterminately. Then the author contrasts this with a non-exhaustive disjunction: 'Zayd is either in Iraq or in the Hejaz.' Proving one negates the other, but negating one does not prove the other because Zayd might be elsewhere. The passage then applies this principle to a theological argument for the beatific vision (ru'yat al-Bārī). The argument attempts to prove that existence is the corrector of vision by exhausting the possible correctors: substance, accident, blackness/color, and motion. But the author warns that this exhaustion may be incomplete, as there could be another common factor like direction. To obtain a certain conclusion, one must genuinely exhaust all possibilities and negate them. The passage concludes that any inference not reducible to these figures (i.e., categorical, conditional, or disjunctive syllogisms) is unproductive.

إنتاج
تناقض

Checkpoint

B) The truth is restricted to the other two, but which one is indeterminate.
C) Nothing follows because three alternatives are too many.
D) The negated alternative is proved false, and the other two are proved false as well.

Key Takeaway
Exhaustiveness of the disjuncts is the key condition for a disjunctive syllogism to be productive: negating all but one proves that one; negating only one leaves the truth among the rest indeterminately.

Evaluating the Argument for Seeing God
Author applies the condition of exhaustiveness to critique a kalam argument for seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess whether a theological argument that uses a disjunctive syllogism is truly exhaustive, and identify potential hidden alternatives.

Core Idea
A seemingly exhaustive argument may hide a missing alternative, undermining the conclusion.

The author applies the condition of exhaustiveness to a classical kalam argument: proving that existence is the corrector of vision (muṣaḥḥiḥ al-ru'ya) for seeing God. He warns that the argument's division of possible correctors may not be truly exhaustive, as there might be another common factor like direction. This lesson guides you through evaluating the exhaustiveness of such a theological argument using the logical tool learned earlier.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction in the argument
Find the list of possible correctors (muṣaḥḥiḥāt) for vision that the proponent claims are exhaustive. The passage states: being a substance, being an accident, being blackness/color, and by elimination, existence is left as the only common factor.
The proponent makes a disjunction: the corrector is either substance, accident, color, or existence.
2
Check for missing alternatives
The author points out that there might be another common property, such as 'being in a direction relative to the seer' (kawnuhu bi-jiha min al-rā'ī). This shows that the original list is not truly exhaustive unless proven otherwise.
The author suggests that we must undertake to exhaust all possible meanings.
3
Determine the consequences of incompleteness
If the division is not exhaustive, negating all but one option does not prove that the remaining option is the corrector. The conclusion remains uncertain until all possibilities are genuinely exhausted and negated.
The author says the argument 'is almost not exhaustive' and requires a burdensome effort to complete.
4
Assess the remedy proposed
The author suggests that to obtain a certain conclusion, one must carefully enumerate all possible candidates and negate each one, showing that seeking the corrector is necessary. Then the conclusion follows.
This mirrors the general rule: exhaustiveness makes the disjunctive syllogism productive.

Conditions of contradiction as before... So proving one of them yields the negation of the other, and negating one of them yields the affirmation of the other. It is not required that the premise be limited to two divisions, rather its condition is that its divisions be exhausted even if they are three. For we say: 'This thing: either equal, or less, or more.' So these are three, but they are exhaustive. So proving one yields the negation of the others, and invalidating two yields the affirmation of the third, and invalidating one yields the restriction of truth to the other two, one of them indeterminately. And what does not yield a conclusion is when it is not exhaustive; like your saying: 'Zayd: either in Iraq or in the Hejaz.' And this is such that proving one of them entails, when negating, the negation of the other, for if it is established that he is in Iraq, then he is excluded from the Hejaz and elsewhere. But invalidating one does not yield the affirmation of the other, for perhaps he is in a third region. • • The speech of one who argues for the affirmation of seeing the Almighty by referring the correctness of vision to existence is almost not exhaustive, unless we undertake to find a way to exhaust it by saying: The corrector of vision cannot be free: either it is being a substance, which is invalidated by accident; or being an accident, invalidated by substance; or being blackness or color, invalidated by motion. So no commonality remains among these differences except existence, so it is the corrector. However, it is possible that there remains another common matter that the researcher did not discover besides existence, such as it being in a direction relative to the seer, for example. If this is also invalidated, then perhaps there is another meaning, unless we undertake to exhaust the meanings and undertake to negate all of them, and show that seeking the corrector of vision is necessary. Then at that point the conclusion is obtained. So these are the figures of syllogisms. So every inference that cannot be reduced to these figures is unproductive, and we will increase its explanation in the section on consequents.

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for a productive disjunctive syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī munfaṣil). The author begins by stating the fundamental rule: in a true contradiction, affirming one entails negating the other, and negating one entails affirming the other. However, for a disjunctive premise, it is not necessary that it have only two disjuncts; it is sufficient that the disjuncts be exhaustive (ḥāṣira), even if there are three or more. The author gives an example: 'This thing is either equal, or less, or more.' These three are exhaustive, so proving one negates the other two, negating two proves the third, but negating only one leaves the truth in the other two indeterminately. Then the author contrasts this with a non-exhaustive disjunction: 'Zayd is either in Iraq or in the Hejaz.' Proving one negates the other, but negating one does not prove the other because Zayd might be elsewhere. The passage then applies this principle to a theological argument for the beatific vision (ru'yat al-Bārī). The argument attempts to prove that existence is the corrector of vision by exhausting the possible correctors: substance, accident, blackness/color, and motion. But the author warns that this exhaustion may be incomplete, as there could be another common factor like direction. To obtain a certain conclusion, one must genuinely exhaust all possibilities and negate them. The passage concludes that any inference not reducible to these figures (i.e., categorical, conditional, or disjunctive syllogisms) is unproductive.

The Rectifier of Vision and the Necessity of Syllogistic Form
Author concludes the rectifier argument and asserts syllogistic reductionism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rectifier of vision according to the author and explain why all valid arguments must reduce to the types of syllogisms.

Core Idea
Only existence remains as the common rectifier; all valid reasoning must be reducible to syllogistic forms.

This passage from Mahakk al-Nazar concludes the discussion on what rectifies vision for different objects. The author methodically eliminates candidates like blackness, color, and direction, leaving existence as the only common attribute. He then makes a foundational claim for all logic: any argument that cannot be reduced to the established types of syllogisms is unproductive. This lesson unpacks both the critical method and the meta-logical principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
The author is searching for a common attribute (musahhih) that allows vision to perceive different objects (e.g., black, white). Consider what could be shared among all visible things.
2
Follow the Elimination
The author considers candidates: blackness/color are invalidated by motion (since moving objects change color). Direction from the viewer is also refuted. Note the conditional reasoning: if blackness/color, then invalidated; since not invalidated, they cannot be the rectifier.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Recognize the Conclusion
After eliminating other possibilities, only 'existence' (al-wujud) remains as the common attribute. The author acknowledges that there might be other hidden meanings, but if one enumerates and negates all, existence is necessary.
Existence is the most universal predicate.
4
Understand the Meta-Claim
The author then declares that these are the 'types of syllogisms' (durub al-aqyisa) and that any inference not reducible to them is unproductive. This asserts that all valid reasoning must follow the recognized categorical syllogistic forms.
This establishes a canon of valid argument patterns.
5
Connect to Later Sections
The author promises further explanation in the section on logical consequences (bab al-lawahiq). This hints that the discussion of syllogistic reduction will be expanded.

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against various proposed common rectifiers for the vision of different objects. The author argues that if the rectifier were blackness or color, it would be invalidated by motion (since moving objects change color or blackness). Therefore, the only commonality among the different objects (e.g., black, white, etc.) is existence itself. He then acknowledges that there might be another hidden common feature, such as direction relative to the viewer, but if that too is refuted, one must enumerate all meanings and negate them, ultimately concluding that the request for a rectifier is necessary. After this, the author states that these (the types of arguments considered) are the types of syllogisms, and any reasoning that cannot be reduced to these types is unproductive, promising further explanation in the section on logical consequences.

The Matter of the Syllogism: Premises Must Match the Conclusion
Author introduces premises as material of syllogism using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given set of premises is suitable for producing a certain or probable conclusion, based on the principle of 'material of the syllogism'.

Core Idea
The material of the syllogism determines the certainty of the conclusion.

This passage opens the second part of the treatise, shifting focus from the form of syllogism to its material—the premises. The author uses a powerful analogy: premises are to a syllogism as cloth is to a shirt or wood to a bed. Just as you cannot make a shirt from wood, you cannot build a productive syllogism from arbitrary premises. The type of conclusion (certain or probable) dictates the required type of premises.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy
The author compares premises to raw materials (cloth, wood) and the syllogism to the finished product (shirt, bed). The form (stitching, shape) was covered earlier. Now focus on the material: just as a sword cannot be made from cloth, a certain conclusion cannot come from probable premises.
2
Identify the two types of conclusions
The text mentions two types: certain (yaqini) and probable (dhanni, i.e., fiqh-related). The desired conclusion determines the material: certain conclusions require certain premises; probable conclusions require probable premises.
In Islamic sciences, certain conclusions are typical in kalam (theology), while probable conclusions suffice in fiqh (jurisprudence).
3
Apply the principle to a syllogism
Given a syllogism, first identify the desired conclusion type. Then check the premises: are they certain (e.g., from necessary knowledge) or probable (e.g., from hadith with weak chain)? The syllogism is only productive if the material matches.
This principle is known as 'maddat al-qiyas' (matter of the syllogism).
4
Consider exceptions: reductio and sophistry
The footnote adds that doubtful and false conclusions can be sought using their own type of premises, as in qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum). Beyond that are illusions and sophistries, which do not yield genuine knowledge.

The second part of the purposes: on explaining the premises that function in the syllogism as the cloth functions for the shirt and the wood for the bed. For what we have mentioned functions as the stitching for the shirt and the shape of the bed for the bed. And just as one cannot take from every body a sword, a bed, and a shirt – for a shirt cannot be made from wood, nor a sword from cloth, nor a bed from a sword – so too one cannot take from every premise and proposition a productive syllogism. Rather, the productive syllogism only submits from certain premises if the desired conclusion is certain, or from probable premises if the desired conclusion is fiqh-related. So let us mention the meaning of certainty in itself to understand its essence, and let us mention its sources to understand the instrument by which certainty is hunted. Commentary
This passage opens the second part of the book, focusing on the premises of syllogisms (مادة القياس). The author uses an analogy: the premises are to the syllogism as cloth is to a shirt and wood is to a bed. Just as you cannot make a shirt from wood, you cannot construct a productive syllogism from any arbitrary premise. The type of premise must match the type of desired conclusion: if the desired conclusion is certain (يقيني), the premises must be certain; if it is fiqh-related (ظني), the premises must be probable. The author then announces that he will define certainty and its sources. The footnote clarifies that this is about the 'matter' of the syllogism, and that the conclusions take the ruling of that matter. It also notes that doubtful and false conclusions are sought by their own type, as in reductio ad absurdum, and beyond that are illusions and sophistries.

Premises as Material: The Epistemic Foundation of Syllogism
Author analogizes syllogistic premises to material for a product

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the analogy between premises in a syllogism and material in a product, and distinguish between certain and conjectural premises based on the desired conclusion.

Core Idea
The quality of a syllogism's conclusion depends on the epistemic quality of its premises.

This passage introduces the crucial concept that premises are the 'material' of a syllogism. Just as a shirt cannot be made from wood, a certain conclusion cannot be drawn from uncertain premises. The author emphasizes that the epistemic status of premises determines the nature of the conclusion, whether certain, conjectural, doubtful, or false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy
Premises are to syllogism as cloth is to shirt and wood is to bed. The author explicitly states: 'premises in syllogism are like cloth in a shirt and wood in a bed.'
2
Notice the constraints
Different materials yield different products: a shirt cannot be made from wood, nor a sword from cloth. Similarly, not every premise yields a productive syllogism; the premises must be suited to the desired conclusion.
3
Extract the key claim
A productive syllogism requires premises of the same epistemic quality as the desired conclusion: certain premises for certain conclusions, conjectural premises for fiqhi conclusions.
This is referred to as the 'material of the syllogism' (maddat al-qiyas).
4
Recognize the types of premises
Certain premises yield certain conclusions (yaqini); conjectural premises yield juristic conclusions (fiqhi). The author also mentions doubtful and false conclusions from their respective premises, as in reductio ad absurdum.
The footnote clarifies that the conclusion follows the ruling of the material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطرف الثاني من المقاصد في بيان المقدمات الجارية من القياس مجرى الثوب من القميص ، والخشب من السرير فإنَّ ما ذكرناه جرى مجرى الخياطة مِنَ القميص ، وشكل السريرِ مِنَ السرير ، وكما لا يمكن أن يُتَّخِذَ مِنْ كلّ جسم سيفٌ وسرير وقميص ؛ إذْ لا يتأتَّى مِنَ الخشب قميص ، ولا من الثوب سيفٌ ، ولا مِنَ السيف سرير .. فكذلكَ لا يمكن أن يُتَّخِذَ مِنْ كل مقدمةٍ وقضية قياس منتج ، بل القياس المنتج لا ينصاعُ إلَّا مِنْ مقدّماتٍ يقينيَّةٍ إنْ كانَ المطلوب يقينياً ، أو ظنيَّةٍ إِنْ كانَ المطلوب فقهيّاً ( ١ ) . فلنذكر معنى اليقين في نفسِهِ لتفهم ذاتُهُ ، ولنذكر مداركَهُ لتفهم الآلةُ التي بها يُقتنصُ اليقينُ . وهذا وإنْ كانَ القولُ يطولُ فيهِ ، ولكنَّا نحرص على الإيجاز بقدر الإمكان . (۱) وهذا ما يُشار إليه بـ ( مادة القياس ) ، ثم النتائجُ لها حكم تلك المادة ، وقد ذكر اليقينية والظنية ، وقد طلب لغاية النتيجة المشكوك فيها والكاذبة من جنسهما كما في قياس الخلف، وما وراء ذلك فوهميَّات وسفسطات
Second Part of the Objectives: Explaining that premises in syllogism are like cloth in a shirt and wood in a bed. For what we have mentioned takes the place of stitching in a shirt and the shape of a bed from a bed. And just as it is impossible to make a sword, a bed, and a shirt from every body — because a shirt cannot be made from wood, nor a sword from cloth, nor a bed from a sword — so too it is impossible to make a productive syllogism from every premise and proposition. Rather, a productive syllogism is only yielded from certain premises if the desired conclusion is certain, or from conjectural premises if the desired conclusion is juristic (fiqhi). (1) So let us mention the meaning of certainty in itself so that its essence is understood, and let us mention its sources so that the instrument by which certainty is hunted is understood. And this, although speech would lengthen in it, we strive for brevity as much as possible. (1) This is what is referred to as the 'material of the syllogism'; then the results have the ruling of that material. And he mentioned certainty and conjecture, and [the material] is sought for the sake of the doubtful and false conclusion from their types, as in reductio ad absurdum (qiyas al-khalf), and beyond that are illusions and sophistries.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage opens the second part of the treatise, establishing a fundamental analogy: syllogistic premises are the material from which a conclusion is formed, just as cloth is the material for a shirt and wood for a bed. The author argues that not every premise can yield a valid syllogism. Just as different materials are suited for different products, different premises are suited for different types of knowledge: certain premises for certain conclusions, and conjectural premises for juristic (fiqhi) conclusions. This underscores the importance of evaluating the epistemic quality of premises. The author then signals his intention to define certainty and its sources, emphasizing brevity despite the length the topic might require. The footnote clarifies that this is called the 'material of the syllogism' (maddat al-qiyas) and that the conclusion follows the nature of the material. It also mentions reductio ad absurdum as a case where the conclusion is drawn from opposite types.

Al-Ghazali's Epistemology: Certainty vs Opinion
Al-Ghazali distinguishes certainty from opinion and critiques common misapplications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genuine certainty (yaqin) from opinion (zann) according to al-Ghazali's criteria, and identify the conditions under which a syllogism yields certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Genuine certainty requires demonstrative syllogism from certain premises, not just accumulated reports.

Al-Ghazali, in this passage from his logic work, draws a sharp epistemological line between the tranquility of opinion and the firmness of certainty. He criticizes those who conflate probable knowledge from tawatur with true certainty, insisting that only a valid syllogism from certain premises can produce yaqin. This lesson unpacks his definitions and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Define the third state (zann)
Identify al-Ghazali's definition of opinion: tranquility of soul towards a belief while being aware of its opposite, or if made aware, not recoiling from accepting it. This is the baseline of opinion.
2
Trace the gradation of zann
Observe how opinion strengthens through multiple testimonies from just individuals, corroborating experiences, and circumstantial evidence. It gradually increases until, at the level of tawatur (mass transmission), it transforms into certainty.
Al-Ghazali describes this as a gradual process, not a sudden leap.
3
Contrast with yaqin
Al-Ghazali reserves yaqin for the first state: firm belief without any awareness of opposite. This genuine certainty is produced only by a syllogism composed of truly certain premises and constructed according to proper conditions.
The conclusion's certainty is proportionate to the epistemic quality of the premises.
4
Critique the muhaddithun's usage
Understand al-Ghazali's objection that hadith scholars and most people mistakenly call probable states 'certainty', which is prone to error. He insists that true certainty is the first state, not the second that results from accumulation.
This critique highlights the risk of treating probable evidence as certain in religious knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظنون
الحالة الثالثة : أن يكونَ لهُ سكون نفس إلى الشيء والتصديق بهِ وهو يشعرُ بنقيضه ، أو لا يشعرُ ولكنَّهُ إِنْ أُشعرَ بهِ .. لمْ ينفرْ طبعُهُ عَنْ قبولِهِ ، وهذا يُسمّى : ظنّاً ، وله درجات في الميل إلى الزيادة والنقصانِ لا تُحصى .
فمَنْ سمعَ مِنْ عَدْلٍ شيئاً .. سكنتْ إليه نفسُهُ ، فإن انضافَ إليهِ ثانٍ .. زادَ السكونُ وقوي الظنُّ ، فإن انضاف إليهِ ثالث زادَتِ القوَّةُ ، فإن انضافَتْ إليهِ تجربةٌ بصدقهم على الخصوص .. زادَتِ القوَّةُ ، فإنِ انضافَتْ إليه قرينة حالٍ ؛ كما إذا أخبروا عَنْ التواتر عن المُحْنِ أمرٍ مَخُوفٍ وهم على صورة مذعورين صفر الوجوه مضطربي الأحوال .. زادَ الظَّنُّ ، وهكذا لا يزال يترقى قليلاً قليلاً في القوَّةِ إلى أن ينقلب الظنُّ على التدريج يقيناً إذا انتهى الخبرُ إلى حدٍ
التواتر
والمحدّثونَ يسمون أكثر هذه الأحوال علماً ويقيناً ، حتَّى يطلقون بأنَّ الأخبار التي تشتملُ عليها الصحاح توجب العلم والعمل .
وكافة الخلق - إِلَّا آحاد المحققين - يسمُّونَ الحالةَ الثانية يقيناً ، ولا يميّزونَ بينَ الحالةِ الثانية والأولى ، والحقُّ : أنَّ اليقين هوَ الأَوَّلُ ، والثاني مظان الغلط
فمهما ألَّفْتَ القياسَ مِنْ مقدّماتٍ يقينيَّة حقيقية ، وراعيت في صورة تأليفه الشروط التي قدَّمناها .. كانَتِ النتيجة الحاصلة يقينيَّةً ضروريةً بحسبِ ذوقِ المقدّماتِ
Opinions: The third state: that he has tranquility of soul towards something and belief in it, while he is aware of its opposite, or he is not aware, but if he were made aware of it, his nature would not recoil from accepting it. This is called 'zann' (opinion), and it has degrees in inclination towards increase and decrease that are countless. So whoever hears something from a just person, his soul is tranquil towards it; if a second joins it, the tranquility increases and the opinion strengthens; if a third joins it, the strength increases; if experience of their truthfulness in particular joins it, the strength increases; if a circumstantial indication joins it, such as if they report from tawatur about a fearsome matter while they are in the form of terrified, pale-faced, disturbed states, the opinion increases. And thus it gradually increases in strength little by little until the opinion gradually turns into certainty when the report reaches the level of tawatur. The muhaddithun call most of these states 'ilm (knowledge) and certainty, so much so that they make the claim that the reports contained in the authentic collections necessitate knowledge and action. And all of creation—except a handful of verifiers—call the second state certainty, and they do not distinguish between the second state and the first. The truth is that certainty is the first, and the second is a place of error. So whenever you compose a syllogism from truly certain premises, and you observe in its form the conditions that we have presented, the resulting conclusion will be certain and necessary according to the taste of the premises.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological analysis by al-Ghazali, distinguishing between genuine certainty (yaqin) and mere opinion (zann). He first defines opinion (zann) as a state where the mind inclines to a belief while being aware of its opposite, or not averse to it if presented. This opinion admits of countless degrees, strengthening through multiple testimonies, corroborating experiences, and circumstantial evidence—until, when the report reaches the level of tawatur (mass transmission), opinion gradually transforms into certainty. Al-Ghazali then critiques the muhaddithun (hadith scholars) who call such probable states 'knowledge' and 'certainty', claiming that the reports in the Sahih collections compel certainty and action. He notes that most people—except a few verifying scholars—also erroneously label this second state as certainty, failing to distinguish between true certainty (the first state) and the second, which is prone to error. The truth, he asserts, is that genuine certainty is the first state, attainable only through a syllogism (qiyas) composed of truly certain premises and constructed according to the conditions previously established. Only then does the conclusion become certain and necessary, proportionate to the epistemic quality of its premises. Thus, al-Ghazali upholds that demonstrative reasoning, not mere accumulation of reports, yields true certainty.

Key Terms
ظن (zann): A state of mind where the soul is tranquil towards a belief while aware of its opposite, or not averse to it if presented; it has countless degrees. [Note: In this text, zann is contrasted with yaqin and considered prone to error, while in later logic texts it often denotes probability with degrees.]
يقين (yaqin): Certainty: the first state of firm belief without any awareness of opposite; it is produced only from truly certain premises in a valid syllogism. [Note: Stable across the tradition as indubitable knowledge, but al-Ghazali restricts it to demonstrative reasoning, rejecting its application to hadith.]
تواتر (tawatur): Mass transmission of a report that eventually transforms opinion into certainty through sheer multitude and consistency. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, tawatur is a source of certain knowledge; here it is described as gradually converting zann to yaqin.]
قياس (qiyas): Syllogism: a form of reasoning composed of premises; when built from truly certain premises and observing proper conditions, it yields certain conclusions. [Note: In mantiq, qiyas refers primarily to categorical syllogism; here al-Ghazali emphasizes the epistemic quality of premises.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hearing a report from a just person
B) Mass transmission (tawatur) of a report
C) Constructing a syllogism from truly certain premises with proper form
D) Accepting the consensus of muhaddithun

Key Takeaway
Genuine certainty is not merely strong opinion; it is the product of a valid syllogism with certain premises, a standard al-Ghazali upholds against common usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Second Method: Syllogisms as a Criterion to Expose Wahm's Error
Author presents the second method: using certain premises in syllogisms as a criterion to reveal wahm's limitation in metaphysical matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a syllogism using premises accepted by wahm to test whether wahm's rejection of a metaphysical conclusion reveals its limitation.

Core Idea
Wahm's rejection of a valid syllogism's conclusion proves its own limitation, not the argument's fault.

The author introduces a second method—a criterion for individual issues—using syllogisms as a balance between reason and wahm. Wahm is not always wrong; it agrees on empirical and mathematical matters. However, when it comes to metaphysical matters, wahm tends to analogize from sensible experience, leading to error. Reason can expose this by constructing syllogisms from premises that wahm itself accepts as certain. If wahm accepts the premises and the logical form but then rejects the conclusion, that reveals its deficiency in grasping the non-sensible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of wahm's agreement
Note that wahm agrees with reason on geometry, arithmetic, and sensible matters. These are areas where both faculties produce the same certain judgments.
Wahm is not inherently false; it has domains of reliability.
2
Identify the area of wahm's error
Wahm errs in metaphysical matters because it represents the non-sensible by the sensible, leading to false analogies.
This is the key: wahm cannot directly conceive of transcendent realities.
3
Understand the trick: build a syllogism from certain premises
Reason constructs a syllogism using premises that are certain and that wahm accepts. For example: 'Everything temporally originated has a cause; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world has a cause.' Wahm accepts both premises and the logical form (if valid).
The premises must be yaqiniyya (certain) and within wahm's agreement.
4
Observe wahm's response to the conclusion
If wahm accepts the premises and the validity of the syllogism but recoils from the conclusion, that inconsistency reveals its limitation in grasping the non-sensible.
This is the criterion: wahm's refusal shows its deficiency, not the argument's invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني : وهو معيار في آحادِ المسائل : وهو أن تعلم جميع قضايا الوهم ليسَتْ كاذبةً ؛ فإنَّها توافق العقل في استحالة وجودِ شخص في مكانين ، بل لا تنازع في جميع العلوم الهندسية والحسابية وما يدركُ بالحس ، وإِنَّما تنازع فيما وراء المحسوسات ؛ لأنَّها تمثّلُ غير المحسوس بالمحسوس ؛ إذْ لا تقبلُهُ إِلَّا على نحو المحسوسات . فحيلةُ العقل في أن يثق بكذبهِ وبقضاياه مهما نظر في غيرِ : أن يأخذ مقدِّماتِ يقينيَّةً يساعد الوهم عليها ، وينظمها بنظم المقاييس التي ذكرناها ؛ فإنَّ الوهم يساعدُ على أنَّ اليقينيَّاتِ إذا نُظمَتْ كذلك .. كانَتِ النتيجة لازمةً ؛ كما سبقَ مِنَ الأمثلة ، وكما في سائر الهندسيَّاتِ ، فيتخذ ذلك ميزاناً وحاكماً بينه وبينه . فإذا رأى الوهم قد كاعَ عن قبول نتيجة دليل قد ساعد على مقدماتها ، وساعد على صحة نظمِها ، وساعد على أنَّ مثل هذا النظم منتج بالضرورة ، وأنَّ تلك المقدّمات صحيحة بالضرورة ، ثم أخذ يمتنع عن قبول النتيجة .. علم أنَّ ذلكَ من قصور في طباعِهِ عن إدراكِ مثل هذا الشيء الخارج عَنِ المحسوسات .
The second way: It is a criterion for individual issues: that you know that not all propositions of wahm are false; indeed, it agrees with reason about the impossibility of a person being in two places, and it does not disagree in all the geometrical and arithmetical sciences and what is perceived by the senses. It only disagrees regarding matters beyond the sensible, because it represents the non-sensible by the sensible; since it only accepts it in the manner of sensible things. So the trick of reason to trust in its falsehood and in its propositions whenever it looks into such matters is: to take certain premises that wahm helps with, and organize them in the order of the syllogisms we have mentioned. For wahm agrees that if certainties are organized in this way, the conclusion follows necessarily, as in the previous examples and in all the geometric sciences. Then it takes that as a balance and arbiter between itself and itself. So when it sees that wahm has recoiled from accepting the conclusion of a proof whose premises it agreed to, and whose validity it agreed to, and agreed that such a syllogism is necessarily productive, and that those premises are necessarily true, and then it starts to refuse the conclusion, it knows that that is due to a deficiency in its nature in perceiving such a thing beyond the sensible.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the second method for uncovering the falsehood of wahm. The author explains that not all wahm judgments are false; many agree with reason, especially in geometry, arithmetic, and sensory matters. The disagreement arises only in metaphysical (supra-sensible) issues because wahm can only represent them according to sensible analogies. The trick (ḥīla) of reason is to construct a syllogism using premises that even wahm accepts as certain. Wahm itself agrees that if certain premises are correctly organized into a syllogism, the conclusion necessarily follows. So when wahm accepts the premises and the validity of the syllogistic form but then balks at the conclusion, this inconsistency shows that its refusal is due to its own limitation in grasping the non-sensible, not due to any defect in the argument.

Key Terms
قضايا الوهم
المقاييس
المقدمات اليقينية
حيلة العقل

C) That the premises must be false
D) That the syllogism is invalid

Key Takeaway
Reason can use syllogisms that even wahm agrees on to reveal that wahm's resistance to metaphysical conclusions stems from its own limitation.

The Syllogism

All Reasoning Reduces to Syllogistic Forms
Author argues that all reasoning necessarily conforms to syllogistic forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the underlying syllogistic structure in any argument presented in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every argument that answers 'why' is a syllogism in disguise.

This passage begins the third art of qiyas, dealing with the applications of syllogism. The author makes a bold claim: any piece of reasoning that can be prefaced with 'because' necessarily matches one of the previously defined syllogistic forms. This includes arguments in everyday conversation, legal reasoning, theological debates, and intellectual discussions. This lesson will help you see the syllogism beneath the surface.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument's claim and evidence
Find the conclusion being argued for and the reason given. For example, 'Wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating.'
2
Check if it answers 'why'
If the argument can be rephrased as 'A is B because C' or answers the question 'why', it is a candidate for syllogistic reduction.
3
Extract the premises and conclusion
Identify the major premise (the general rule), minor premise (the specific case), and the conclusion. In the wine example: Major: All intoxicants are forbidden. Minor: Wine is an intoxicant. Conclusion: Wine is forbidden.
4
Map onto syllogistic figure and mood
Determine the figure (position of the middle term) and mood (types of propositions). The wine example is first figure (Barbara): All M are P, All S are M, All S are P.
5
Verify validity
Check if the syllogism respects the conditions of the figure and mood. If invalid, identify which premise is missing or problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث من القياس في اللواحق. وفيه فصول. الفصل الأول: في بيان أن كل ما تنطلق به الألسنة في العادات والمحاورات والفضيات والعقليات مما يذكر في معرض الدليل والتعليل، ويمكن أن يُقرن به (لأن) ويذكر في جواب (لم).. فكل ذلك يرجع بالضرورة إلى ضروب النظم التي ذكرناها، لا يشذ منها شيء ألبتة. وأسباب قصور الدليل عن ضروب النظم المتقدمة أربعة أسباب، وذلك: إما قصور علم الناظر بتمام نظم القياس. وإما إهماله بعض المقدمات لكونها واضحة. وإما إهماله لها لكونها مشتملة على موضع التلبيس، فيحترز من أن يصرح به فيُطَّلع على تلبيسه.
The third art of syllogism concerning the accessories. It contains chapters. The first chapter: On demonstrating that every discourse that tongues utter in habits, conversations, dialectics, and intellectual matters, that is mentioned in the context of evidence and explanation, and can be prefaced with 'because' and is mentioned as an answer to 'why' — all that necessarily reduces to the types of arrangement that we have mentioned; nothing at all deviates from them. And the reasons for the insufficiency of the evidence [to conform] to the aforementioned arrangement types are four reasons: either the deficiency of the reasoner's knowledge of the complete arrangement of the syllogism, or his omission of some premises because they are obvious, or his omission of them because they contain a point of deceit, so he avoids stating it explicitly lest his deceit be exposed.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage begins the third art of qiyas, focusing on 'al-lawaḥiq' (the accessories or applications of syllogism). The first chapter makes a universal claim: every piece of reasoning used in everyday discourse, conversation, dialectical disputes (al-faḍiyyāt), or intellectual discussions that functions as evidence (dālīl or ta'līl) can be prefaced with 'because' (li-anna) and answers 'why' (lima). Such reasoning necessarily conforms to the types of arrangement (ḍurūb al-nuẓum) previously defined—referring to the figures and moods of the categorical syllogism. Nothing escapes this reduction. The author then explains why arguments often appear not to match these forms: there are four causes (though only three are listed in the text). The first is the reasoner's ignorance of the complete syllogistic arrangement. The second is conscious omission of premises that are obvious. The third is concealment of premises that would reveal deceit (talbīs). The passage thus emphasizes the comprehensiveness of syllogistic theory and provides practical reasons for superficial deviations.

Key Terms
ضروب النظم
التلبيس
اللواحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Analogical reasoning
D) Premises alone

Key Takeaway
Every argument that can be prefaced with 'because' is ultimately a syllogism, even if premises are omitted for clarity or deception.

Three Reasons for Apparent Deviation from Syllogism
Author lists three causes for arguments not appearing as full syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three reasons for apparent deviation from syllogistic forms and apply them to real arguments from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Premises are often omitted due to ignorance, obviousness, or deceit, not because the argument isn't syllogistic.

The author of this passage acknowledges that many arguments do not look like textbook syllogisms. He lists three causes: the reasoner's ignorance of the complete arrangement, omission for the sake of clarity, and omission to conceal deception. This lesson will help you diagnose why an argument seems incomplete and how to restore the full syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument's apparent structure
Look at how the argument is presented. Are all premises explicit? Is the conclusion stated? For example, a scholar might say 'This transaction is riba because it involves a delay in exchange.'
2
Determine if premises are missing
Check if the argument can be completed by adding a general rule (major premise) or a specific classification (minor premise).
3
Classify the reason for omission
Is the missing premise left out because the speaker doesn't know the full form (ignorance), because it's obvious to the audience (clarity), or because stating it would reveal a weak or deceitful link (deceit)?
4
Reconstruct the missing premises
Add the omitted premises to form a complete categorical syllogism. For the riba example: Major: Every sale with delay and excess is riba. Minor: This transaction involves delay and excess. Conclusion: This transaction is riba.
5
Assess validity after reconstruction
Once the syllogism is complete, evaluate whether it is valid. If not, the original argument is flawed regardless of the omission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث من القياس في اللواحق. وفيه فصول. الفصل الأول: في بيان أن كل ما تنطلق به الألسنة في العادات والمحاورات والفضيات والعقليات مما يذكر في معرض الدليل والتعليل، ويمكن أن يُقرن به (لأن) ويذكر في جواب (لم).. فكل ذلك يرجع بالضرورة إلى ضروب النظم التي ذكرناها، لا يشذ منها شيء ألبتة. وأسباب قصور الدليل عن ضروب النظم المتقدمة أربعة أسباب، وذلك: إما قصور علم الناظر بتمام نظم القياس. وإما إهماله بعض المقدمات لكونها واضحة. وإما إهماله لها لكونها مشتملة على موضع التلبيس، فيحترز من أن يصرح به فيُطَّلع على تلبيسه.
The third art of syllogism concerning the accessories. It contains chapters. The first chapter: On demonstrating that every discourse that tongues utter in habits, conversations, dialectics, and intellectual matters, that is mentioned in the context of evidence and explanation, and can be prefaced with 'because' and is mentioned as an answer to 'why' — all that necessarily reduces to the types of arrangement that we have mentioned; nothing at all deviates from them. And the reasons for the insufficiency of the evidence [to conform] to the aforementioned arrangement types are four reasons: either the deficiency of the reasoner's knowledge of the complete arrangement of the syllogism, or his omission of some premises because they are obvious, or his omission of them because they contain a point of deceit, so he avoids stating it explicitly lest his deceit be exposed.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage begins the third art of qiyas, focusing on 'al-lawaḥiq' (the accessories or applications of syllogism). The first chapter makes a universal claim: every piece of reasoning used in everyday discourse, conversation, dialectical disputes (al-faḍiyyāt), or intellectual discussions that functions as evidence (dālīl or ta'līl) can be prefaced with 'because' (li-anna) and answers 'why' (lima). Such reasoning necessarily conforms to the types of arrangement (ḍurūb al-nuẓum) previously defined—referring to the figures and moods of the categorical syllogism. Nothing escapes this reduction. The author then explains why arguments often appear not to match these forms: there are four causes (though only three are listed in the text). The first is the reasoner's ignorance of the complete syllogistic arrangement. The second is conscious omission of premises that are obvious. The third is concealment of premises that would reveal deceit (talbīs). The passage thus emphasizes the comprehensiveness of syllogistic theory and provides practical reasons for superficial deviations.

التلبيس
قصور العلم

Premises are often omitted in real arguments; recognizing the motive for omission (ignorance, obviousness, or deceit) helps in evaluating the argument's validity.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Reconstructing Elliptical Syllogisms: Omission of Obvious Premises
Author introduces third art of qiyas and exemplifies omission of obvious premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify elliptical syllogisms in Islamic discourse and reconstruct the omitted premise to form a complete categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Many valid arguments omit obvious premises; the syllogism remains valid.

The author teaches us that when an expert reasoner says 'This man deserves stoning because he committed adultery while married', he is actually using a full syllogism where the major premise 'Every married adulterer deserves stoning' is left unsaid. This lesson will train you to spot such elliptical arguments and reconstruct their full form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit premise
In the statement ‘This man deserves stoning because he committed adultery while married’, the explicit premise is ‘he committed adultery while married’ and the conclusion is ‘he deserves stoning’. The major premise is missing.
2
Determine the missing universal premise
The missing connection is a universal rule that links the given premise to the conclusion. For the stoning example, the missing premise is ‘Every married adulterer deserves stoning’.
3
Reconstruct the full syllogism
Place the universal premise as the major, the given premise as the minor, and the conclusion. Check the figure and mood. The stoning example yields first figure, Barbara (all M are P, all S are M, all S are P), which is always valid.
4
Verify the argument's soundness
Ensure the major premise is actually true in Islamic law. If the major premise is well-known and true, the elliptical argument is sound. If there is doubt, the premise must be examined separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثَّالِثُ مِنَ القِيَاسِ في اللواحق وفيه فصول الفصل الأول في بيان أن كل ما تنطلق به الألسنة في العادات والمحاورات والفضيات والعقليات مما يذكر في معرض الدليل والتعليل ، ويمكن أن يُقرن بهِ ( لأنَّ ) ويذكر في جوابِ ( لِمَ ) .. فكلُّ ذلك يرجعُ بالضرورة إلى ضروبِ النظم التي ذكرناها ، لا يشدُّ منها شي ألبتة . وما ترى تأليفه وإطلاقه على غير ذلكَ النظم .. فلَهُ أربعةُ أسباب ، وذلك : - إمَّا قصور علم الناظر بتمام نظم القياس . - وإمَّا إهمالُهُ بعض المقدماتِ لكونها واضحةً . - وإمَّا إهماله لها لكونها مشتملة على موضع التلبيس ، فيحترزُ مِنْ أن يصرّحَ بِهِ فَيُطَّلع على تلبيسِهِ - وإما لتركيب الضروبِ التي ذكرناها وجمع جملةٍ مِنْ آحادِها في سياق كلام واحد فإِنَّا لم نذكرُ إلَّا المفرداتِ مِنَ النظم ، فأما تلك المفردات إذا تداخلَتْ .. حصلَ منها تأليفات كثيرة أعرضنا عن تفصيلها للإيجاز . ولنورد لكلّ واحدٍ منها مثالاً الأوّلُ : فيما تُرِكَ إحدى مقدمتيه لوضوحِهِ : وهو أكثرُ الأدلَّةِ العقلية والفقهية ؛ إذ يحترزُ عن التطويل، وكذا في المحاورات ؛ و كما يقولُ القائلُ : هذا يجب عليه الرجم ، فيقال : لم ؟ فيقولُ لأنَّه زنى وهوَ محصنٌ . وكانَ تمامُهُ أن يقول : كلُّ مَنْ زنى وهوَ محصنٌ .. وجب عليهِ الرجم ، وهذا قد زنى وهوَ محصنٌ ؛ فإذا يجب عليه الرجم ، ولكن ترك مقدمة الحكم وذكر مقدمة المحكوم عليه ؛ لأنَّه يراه مشهوراً . وكذلك يُقالُ : العالم لوجودِهِ سبب ، فيُقالُ : لِمَ ؟ فنقولُ : لأَنَّهُ حادث ، وهذا لا يكفي ولكن تمامُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : كلُّ ما هو حادث فله سببٌ ، والعالم حادث ؛ فإذا له سببٌ . وكما يُقالُ : نكاحُ الشَّغارِ فاسد ، فيُقالُ : لِمَ ؟ فنقولُ : لأَنَّهُ منهي عنه .
The third art of the syllogism: on the appendages. It contains chapters. Chapter one: In explaining that everything that tongues utter in customs, conversations, legal matters, and intellectual matters, that is mentioned in the context of evidence and explanation, and can be accompanied by 'because' and mentioned in answer to 'why' — all of that necessarily reduces to the forms of organization that we have mentioned, not differing from them at all. What you see composed and used in other than that organization has four causes: either the deficiency of the reasoner's knowledge of the complete organization of the syllogism, or his omission of some premises because they are obvious, or his omission of them because they contain a point of deception, so he avoids stating it explicitly so that his deception may be exposed, or due to the combination of the forms we mentioned and gathering a number of their individuals in the flow of one speech. For we have only mentioned the individual forms of organization. As for those individuals, when they interweave, they produce many compositions whose details we have omitted for brevity. And we will present an example for each of them. The first: concerning what one of its premises is omitted due to its obviousness. This is the most common in intellectual and jurisprudential evidence, since it avoids prolixity, and similarly in conversations. As when someone says: 'This man deserves stoning.' It is asked: 'Why?' He says: 'Because he committed adultery while being married.' The complete argument would be: 'Every one who commits adultery while married deserves stoning; and this man committed adultery while married; therefore he deserves stoning.' But he omitted the general premise and mentioned the specific premise because he considers it well-known. Similarly, it is said: 'The world has a cause for its existence.' It is asked: 'Why?' We say: 'Because it is originated (hadith).' This is insufficient; rather the complete argument would be: 'Everything originated has a cause; and the world is originated; therefore it has a cause.' And as it is said: 'The marriage of shighar is invalid.' It is asked: 'Why?' We say: 'Because it is forbidden.'
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage introduces the third art of qiyas, focusing on the 'appendages' (al-lawāḥiq) — supplementary discussions about how everyday arguments relate to the formal syllogism. The author states that every statement used as evidence (dálil) or explanation (ta'líl) necessarily reduces to the syllogistic forms previously explained. He then identifies four reasons why a statement might appear not to be in proper syllogistic form: (1) the reasoner's ignorance of the complete syllogistic arrangement, (2) omission of obvious premises to avoid prolixity, (3) omission to conceal a deceptive point, and (4) combination of multiple syllogistic forms into a single compound statement. The author then proposes to give an example for each cause, starting with the first. In the first cause, a premise is omitted because it is obvious. The examples are drawn from Islamic jurisprudence and theology: the stoning of an adulterer (the major premise 'Every married adulterer deserves stoning' is omitted as commonly known), the origination of the world (the major premise 'Everything originated has a cause' is omitted), and the invalidity of shighar marriage (the major premise 'Every forbidden marriage is invalid' is omitted). In each case, the argument is valid but elliptical, and the missing premise can be supplied to reveal the full categorical syllogism.

Key Terms
اللواحق (al-lawāḥiq): Appendages or corollaries of the syllogism.
القياس (al-qiyās): Categorical syllogism composed of premises yielding a conclusion.
مقدمة (muqaddima): Premise of a syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Elliptical syllogisms are common in Islamic discourse; they are valid as long as the omitted premise can be supplied and the resulting syllogism is valid.

The Syllogism
