Omission of a Syllogistic Premise: Clarity vs. Deception
Explains omitted premises for clarity or deception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct missing premises in categorical syllogisms, distinguishing between omission for brevity and omission for deception.

Core Idea
Omission of a premise can be legitimate for clarity or deceitful to hide a false claim.

In everyday arguments, we often leave out premises that seem obvious. But is that always innocent? The author of this passage shows that the same technique can be used to make arguments concise or to conceal a weak premise. By analyzing examples from Islamic jurisprudence and everyday speech, you will learn to spot when an omission is helpful and when it is deceptive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion and given premise
Listen to the argument as presented. What conclusion is being drawn? What single premise is provided as support? The arguer says 'This man must be stoned because he committed adultery while muhsan.' The conclusion is 'he must be stoned'; the given premise is 'he committed adultery while muhsan'.
The given premise is usually the minor premise (subject of the conclusion) or the major premise.
2
Reconstruct the full syllogism
Add the missing premise to form a complete categorical syllogism. For the stoning example: missing major premise 'Everyone who is muhsan and commits adultery must be stoned.' The full syllogism then is: All muhsan adulterers must be stoned; this man is a muhsan adulterer; therefore he must be stoned.
The missing premise can be either major (concerning the predicate) or minor (concerning the subject).
3
Check the missing premise for truth
Is the omitted premise obviously true, or is it questionable? If it is a well-known ruling (e.g., from Quran or consensus), omission is likely for brevity. If it is disputable or false, omission might be deceptive. In the shighar marriage example, the premise 'every prohibited act is invalid' is not universally accepted in fiqh.
In the deception examples, the omitted premise is actually false, making the argument unsound.
4
Assess the arguer's intention
Determine whether the omission is for clarity (to avoid tedious repetition of an obvious truth) or for deception (to prevent the opponent from challenging a weak premise). The passage suggests that if the missing premise is non-obvious and the arguer avoids stating it, deception is likely.
Deceptive omission often occurs in polemics to give a false impression of a valid proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما لتركيب الضروب التي ذكرناها وجمع جملة من آحادها في سياق كلام واحد فإنا لم نذكر إلا المفردات من النظم، فأما تلك المفردات إذا تداخلت.. حصل منها تأليفات كثيرة أعرضنا عن تفصيلها للإيجاز. ولنورد لكل واحد منها مثالاً. الأول: فيما تركت إحدى مقدمتيه لوضوحه: وهو أكثر الأدلة. مثال وضوح إحدى المقدمتين العقلية والفقهية؛ إذ يحترز عن التطويل، وكذا في المحاورات؛ وكما يقول القائل: هذا يجب عليه الرجم، فيقال: لم؟ فيقول: لأنه زنى وهو محصن. وكان تمامه أن يقول: كل من زنى وهو محصن وجب عليه الرجم، وهذا قد زنى وهو محصن؛ فإذا يجب عليه الرجم، ولكن ترك مقدمة الحكم وذكر مقدمة المحكوم عليه؛ لأنه يراه مشهوراً. وكذلك يقال: العالم لوجوده سبب، فيقال: لم؟ فنقول: لأنه حادث، وهذا لا يكفي ولكن تمامه أن يقال: كل ما هو حادث فله سبب، والعالم حادث؛ فإذا له سبب. وكما يقال: نكاح الشغار فاسد، فيقال: لم؟ فنقول: لأنه منهي عنه. وتمامه أن يقول: كل منهي عنه فهو فاسد، ونكاح الشغار منهي عنه؛ فهو إذا فاسد، ولكن ترك ذلك للشهرة والتلبيس أيضاً؛ حتى لا يقول الخصم: أسلم أنه منهي عنه، ولكن لا أسلم أن كل منهي عنه فاسد، فيترك المقدمة الممنوعة. وأما مثال ما يترك لأجل التلبيس: فأن تقول: فلان خائن في حقك، فيقال: لم؟ فيقول: لأنه يناجي عدوك. وتمامه أن يقول: كل من يناجي العدو فهو خائن، وهذا يناجي العدو؛ فهو إذا خائن، ولكن ترك مقدمة الحكم؛ فإنه لو صرح به.. ربما يذكر أنه ربما يناجي العدو ليخدعه أو يستميله أو ينصحه، فلا يسلم أن كل من يناجي العدو فهو خائن، فيترك ذلك حتى لا يتذكر محل الكذب. وربما يترك مقدمة المحكوم عليه، فيقول: لا تخالط فلاناً، فيقال: لم؟ فيقول: لأن الحساد لا تؤمن مخالطتهم. وتمامه أن تقول: الحساد لا يخالطون، وهذا حاسد؛ فينبغي ألا يخالط، فتركت مقدمة المحكوم عليه؛ وهو قولك: هذا حاسد. وكل من يقصد التلبيس في المجادلات والمحاورات.. فطريقه إهمال إحدى المقدمتين؛ إيهاماً بأنه واضح، وربما يكون الكذب.
And as for the composition of the syllogistic moods that we mentioned and gathering a number of their individuals in the context of a single speech—indeed we mentioned only the individual parts of the syllogism. As for those individuals when they overlap, many composite arguments result, whose details we omitted for brevity. We will provide an example for each of them. The first: concerning that in which one of its two premises is omitted due to its clarity: this is the majority of proofs. Example of the clarity of one of the two premises, rational and juristic, as one avoids prolixity, and similarly in dialogues. It is as when someone says: 'This man must be stoned.' It is said: 'Why?' He says: 'Because he committed adultery and is muhsan.' The complete argument would be: 'Everyone who commits adultery and is muhsan must be stoned, and this one committed adultery and is muhsan; therefore, he must be stoned.' But he omitted the major premise and mentioned the minor premise because he considers it well-known. Similarly, it is said: 'The universe has a cause for its existence.' It is said: 'Why?' We say: 'Because it is originated.' But this is not enough; the complete argument is: 'Everything originated has a cause, and the universe is originated; therefore, it has a cause.' And as it is said: 'The marriage of shighar is invalid.' It is said: 'Why?' We say: 'Because it is prohibited.' The complete argument is: 'Everything prohibited is invalid, and the marriage of shighar is prohibited; therefore, it is invalid.' But that was omitted for notoriety and also for deception, so that the opponent does not say: 'I concede that it is prohibited, but I do not concede that everything prohibited is invalid,' so the disputed premise is omitted. As for the example of what is omitted for the purpose of deception: that you say: 'So-and-so is treacherous regarding your rights.' It is said: 'Why?' He says: 'Because he converses secretly with your enemy.' The complete argument is: 'Everyone who converses secretly with the enemy is treacherous, and this one converses secretly with the enemy; therefore, he is treacherous.' But he omitted the major premise; for if he stated it explicitly, it might be remembered that perhaps he converses secretly with the enemy to deceive him or to win him over or to advise him, so it would not be conceded that everyone who converses secretly with the enemy is treacherous. So he omits that so that the false part is not remembered. And sometimes the minor premise is omitted, as when one says: 'Do not associate with so-and-so.' It is said: 'Why?' He says: 'Because associating with the envious is not safe.' The complete argument is: 'The envious are not to be associated with, and this one is envious; therefore, he should not be associated with.' So the minor premise was omitted, which is your saying: 'This one is envious.' And everyone who intends deception in disputations and dialogues—his method is to neglect one of the two premises, giving the impression that it is obvious, while perhaps it is false.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the practice of omitting one premise in a syllogism, either because it is obvious or to deceive. The author begins by noting that they previously discussed only individual syllogistic moods; now they consider compounded arguments. The first category is omission for clarity: the arguer leaves out a premise that is well-known to avoid verbosity. Examples include: 'This man must be stoned because he committed adultery while muhsan' — the major premise 'every muhsan adulterer must be stoned' is omitted as obvious. Similarly, 'The universe has a cause because it is originated' omits 'everything originated has a cause'. The third example, about shighar marriage, introduces an element of deception: the major premise 'everything prohibited is invalid' is omitted not only for notoriety but also to prevent the opponent from disputing it. The second category is outright deception: the arguer omits a premise that is actually false, so that the flaw remains hidden. Examples include accusing someone of treachery because he converses with an enemy, omitting the major premise that all such conversation implies treachery (which is false, as there are other motives). Another example omits the minor premise: 'Do not associate with so-and-so because associating with the envious is unsafe' omits the minor premise 'this one is envious'. The author concludes that the deceptive method always involves omitting a premise under the guise of obviousness, while in reality it may be false.

Key Terms
مقدمة
تلبيس
الضروب

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the minor premise alone proves the conclusion.
D) To avoid repeating what the opponent already knows.

Key Takeaway
Omission of a premise can serve clarity or concealment; always reconstruct the full syllogism to check the truth of the missing premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
